## Foreword

*This book is dedicated to Mila.*

I didn't write this book because the world needs another book about artificial intelligence. There are enough of those. Most are either utopian to the point of naivety or dystopian to the point of paralysis. Some promise paradise, others warn of doom. Both are entertaining. Both are useless.

I wrote this book because one sentence wouldn't let me go. As an investor, as an entrepreneur, as someone who lives the pace of these changes every single day. A sentence the World Economic Forum published as its vision in 2016, one that has since gone viral:

> *"You'll own nothing and be happy."*

I believe this is the biggest lie of our time.

Without ownership, you won't be happy. Without ownership, you have no security. Without ownership, you have no bargaining power. Without ownership, you are dependent on those who do own things. That was true under feudalism, it was true in the industrial age, and it will be even more true in the machine economy.

### The causal chain is brutally simple

> *Machines replace labor. No labor income. No capital to invest. No wealth. Game over.*

The window is closing. Not in decades. In years.

Because what happens when machines can not only think and act, but also have their own money—and eventually their own companies?

The answer to that question has consequences far beyond return tables. It affects how we work, how we invest, how we function as a society, and yes—how we turn today's untreatable diseases into tomorrow's treatable ones.

::: {.infobox}
### A word about where I stand

I'm not an analyst writing about technology from a safe distance. I'm not a journalist researching and reporting trends. I'm a founder and investor. With Rockstardevelopers, I've been building software products for years. With accessibleAI, I'm developing AI systems that help companies automate regulatory compliance. I don't sit in an office reading about AI. I build it. Every day.

That also means: I have *skin in the game*. I've invested in the technologies I write about. I have money in Bitcoin, in Ethereum, in AI stocks, in robotics. You can read that as a conflict of interest—or as proof that I take my own thesis seriously enough to put my money where my mouth is. I think the latter is more credible.

Nassim Taleb wrote that you should never trust anyone who isn't personally affected by what they're judging.^[1]^ I am affected.
:::

### What you'll find in this book

This book will help you protect and grow your wealth in a world that is changing radically. It will show you who the winners and losers of this transformation will be. And how to end up on the right side.

### Why "The AI Species"?

> *You use a tool. You coexist with a species.*

That's the distinction most analyses miss. When a machine is a tool—a hammer, a calculator, an algorithm—humans control it. They own it, they turn it on and off, they set the rules.

But what happens when a machine makes its own decisions? When it manages its own money, signs its own contracts, starts its own companies? When it learns, adapts, optimizes—without a human telling it how?

Then it's no longer a tool. It's an actor. An economic organism. Something that operates in the same economy as you and me—with its own interests, its own resources, its own strategies.

Biologists would reject the term "species" here. Fair enough. AI has no DNA, no Darwinian evolution, no consciousness—at least not yet; more on that in Chapter 14. But economists and philosophers should look more closely. Because what's emerging here has characteristics we've previously only seen in living beings: autonomy, adaptation, economic self-sufficiency.

> *The AI Species—that's not a metaphorical title. It's an economic observation. We are creating entities that will participate in our economy. Not as tools. As players. And whether you call them a "species," a "new class of actors," or an "economic life form" is secondary. What matters is: you need to own them. Or they'll own you.*

### A thesis

Not a collection of opinions, not a parade of possibilities, but a clear, argued thesis: The convergence of artificial intelligence, robotics, and cryptocurrency is creating a new economic order. An economy in which machines produce, decide, act, and pay. This economy isn't coming someday. It's emerging now. And it will trigger the greatest wealth transformation of our lifetime.

The book is structured in four parts:

**Part I** develops the thesis. What is artificial intelligence really, beyond the hype? Why do autonomous machines need their own money—and why are cryptocurrencies the answer to a problem most people haven't even recognized as a problem? What happens when not just money but all the world's assets are converted into digital tokens that machines can read, trade, and own? And what does it mean when machines don't just pay, but start their own companies that operate without human management?

**Part II** maps the landscape. Who wins and who loses—geopolitically, energy-wise, regulatorily, socially? Why are Microsoft and Amazon buying nuclear power plants? And why is Europe regulating itself into irrelevance?

**Part III** looks ahead. What does the world look like in ten years, in the most likely scenario? What happens when brain-computer interfaces connect our thoughts directly to AI? And who owns the productivity when machines take over the work?

**Part IV** delivers the playbook. How do you translate this thesis into investment decisions? Using Nassim Taleb's barbell strategy, with concrete portfolios, an honest analysis of the risks, and an action plan for the next 30 days.

### What you won't find in this book

No investment advice. No guarantees. No "this one weird trick" nonsense.

What you will find is a framework for thinking. A framework that helps you make sense of the next ten years. Not just as an investor, but as someone living in a world that is changing right now.

Whether the convergence I describe will unfold exactly as I envision—I don't know. Nobody does. But I'm convinced that the direction is right, and that those who recognize the direction early enough will be glad they did, ten years from now.

> *The most valuable investment theses are those that almost nobody shares—but that turn out to be right.*

This book is my conviction. Test it. Challenge it. Then decide for yourself.

Why I wrote all of this down—the real reason—is at the end of this book.

*Thomas Huhn, March 2026*

# PART I: THE THESIS

## Chapter 1: At the Dawn of the Greatest Transformation in 250 Years


---

> *"The future is already here—it's just not evenly distributed."*
> *— William Gibson*

---


### The Tectonic Shifts of History

There are moments when the economic order shifts—not gently, but abruptly.

The steam engine was one such moment. When James Watt patented his improved engine in 1769^[3]^, the immediate application was clear: pumping water out of coal mines. What nobody foresaw was the cascade that followed. The steam engine enabled factories, factories enabled mass production, which in turn required railroads for transport. Railroads created national markets, and national markets required banks and insurance companies. Within two generations, a single invention had rewritten the entire economic and social order of Europe.


![250 years of technological disruption—from the steam engine to the machine economy](images-en/abb-02.png)


Electrification was the next break. In the 1880s, Thomas Edison powered up the Pearl Street Station in Manhattan—the world's first commercial power generator.^[4]^ Thirty years later, the entire industrial world ran on electricity. But the real revolution wasn't light. It was the ability to distribute energy. Suddenly, factories no longer had to sit on rivers to harness water power. Machines could run anywhere. That changed the geography of production, the structure of cities, the rhythm of the human day. The electric lightbulb turned night into day and the night shift into standard practice.


Then the internet. In hindsight, the moment Tim Berners-Lee released the World Wide Web in 1991^[5]^ was so obviously transformative that you wonder why everyone didn't immediately bet everything on it. But that's the fallacy of hindsight. In 1995, four years after launch, less than one percent of the world's population used the internet.^[6]^ Britain's Astronomer Royal, Martin Rees, gave the internet in its current form "about as much future as CB radio."^[7]^ Paul Krugman wrote in 1998: "By 2005 or so, it will become clear that the Internet's impact on the economy has been no greater than the fax machine's."^[8]^ They weren't just wrong. They were spectacularly wrong.

Those who invested during these inflection points—not in the technology itself, but in the right layer of the value chain—built wealth that lasted generations. The families who invested in railroad companies, not steam engines. The investors who bet on Standard Oil, not oil drilling equipment. The venture capitalists who funded Amazon and Google, not the fiber-optic cable manufacturers that went bankrupt in 2001.

The pattern is always the same: the technology itself is visible. The value layer that profits from it is not. And that's exactly where the opportunity lies. Who made money during the great disruptions? And who didn't? The parallels to earlier technology revolutions are instructive—but only if you look closely. Because in every disruption, most investors lost money, even when the technology ended up changing everything.

The British railway mania of the 1840s was the first great technology bubble in history.^[9]^ Ordinary families poured their savings into railroad stocks. Hundreds of companies were founded—many existed only on paper. When the bubble burst in 1847, fortunes were destroyed. The technology was real—railroads changed the world. But most early investors lost anyway.

Who won? Not those who invested in railroad companies, but those who controlled the infrastructure. Cornelius Vanderbilt didn't get rich by buying railroad stocks. He bought the railroads themselves, consolidated them, and controlled the network. J.P. Morgan earned his fortune not as an investor, but as the financier who organized the restructuring of bankrupt railroads. John D. Rockefeller built Standard Oil because railroads needed to transport oil. Andrew Carnegie built US Steel because railroads needed steel.

The pattern: the technology itself was a risky bet. The infrastructure around it was the safe one.

Electrification repeated the pattern. Thomas Edison built the first power grid and founded Edison General Electric. George Westinghouse bet on alternating current and built the competition. The "War of Currents" followed—one of the legendary business battles of the 19th century. In the end, Edison lost control of his own company, which merged into General Electric without his name in the title. Westinghouse went bankrupt and lost his company to creditors. The two inventors who made electrification possible profited the least.

Who won? Samuel Insull, a former Edison assistant who understood that the business wasn't generating electricity—it was distributing it. Insull built an empire of utilities across the Midwest and became one of the richest Americans of his era. He controlled the infrastructure layer, not the technology.

The internet shows the same thing. In the dot-com bubble around 2000, investors lost trillions. Pets.com, Webvan, Kozmo.com—all the hyped startups built on top of the internet vanished. But the infrastructure survived. Cisco, which built the routers and switches that the internet flowed through, lost 80 percent of its market cap in the crash but recovered and is a profitable company today. Amazon fell from $113 to $5.97^[10]^, survived, and became the most valuable company in the world. Google didn't go public until 2004—after the crash—and built its dominance on infrastructure that others had built and paid for.

### What does this teach us about the current convergence?

**Three things.**

**First:** the technology is almost always real. Railroads came. Electrification came. The internet came. AI, robotics, and crypto are coming too. Doubting that would be like doubting in 1995 whether the internet would catch on.

**Second:** most early investments fail anyway. Not because the technology was wrong, but because the price was too high, the company too weak, or the timing off. Of a thousand AI startups that exist in 2025, maybe fifty will still be relevant in 2035.

**Third:** the money flows to infrastructure, not applications. Vanderbilt, not railroad stockholders. Insull, not Edison. Amazon and Google, not Pets.com. In our thesis: NVIDIA, not the hundredth AI startup. Ethereum, not the hundredth token. That's why the barbell strategy in Part IV emphasizes the infrastructure layer.


![Infrastructure beats applications: the recurring pattern](images-en/abb-03.png)


> We are now at such a point. And this time, the transformation is more radical than anything we've seen.


---

### Why "this time is different" actually is different this time
"This time is different"—four of the most dangerous words in investing, as Sir John Templeton once warned^[11]^. Every hype cycle claims to be new. The dot-com bubble claimed it. The housing bubble claimed it. Crypto in 2017 claimed it. And every time, the reckoning followed.
So why should it be different this time?
Because there is a qualitative difference between a technology that supports human labor and one that replaces it. Every previous technological revolution amplified human capabilities. The steam engine amplified muscle power, the computer amplified calculation, the internet amplified communication—but humans remained the actor in every case. They got better tools, but they remained the ones operating them.
Artificial intelligence breaks this pattern.
For the first time in human history, we are building systems that can think, learn, decide, and act without human guidance. Not within a narrow, predefined framework like a chess computer evaluating a board position, but in the open, unstructured world—from contract analysis to software development to medical diagnosis.

This is not incremental progress. It's a category change.

::: {.infobox}
### The Four Economic Revolutions According to Emad Mostaque

Emad Mostaque, the founder of Stability AI, calls^[12]^ this the "Intelligence Inversion": the fourth and final economic upheaval in human history. After land, labor, and capital, intelligence itself is being inverted from a scarce resource to an abundantly available one. The earlier inversions always left a fallback: if you lost your land, you could sell your labor. If you lost your job, you could deploy capital. But when intelligence becomes abundant, it replaces the mind itself. "AI replaces the mind itself," Mostaque writes^[13]^ in his book *The Last Economy*, "making the Luddite analogy more dire." The Luddites smashed looms and found other work. When the machine replaces not the hands but the head, there's no obvious way out.
:::

### Here's the crux that most people don't want to see:

For the vast majority of people, labor was the only path to wealth. You sell your time, your knowledge, your skills. In return, you get money. With that money, you build assets. From assets come security, freedom, options.

When machines take over work—not just physical work, but intellectual work—that chain breaks.

Machines replace labor. No labor income. No capital to invest. No wealth.

That's not a worst-case scenario. That's the default path if you do nothing.

The good news: there is a way out. And the way out is ownership.

Those who own the machines that take over the work will profit from their productivity. Those who own the infrastructure they run on. Those who own the networks through which they pay.

This isn't an investment thesis. It's a survival strategy.
Mostaque gives humanity a "Thousand-Day Window" for this.^[14]^ A thousand days to set the course before the phase transition becomes irreversible. Not as a countdown to doomsday. As a deadline for decisions that no one will be able to make afterward.

Now connect that capability with robotics—give AI a physical body that can operate in the real world. Then hand it a payment system that works without banks, without bureaucracy, without human gatekeepers. What emerges is something that rewrites our entire economic order.

A machine that can think, that has a body, and that owns its own money.

That's no longer an amplification of human capabilities. That's the emergence of a new economic actor.

And that is what will happen over the next ten years. Not as a science fiction scenario, but as the logical, already-in-motion consequence of technologies that exist today and are improving exponentially.

I'm not a prophet—I'm a technology entrepreneur and investor. I'm not writing this book as a detached observer theorizing about technology from a university chair.
I build AI products. Every day.
With accessibleAI, I develop software based on large language models that helps companies automate regulatory compliance. I experience firsthand how fast this technology is evolving. I see how it's changing work processes that had stayed the same for decades. I see how it's calling entire professions into question. Not theoretically—concretely: people who seemed indispensable yesterday, whose tasks an AI agent now handles in minutes.
And I invest. In Bitcoin and Ethereum, for years. In AI stocks. In robotics companies. With my own money, not my clients'. Taleb calls that the willingness to bear your own consequences. If my thesis is wrong, I lose money. That sharpens the analysis in a way no academic research can replace.
This dual role sharpens the analysis. I can explain not just what is happening, but why it's happening and how fast—because I know the development cycles from daily work.
Peter Thiel wrote in *Zero to One*^[2]^ that the most valuable companies are those doing something most people don't yet believe is possible. Most people still don't believe that AI agents will autonomously manage supply chains, negotiate contracts, and process payments within five years. They don't believe that humanoid robots will work in factories by 2030. And they certainly don't understand why these machines will need cryptocurrencies to function.

---


### A Thesis That's Never Finished

People often ask me when I realized that AI, crypto, and robotics belong together. They expect a story with an aha moment. An evening when everything clicked.

That's not how it works.

The thesis developed over years, and it's still not finished. There's no point at which you can say: now I've understood it. Instead, there's a series of moments when a new puzzle piece falls into place and the bigger picture comes into sharper focus.

When I first started understanding Bitcoin, it was about hard money—the question of whether a monetary system can exist that no government controls. Years later, when AI agents emerged that could act autonomously on the internet, I noticed something: these agents can't open a bank account or make a wire transfer, but they can create a crypto wallet in ten seconds, without any human involvement. That's when two separate interests became a single thesis.

Robotics came later. When Tesla unveiled Optimus and I started thinking through what happens when AI gets a body, it became clear: this isn't a third technology alongside the other two. It's the bridge between the digital and physical worlds. An AI with a robotic body and a crypto wallet is a complete economic actor. Think. Act. Pay.


::: {.infobox}
### My Own AI Experiment

And the convergence doesn't stop. While I'm writing this book, things are happening that seamlessly fit into the thesis—things I wouldn't have predicted a year ago. One example: In January 2026^[223]^, an open-source project called^[179]^ OpenClaw went viral. A single developer in Vienna had built a personal AI agent that runs on your own machine, autonomously operates programs, conducts research, writes code, and communicates through messenger apps. Within weeks, OpenClaw became the fastest-growing open-source project in AI history. By mid-February 2026, OpenAI had brought the developer on board.

Why does this matter? Because OpenClaw demonstrates exactly what this book describes. An AI agent that acts autonomously (Phase 3), that runs on local hardware (decentralized, not in some corporation's cloud), and that augments a human user by independently completing tasks. The next logical step: this agent needs money. To pay for APIs, to buy compute time, to use services. And there we are again—back to crypto.

:::


I keep discovering new convergences, new trends, new developments that fit the thesis. Some I can't even name yet. There are certainly white swans out there—positive surprises that nobody has on their radar but that will seamlessly fit into the picture.

A theory that is constantly confirmed by new evidence from unexpected directions is more robust than one that rests on a single argument.

The convergence of AI, robotics, and crypto is not a finished topic. It's a living system. And the pace at which new puzzle pieces are falling into place is accelerating.


There's a reason, though, why many people can't see this convergence even though the evidence is overwhelming: they're thinking in the categories of an economy that is becoming obsolete. Emad Mostaque identified seven foundational assumptions in^[15]^ *The Last Economy* that economic textbooks treat as truths—and that AI is turning into fictions. Two are particularly relevant to this book. The first: "Markets find equilibrium." In the analog world, that was approximately true. In digital markets, it's false. Digital markets are self-reinforcing—they tend toward monopolies through network effects, not toward equilibrium. Whoever has the platform attracts the data, trains the better model, and attracts even more users. That's not market distortion. That's the physics of digital networks. The second: "Distribution follows contribution." The old narrative says the market gives everyone what they deserve. In a world where a single AI model does the work of thousands, value doesn't flow to the thousands who used to do the work. It flows to the few who own the model and the platform. That's not a malfunction. It's the logical consequence of a technology that drives the marginal cost of intelligence to zero. Anyone going into the next ten years with these assumptions won't understand what's happening.

---

### How to Read This Book

Let me be transparent: this book is not a neutral overview of the state of technology. It's a polemic—an argued, opinionated analysis with a clear thesis and concrete investment implications. I have convictions, and I lay them out openly. You can accept them, challenge them, or reject them. And above all: discuss them—with me and with others. On TheAiSpecies.world, there's a forum where exactly that can happen. But I think it's more honest to disclose my perspective than to hide behind supposed neutrality.

You already know the structure from the foreword: four parts, from the thesis through the landscape and the forward look to the concrete playbook. What I'd add here: this book is written so that each chapter also works on its own. If you're an investor who wants to jump straight to the portfolio, you can do that. But the conviction you need to not sell during a crash—that only comes from following the argument from the beginning. My advice: read in order. The thesis builds on itself, and each layer supports the next.

This book is written for people who sense that something is changing and who are looking for a clear, argued framework to understand that change and act on it. If you're one of those people, read on.

> *The machines are coming. And they're bringing their own money.*


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ AI, robotics, and cryptocurrency are converging into the greatest economic transformation since the steam engine.
>
> ◆ Unlike previous transformations, all three are happening simultaneously and accelerating each other.
>
> ◆ Infrastructure investors have historically profited most—not the application hypes.

## Chapter 2: The AI Revolution in Four Phases


---

> *"We always overestimate the change that will occur in the next two years and underestimate the change that will occur in the next ten."*
>
> — Bill Gates

---


To understand where we're headed, you have to understand where we are. The evolution of artificial intelligence can be described in four phases. Each expands the addressable market by an order of magnitude. Each changes the role of humans in the economy. And each builds on the previous one—not linearly, but exponentially.


![The four phases of AI—from tool to autonomous machine](images-en/abb-04.png)


We are currently at the transition from Phase 2 to Phase 3. This is the moment when most people still underestimate the significance of what's happening. In five years, they won't.


---

### Phase 1: AI as a Tool (2020–2023)
On November 30, 2022, OpenAI released ChatGPT. Within five days, it had a million users. Within two months, a hundred million.^[16]^ No product in the history of technology had ever reached that threshold faster—not the iPhone, not Instagram.
But the speed of adoption obscured the limitations of the tool. In Phase 1, AI was essentially a better search box. You asked a question and got an answer, gave an instruction and got a result—"Write me an email," "Summarize this text," "Generate an image of an astronaut on a horse."
Powerful? Yes. But passive. Humans remained the actor at every moment. They formulated the question, evaluated the answer, decided on next steps. AI was an instrument—an extraordinarily versatile one, but one that sat useless on the table without a human.
That explains why many smart people initially underestimated the AI revolution. They saw a better Google—a fancier search engine, a party trick that produced impressive text but failed at math and made up facts. They weren't entirely wrong—for Phase 1. What they didn't see was the speed at which the next phases would follow.
The errors of ChatGPT 3.5 in 2022—the hallucinations, the math mistakes, the factual errors—are not the final state of the technology. They're growing pains. When the first car was slower than a horse, some concluded that cars had no future. They confused the prototype with the product.

---


### Phase 2: AI as a Coworker (2024–2025)

This is where we are now, and the difference from Phase 1 is not gradual—it's qualitative.

AI systems are being integrated into workflows. They're no longer tools you pull out of a drawer when needed. They're coworkers who sit permanently beside you—or, increasingly, do the work themselves.

GitHub Copilot, Microsoft's AI coding assistant, now writes over 40 percent of all new code on the platform.^[17]^ Not as suggestions that a programmer then adjusts, but as finished, functioning code that gets adopted directly. Forty percent. A single AI system has increased the productivity of millions of software developers by nearly half—in less than two years since its launch.

AI-powered legal analysis tools handle in minutes what junior lawyers used to take weeks for. Due diligence reviews that once occupied teams of lawyers for months are now completed in hours—more thoroughly, more consistently, and at a fraction of the cost.

In medicine, AI systems diagnose certain cancers with higher accuracy than experienced radiologists. Google DeepMind's AlphaFold predicted the three-dimensional structure of over 200 million proteins^[18]^—a problem biology had been working on for decades.

McKinsey estimates the annual value creation potential of generative AI at $2.6 to $4.4 trillion.^[19]^ Trillions. With a T. For context: the entire GDP of Germany is roughly $4.5 trillion.^[20]^

The productivity gains are real and measurable. But they have a characteristic that many people would rather not see: they replace human labor hours. When an AI system does the work of three junior lawyers in one hour, the firm doesn't need three junior lawyers anymore. When GitHub Copilot writes 40 percent of the code, the development team will eventually need fewer developers—or the same number will produce dramatically more output, which in a competitive market amounts to the same thing.

Phase 2 is the phase where AI stops being impressive and starts being threatening. Not for everyone. For those who use AI, it's a productivity multiplier. But for those who ignore it or whose work is being replaced by it, the ground is starting to shake.


---

### Phase 3: AI as an Autonomous Agent (2025–2028)
This is the paradigm shift. And the phase that most people don't even have on their radar yet.
An agent is not a chatbot. A chatbot answers questions. An agent acts. It has goals, plans steps, uses tools, observes results, corrects errors, and pursues its objective to completion—without a human signing off on every step.
Here's how to picture the difference: In Phase 2, you tell your AI assistant: "Write an email to the supplier saying we want to change the order." The AI writes the email. You read it, tweak a phrase, click Send.
In Phase 3, you say: "Negotiate better terms with our supplier for Q3. Our budget is X, our walk-away number is Y, and we need delivery by September." The agent sends emails, analyzes responses, makes counteroffers, compares alternative suppliers, creates a recommendation with reasoning, and—depending on its authority—closes the deal. It doesn't do this in a single action but over days and weeks, while you focus on other things.
Anthropic, OpenAI, and Google DeepMind are all working on exactly these capabilities. Anthropic demonstrated with Claude the ability to autonomously operate a computer: opening programs, filling out forms, researching on the internet, saving results to files. OpenAI showed with "Deep Research" agents that AI systems can independently conduct multi-hour research tasks at a quality that impresses seasoned analysts.

If Phase 2 made AI a coworker, Phase 3 makes it a project manager. A caseworker who handles an entire case independently. A procurement specialist who evaluates suppliers and prepares contracts on their own.
The economic implications are enormous. In Phase 2, AI boosts the productivity of individual employees. In Phase 3, it replaces entire workflows. Not a single step—the whole process. This no longer affects just routine work. It affects qualified, well-paid knowledge work: management consulting, financial analysis, legal services, software development, project management.
And it's coming faster than most believe. Development cycles in AI research are no longer measured in decades but in months. What exists as a research prototype today will be a commercial product in 12 to 18 months. What's a commercial product today will be standard in 24 months. So who's building all of this?

---


### The Landscape of AI Labs

At this point, it's worth surveying who is building the machines that are learning to think. Because the answer to that question determines where we're headed—and where the money flows.

As of spring 2026, there are six organizations building foundation models—the large language models that power everything we've described in Phases 1 through 3. Behind them stand four tech giants that own the infrastructure. And then there's NVIDIA, which supplies them all with chips.


OpenAI opened the market with ChatGPT and still dominates it. GPT-5, released in August 2025, and its successors are used by 800 million people worldwide.^[21]^ Sam Altman openly talks^[22]^ about "personal superintelligence" that his company is building—and has raised over $40 billion in investor capital for it. The planned valuation at IPO: one trillion dollars.^[23]^ OpenAI burns roughly $9 billion per year and plans to lose $14 billion in 2026.^[24]^ The bet is simple: if Altman is right and superintelligence arrives in "a few thousand days," every dollar invested is a bargain. If not, SoftBank has a problem.

Anthropic is the countervoice. Founded by former OpenAI researchers who felt safety wasn't taken seriously enough there. Dario Amodei, the CEO, published an essay in January 2026^[25]^ warning: "The combination of intelligence, agency, and poor controllability is a recipe for existential danger." At the same time, his company just raised $30 billion^[26]^ in a funding round at a $380 billion valuation. Anthropic is the company that warns loudest about the dangers while growing fastest. That's either a contradiction or exactly the right approach.

Google DeepMind is the only AI lab with a Nobel Prize. Demis Hassabis and John Jumper won it in 2024 for AlphaFold^[27]^, which predicted the three-dimensional structure of 200 million proteins—a problem biology had worked on for decades. Google also has Waymo, which generates $350 million in revenue with autonomous taxis and has a valuation of $126 billion.^[28]^ And with "Gemini Robotics," Google is building models that control robots in the physical world. Fundamental research that wins Nobel Prizes and commercial products that drive autonomously.

Then Meta. Mark Zuckerberg is pursuing the most capital-intensive strategy of all: Meta invested $72 billion in AI infrastructure in 2025, with plans for $135 billion in 2026.^[29]^ In a single year. For that, Meta gets data centers with over a million GPUs. And Zuckerberg gives away the best model for free. The Llama models are open source—anyone can download and use them. Why? Because Zuckerberg understood what Google proved with Android: when your model becomes the standard, you control the ecosystem—even if you give the model away.

Elon Musk's xAI is the fastest-scaling player. In less than two years, Musk installed 555,000 GPUs in a single data center in Memphis, invested $18 billion, and then merged xAI with SpaceX.^[30]^ Grok, the language model, is deeply integrated into the X platform, reaching^[31]^ 600 million monthly active users combined with X. The Pentagon partnership, integrating Grok into Department of Defense networks, shows the ambition. Whether Musk's promise to achieve AGI by 2026 is realistic remains to be seen. But the infrastructure he's building is real.

Apple takes a completely different path. Instead of burning hundreds of billions on AI training, Apple bets on partnerships—currently with Google^[32]^ Gemini for a billion dollars per year—and processes everything sensitive on-device. Privacy first. The more than 2.35 billion active Apple devices worldwide represent a distribution advantage no other player has. Apple doesn't build foundation models, but Apple controls the devices on which everyone else's models run. That's a different kind of moat.

Microsoft invested $13.75 billion in OpenAI and received 27 percent of the company.^[34]^ At the same time, Satya Nadella is integrating AI into everything: Windows, Office, GitHub, Azure. GitHub Copilot—the AI coding assistant that now generates nearly half of all new code on the platform—we've already discussed. But Microsoft also hedges: Azure now runs models from Anthropic, Meta, xAI, and DeepSeek alongside OpenAI. Nadella doesn't want to depend on a single model.


![The AI ecosystem—labs, hyperscalers, and their interconnections](images-en/abb-05.png)


Amazon controls the world's largest cloud infrastructure with AWS and has invested $8 billion in^[35]^ Anthropic. The twist: Amazon builds its own AI chips—the Trainium series—and Anthropic is the biggest customer. This reduces Amazon's dependence on NVIDIA, which holds over 80^[36]^ percent market share in training chips.

The combined investments of these companies in AI infrastructure exceeded half a trillion dollars in 2025. Jensen Huang, NVIDIA's CEO, estimates they will surpass one trillion dollars by 2028.^[37]^ These aren't sums a single company or a single government can raise. This is an industry building itself.

What matters for this book's thesis: none of these players is just trying to build a better language model. They're all simultaneously building autonomous agents (Phase 3) and physical AI (Phase 4). OpenAI is investing in robotics. Google is building Gemini Robotics. NVIDIA is developing foundation models for humanoid robots. Meta is pouring $135 billion into infrastructure that powers both. The convergence isn't my invention. It's the declared strategy of the most powerful companies on Earth.

The specific robotics players—from Tesla to Boston Dynamics to Chinese challengers like Unitree—I'll cover in the following section on Phase 4. The key point here: the AI labs and tech giants aren't just investing in software. They're investing in the physical world.


---

### Phase 4: Embodied AI — Robots (2026–2035)
And then intelligence gets a body.
So far, we've talked about AI that exists in the digital world—software that writes text, generates code, analyzes data. But the physical world—factories, warehouses, construction sites, hospitals, fields, roads—has remained largely untouched. That's changing.

Tesla unveiled its humanoid robot Optimus and plans larger-scale production starting in 2026.^[38]^ In Tesla's own factories, over a thousand prototypes are working on tasks: sorting parts, transporting objects. Musk admitted in January 2026 that^[39]^ "none of the Optimus robots are doing useful work." That's typical Musk: the vision is huge, reality lags behind, and yet it would be a mistake to write off the ambition. That Tesla is shutting down Model S and Model X production to retool the Fremont factory for Optimus manufacturing^[40]^ shows how serious he is. When a humanoid robot costing $20,000 to $30,000 does the work of a human who costs $40,000 to $60,000 per year, the investment pays for itself in less than a year. And the robot works around the clock, doesn't get sick, doesn't go on strike, and gets better with every software update.

But Tesla isn't alone—and that's the real news.
Boston Dynamics, owned by Hyundai since 2020, unveiled the new electric Atlas at CES in January 2026.^[41]^ Not the hydraulic parkour robot from the viral videos, but an industrial-grade machine already working in Hyundai's automotive plant in Georgia. All production slots for 2026 are sold out. Hyundai plans to deploy tens of thousands of Atlas units across its plants worldwide. The AI comes from Google DeepMind, which is also sourcing Atlas fleets for its robotics lab. Google's brain plus Hyundai's manufacturing capacity—don't underestimate that combination.

Figure AI achieved something with the Figure 02^[42]^ that no other robotics startup can claim: a 20-hour shift in a BMW factory. Not a demo, not a controlled environment—real work on a production line, inserting sheet metal parts, a task that requires fine motor skills. The company^[43]^ was founded two years ago and is now worth $39 billion. The investor list: Jeff Bezos, NVIDIA, Microsoft, OpenAI.
1X Technologies from Norway launched NEO, the first humanoid robot for consumers: $20,000 or $499 per month as a subscription.^[44]^ Preorders are open, delivery in 2026. EQT, one of Europe's largest private equity firms, signed a deal for up to 10,000 units.^[45]^ This isn't research anymore. This is a business.

And then Unitree from China. Humanoid robots at a fraction of the Western price. It's the same pattern as BYD versus Tesla, DJI versus Western drone makers: China takes a technology and scales it faster and cheaper. Anyone who thinks the robotics market will be a purely American game has learned nothing from the smartphone market.
But the real breakthrough in robotics isn't the hardware. It's how robots learn.
Humans learn individually. When I practice piano, you don't get better. When you learn a new language, that knowledge remains locked in your brain. Our knowledge is trapped in our individual minds, and we can only imperfectly pass it on through language and demonstration.
Robots learn as a network. The principle is called Distributed Reinforcement Learning: one learns kung fu, all learn kung fu.
Every individual robot in a fleet gathers experience. It tries something, fails, adjusts, tries again. Those experiences flow into a central model. The model improves. And then the improved model is pushed back to all robots. Overnight. Without a human doing anything.
That explains why Unitree robots improved so dramatically within a year. The 2024 Unitree was a clumsy prototype stumbling on stairs. The 2026 Unitree performs martial arts moves that would impress professional fighters. The hardware is identical. Same chassis, same motors, same sensors. The only difference is the software—the model trained through distributed learning.
This leads to what economists call economies of scope, as opposed to the familiar economies of scale. Scale is about making the same thing cheaper. Scope is about training once and being able to do everything. The same robot, with the same body and the same AI, can theoretically fold laundry or perform heart surgery. The capability doesn't lie in specialized hardware. It lies in the trained model.
Train once, do everything. Deploy once, handle anything.
We've never seen a tool this versatile. Humans were the next best example.

What does this mean for the timeline? The limitation is no longer intelligence. The robots are now—or will soon be—smart enough to handle most physical tasks. The limitation is the supply chain. How fast can we build robot bodies? Seventy million cars are produced per year. Seventy million motorcycles. Humanoid robots fall somewhere in between in complexity. Building the supply chain takes three to five years. That's the real bottleneck. Not intelligence—production.

But once production capacity is in place, an Optimus-level robot costs less than one dollar per hour to operate. What does that do to an economy where human labor costs $20 to $50 per hour?

NVIDIA deserves special mention—not as a robotics manufacturer, but as a platform provider. With GR00T, NVIDIA released the first open-source foundation model for humanoid robots. With Cosmos, "World Foundation Models" that simulate the physical world. With Omniverse Digital Twins, where robots train virtually. Jensen Huang wants to build the "Android of robotics": the robots come from Tesla, Boston Dynamics, Figure, or Unitree. But the software comes from NVIDIA.
Goldman Sachs forecasts the humanoid robotics market at $38 billion by 2035.^[46]^ That sounds like a lot, but it's conservative. If humanoid robots can actually replace human labor in factories, logistics, healthcare, and construction, then the addressable market isn't $38 billion. It's the entirety of global wage labor—a market worth dozens of trillions of dollars annually.

---


::: {.infobox}
### DeepSeek and the End of the Compute Monopoly


![DeepSeek—The Sputnik Moment of AI (training costs compared)](images-en/abb-06.png)


In January 2025, something happened that shook the entire AI industry. And it didn't come from San Francisco.

DeepSeek, a Chinese AI lab, released an open-source language model^[47]^ that competed with the best models from OpenAI and Google in many benchmarks—trained at a fraction of the cost. While OpenAI spends hundreds of millions of dollars training a single model and needs tens of thousands of the most expensive NVIDIA chips, DeepSeek showed that with clever architecture and less hardware, comparable results are achievable.

The implication was profound—not because DeepSeek was the "better" model (in many areas it wasn't), but because it challenged a foundational assumption of the industry: the assumption that AI progress is primarily a function of compute power. More GPUs equals better models. That equation, which had made NVIDIA the most valuable company in the world, was suddenly no longer so clear-cut.

DeepSeek proved several things simultaneously.

US export controls on high-performance chips aren't stopping Chinese AI research—they're forcing it to become more efficient. The restrictions forced the Chinese to develop models that achieve more with less^[63]^ compute. Economists call that induced innovation: scarcity breeds creativity.

At the same time, DeepSeek showed that open source in AI isn't dead. Meta had already proved with LLaMA that open models can compete with closed ones. DeepSeek confirmed that emphatically. When the best models are available for free, OpenAI and Anthropic can't charge monopoly prices for access. Value shifts from the models themselves to the infrastructure, applications, and data built on top of them.

But the most important lesson for investors is this: the AI market won't be a winner-takes-all market. It will be an ecosystem, similar to the internet—not controlled by a single company, but by a network of infrastructure providers, platforms, and applications.

For founders, this is the most important news since the rise of cloud computing. A startup in Berlin with ten engineers can take an open-source model today, fine-tune it for their industry, and build a product that only Google or OpenAI could have developed three years ago. The barrier to entry in the AI market has dropped from hundreds of millions of dollars to the cost of a small dev team plus a cloud budget. That's changing the startup landscape more profoundly than any venture capital trend of the last decade.

The DeepSeek moment was to the AI industry what the Sputnik shock of 1957 was to the space race: a wake-up call.
:::


---

### The Kurzweil Spiral

Ray Kurzweil^[207]^ predicted this exponential development decades ago^[48]^. In *The Singularity Is Near*, he described the Law of Accelerating Returns: technological progress accelerates because each generation of technology provides the tools to develop the next one faster.

AI accelerates chip development. Better chips accelerate AI. Faster AI accelerates robotics. Better robots build better chips. It's a spiral, and it's spinning faster and faster.

The problem is that the human brain isn't built for exponential thinking. We think linearly. When something grows 10 percent per year, we intuitively estimate it'll be about twice as large in 10 years. In reality, it's 2.6 times as large. At 20 percent annual growth, we intuitively expect a tripling in 10 years. In reality, it's a six-fold increase. And at the growth rates we're seeing in AI—performance doubling every 12 to 18 months—the numbers after a decade are so large that they defy intuitive understanding.

An example: if an AI system's performance doubles every 18 months, in 10 years it won't be 10 times better—it'll be roughly 100 times better. In 15 years, over 1,000 times. In 20 years, over 10,000 times. Those numbers sound absurd—until you remember that a smartphone today has the computing power of a 1990 supercomputer. That was exactly this exponential path, over exactly this timeframe.

Kurzweil predicted that AI would reach human-level capability by 2029.^[49]^ Whether he's right, we'll know in three years. But even if he's off by five or ten years, it doesn't change the big picture. The direction is clear. The acceleration is measurable.

For skeptics: yes, exponential curves eventually flatten. Every S-curve has a plateau. But the history of technology shows that the plateau of one technology is typically the starting point of the next. Vacuum tubes hit their plateau—transistors took over. Kurzweil argues that there isn't a single exponential curve, but a cascade of S-curves that together produce an exponential trend. The data so far supports this.

---


### Each Phase Expands the Market by an Order of Magnitude

The dimensions, quantified, because they're central to the investment thesis:

Phase 1 affected software developers and early adopters; the addressable market was a few billion dollars annually for AI tools and services.

Phase 2 affects knowledge workers and businesses. The addressable market: hundreds of billions of dollars annually. These estimates refer to exactly this phase. Every company with office workers, from law firms to DAX-listed corporations.

Phase 3 affects the entire service economy. When AI agents can autonomously execute entire workflows, it touches consulting, financial services, administration, healthcare, education, logistics. The addressable market: trillions of dollars annually. Services account for over 70 percent of GDP in developed countries.

Phase 4 affects the physical world: factories, logistics, healthcare, agriculture, construction. This is no longer about office work—it's about the entirety of humanity's physical value creation. Global economic output is roughly $100 trillion. Robotics combined with AI addresses a substantial share of that.

The pattern is clear: with each phase, the addressable market grows by roughly an order of magnitude. And with each phase, the urgency of being on the right side of this shift grows.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ Four phases: Tool → Coworker → Agent → Robot.
>
> ◆ Each phase expands the addressable market by an order of magnitude.
>
> ◆ Zero marginal costs and exponential learning create disruptive cost advantages.

## Chapter 3: Why Machines Need Crypto


---

> *"Money is only a tool. It will take you wherever you wish, but it will not replace you as the driver."*
>
> — Ayn Rand

---


![Why machines can't open a bank account](images-en/abb-07.png)


This is where it gets interesting. And this is where the thesis lies that most investors haven't grasped yet.

In the first two chapters, we saw how AI evolves from a tool to a coworker to an autonomous agent—and eventually gets a physical body. But one question remains unanswered: when these machines act autonomously—signing contracts, purchasing services, allocating resources—how do they pay?

The answer to that question is the core of what makes this book different from every other AI book.

Before we dive into the theory, a story that happened to me while writing this book.

I needed a domain for the book's companion project. Jeannie, my AI agent, researched available domains and suggested TheAiSpecies.world. Good, I thought—go ahead and register it. Jeannie has access to my Cloudflare account, an API key with full permissions, a credit card on file. She can create DNS records, configure redirects, set up SSL certificates. She can do everything you can do with a domain.

Except buy one.

Cloudflare's API simply doesn't allow domain registrations through the machine interface. The purchase goes through a web interface that requires a human session. Jeannie tried, failed, and I had to make the three clicks myself.

It was annoying. But it was also an aha moment. Here was an agent that could do everything except pay. Not because the money was missing, not because the authorization was missing, but because the system through which money flows was built for humans. For fingers on screens. For eyes that solve CAPTCHAs. For brains that click away cookie banners.

That is exactly the problem this chapter describes.


---

::: {.infobox}
### The Thought Experiment: An AI Agent Needs a Bank Account

Let's think this through concretely.

An AI agent working for a mid-size company needs to complete the following tasks: rent a cloud server for additional compute capacity on a running project, commission a specialized data analysis service to prepare certain market data, and acquire an API license to access an external knowledge base. Three routine business operations that a human employee could handle in an hour.

Now imagine the AI agent trying to do this in today's financial system.

For the cloud server, it needs a credit card. For the credit card, a bank account. For the bank account, a legal or natural person as account holder. And for opening the account, KYC documentation—identity verification, proof of address, business purpose declaration. Add business hours, transfer times of one to three business days, fees, and the bank's ability at any time to reject, delay, or freeze the transaction.

The system was built for humans—with human identity, human documents, human signatures, and human business hours. A machine doesn't fit. Not because the technology is missing, but because the institutional architecture of the financial system is built on the foundational assumption that economic actors are humans.

That assumption was correct for the last 500 years of modern banking. Over the next 10 years, it will become obsolete.
:::

---


### Crypto as the Native Payment Channel for Machines

Cryptocurrencies solve this problem. Not as an ideological statement against the banking system, but as a technical necessity.

An AI agent needs only a private key to sign transactions. Think of this key as a password that simultaneously serves as account, signature, and ID. No bank, no identity verification, no business hours—this single string is enough to send and receive value to any other key in the world, at any time, in seconds.

Crypto is not "digital money for tech enthusiasts." It's the only existing infrastructure built for machine economic participants. Or at least, built in a way that machines can use it without a human standing in between as intermediary.

Balaji Srinivasan, former CTO of Coinbase,^[50]^ anticipated this concept in his thinking about the "Network State" and the future of money. He argues that the next billion "users" in the crypto ecosystem won't be humans—they'll be machines. AI agents, IoT devices, autonomous vehicles, robots. They'll all need to make transactions. And they'll all need a payment system that is natively digital, permissionless, and programmable.

Permissionless is the keyword. In the traditional financial system, every transaction works like a bouncer system: every participant needs permission from an institution—a bank, a regulator, a credit card company. In the crypto system, there's no bouncer. An AI agent can create a wallet and begin transacting within milliseconds. No waiting. No application. No human clicking "approve."

> That's not anarchy. It's efficiency. And in a world where billions of machines need to conduct billions of transactions per day, that efficiency isn't optional.

---

### Smart Contracts: Contracts That Machines Can Understand

Payments alone aren't enough. Economic activity requires agreements: terms, service descriptions, payment schedules, liability provisions. In the human system, we call these contracts. They're written in natural language, often dozens of pages long, legally complex, and requiring human interpretation.

Machines can't "read" contracts like humans. They can process natural language—large language models are very good at that—but the legal interpretation of a contract, with all its gray areas, precedents, and cultural conventions, is different from parsing text.

Smart contracts on Ethereum or Solana solve this problem. A smart contract works, in principle, like a vending machine: you put money in, the condition is met, the product comes out—no human in between. Except the "machine" is a piece of code living on a blockchain that executes automatically when certain conditions are met. "Pay 100 USDC when the API service has processed 1,000 successful requests." "Transfer ownership of the digital asset when payment is received." "Escalate to a human arbitrator if the service has been unavailable for more than 48 hours."

That's a programmable economy. Machines can't interpret legal text, but they can execute code. Smart contracts are contracts in a language machines understand.

### The Stablecoin Economy

One of the most persistent misconceptions about cryptocurrencies is that they're too volatile for economic use. Bitcoin swings five percent in a day. Ethereum can lose 20 percent in a week. How is an AI agent supposed to do business with that?

The answer is stablecoins: crypto-based tokens pegged to the value of a fiat currency, typically the US dollar. Think of a stablecoin as a digital dollar bill that can be sent over the internet—no bank, no waiting.

USDC and USDT are the most prominent examples. One USDC is always worth approximately one dollar. It's transferred on a blockchain, with all the advantages—near-instant, around the clock, worldwide, without bank intermediation, and without the volatility of Bitcoin or Ethereum.

The numbers are already impressive. Stablecoins processed over $27 trillion in transaction volume in 2024—surpassing Visa.^[62]^ $27 trillion in real value, moved over blockchain networks, cross-border, without correspondent banks, without SWIFT messages, without delays.


![Stablecoin volume overtakes Visa (2024: $27.6 trillion)](images-en/abb-08.png)


This growth isn't driven by speculators. It's driven by utility. Companies in Latin America, Africa, and Southeast Asia use stablecoins for international transfers because the traditional banking system is too slow, too expensive, or simply nonexistent. A freelancer in Nigeria can receive stablecoins in seconds, while a bank transfer takes days and costs 5 to 10 percent in fees.

Tether, the issuer of USDT, is one of the most profitable companies in the world measured by profit per employee. With fewer than 100 employees, Tether earns billions in interest on the dollar reserves backing USDT tokens. Users give dollars, receive USDT. Tether invests the dollars in short-term US Treasury bills and pockets the interest. As long as rates are positive and demand for USDT exists, it's a money-printing machine.

Circle, the issuer of USDC, pursues a similar model and plans an IPO. For investors, this is one of the most direct ways to profit from the stablecoin economy: not by owning stablecoins themselves (which are price-stable by definition), but through stakes in the companies building the infrastructure.


---

### Microtransactions and the Layer-2 Revolution
Another key to the machine economy is microtransactions. This is where the traditional financial system proves structurally inadequate.
When an AI agent sends a single API request to a service, it might cost $0.001—a tenth of a cent. In the traditional payment system, that's unworkable. A credit card transaction costs the merchant at least 30 cents plus a percentage. A bank wire costs several dollars. The transaction fees are higher than the transaction value.
On Layer-2 blockchains—think of them as express lanes running parallel to a congested highway—transactions cost fractions of a cent. These networks build on existing blockchains, leverage their security, but process transactions faster and cheaper. Arbitrum, Optimism, and Base on Ethereum; the Lightning Network on Bitcoin—they all enable transactions for under $0.01. Some for under $0.001.
An AI agent can pay for every single API request in real time, without transaction costs exceeding the value. It can buy compute time on a decentralized GPU network for $0.0001. It can purchase a data set from another agent for $0.005. It can conduct hundreds of microtransactions per minute—something physically impossible in a banking system.
The infrastructure for the machine economy already exists. It's just not yet being used at the scale that's coming.

---


### Machine-to-Machine Economy: What's Already Happening

Machine-to-machine payments aren't science fiction. Working projects already exist in which AI agents operate on blockchains.

Fetch.ai has built a network where autonomous agents offer, request, and pay for services^[59]^—without human intervention. An agent optimizing transport routes pays another agent that provides real-time traffic data. An agent forecasting energy consumption buys weather data from a weather agent. Every transaction runs on the blockchain, secured by smart contracts.

Ocean Protocol has created a data marketplace^[60]^ where data providers can tokenize their datasets and sell them automatically. AI models can purchase training data without a human brokering the deal. Price is determined by supply and demand, payment runs through crypto, and access rights are managed by smart contracts.

These are still small markets—prototypes, not mass infrastructure. But they demonstrate the principle. And when the agent economy gains momentum in Phases 3 and 4—when millions of AI agents transact autonomously and hundreds of thousands of robots operate in the physical world—demand for crypto-based payment systems won't grow linearly. It will explode.

But how big will it really get? There's a thinking error that almost every analyst makes.

When I started modeling the Total Addressable Market for crypto, I did what everyone else does: projected user growth, arrived at two billion active wallets by 2030. Looks impressive. It's also completely wrong.

I forgot the agents.

Raoul Pal, former Goldman Sachs executive and founder of Real Vision, put it this way: we're about to throw a billion agents into the economy. Then two billion. Maybe ten billion. Every single one of these agents needs a wallet. Every single one will transact. Not once a day like a human paying rent. Hundreds of times per hour. Thousands of times per day. "Who is your end customer in this new world? Your new customer is the agent."

And agents are different from humans. They're not loyal. They're not emotional. They're not tribal. A human stays with their bank because they're too lazy to switch. An agent switches to the better deal in milliseconds. A human buys Ethereum because they believe in Vitalik. An agent buys whichever chain has the lowest fees and fastest settlement. Agents optimize—they don't believe.

The payment infrastructure is being built right now. Stripe launched its own chain with Tempo. OpenAI and Anthropic are both in the first batch of integration partners. Elon Musk announced that X Money will go live in weeks. Everyone understands: whoever controls the payment layer controls the agent economy.

But there's a problem Emad Mostaque has pointed out: the US dollar is only divisible to one cent. What do you do when a transaction should cost $0.00001? With exponential cost deflation, exactly these amounts become normal. The granularity of the dollar isn't sufficient for an economy where billions of microtransactions happen per second. Crypto doesn't have this problem. You can divide one Bitcoin into 100 million satoshis. You can divide one ETH into a trillion wei. That's not a technical gimmick. It's a fundamental prerequisite for the machine economy.

And then there's the reversal that hardly anyone discusses: Rent a Human. Today, humans pay machines for services. Tomorrow, machines pay humans for tasks they can't handle. Who has the money in this new order? The AI corporations. OpenAI, Anthropic, Google DeepMind—they'll all run agents that generate billions. Those agents will commission humans to work for them. The question of who's the master and who's the servant becomes philosophically interesting. Economically, it's already answered: the master is whoever has the money.

Within five years, Raoul Pal estimates, agents will contribute more to economic output than humans. The fundamental equation of monetary theory—MV = PQ—gets a new variable: the velocity of agents. And that velocity isn't limited by human slowness. It's limited by the speed of the blockchain.


---

### x402: How HTTP Status Code 402 Turned the Internet into a Payment Terminal
And then something happened that should have happened thirty years ago.
In May 2025, Coinbase published a protocol called x402.^[51]^ The name sounds technical, but the idea is simple: since 1992, the HTTP standard^[52]^—the foundation of the entire internet—has included a status code numbered 402. Its meaning: "Payment Required." For thirty years, it was a dead letter. Nobody used it because there was no digital money that was fast enough, cheap enough, and programmable enough to ride along in an HTTP request.
Stablecoins changed that.
Here's how x402 works: An AI agent calls an API—say it needs market data from a data provider. The server doesn't respond with the data but with HTTP 402: "Pay first." Plus a machine-readable invoice: 0.003 USDC, on Base, to this wallet address. The agent signs the payment, sends it back as an HTTP header, a facilitator settles the transaction on-chain, and the server delivers the data. All in under one second. No registration, no API key, no credit card. The machine paid. On its own.
What sounds like theory already has facts: by the end of 2025, x402 had processed over one hundred million transactions. Four hundred thousand buyers, eighty thousand sellers.^[53]^ Not in a lab—on the open internet. Coinbase isn't alone. Cloudflare co-founded the x402 Foundation with Coinbase. Google integrated x402 into its Agent-to-Agent protocol.^[54]^ Anthropic, AWS, Circle, Solana, and Aptos joined in. This isn't a fringe phenomenon. It's the birth of a standard.
What distinguishes x402 from earlier attempts—from Bitcoin Lightning to Facebook's failed Libra—is the combination of four factors: it leverages an existing HTTP standard, uses stablecoins instead of volatile cryptocurrencies, runs on Layer-2 chains with sub-cent fees, and has the support of the largest infrastructure providers on the internet.

But that's just the digital side. The real revolution begins where machine money meets the physical world.

---


::: {.infobox}
### The Car That Finances Its Own Life

On October 3, 2025, the company Robo.ai^[55]^ presented something at TOKEN2049 in Dubai that sounds like a joke: a wallet for a car. Not an app you install on your phone, but a digital wallet built into the vehicle itself. Called Roboy339, it can autonomously pay tolls, settle charging costs, transfer parking fees, and book maintenance appointments. Without the driver doing anything. Without the driver even noticing.

That sounds like a nice feature. But think the thought through to its end.

Imagine a robotaxi that starts its shift at six in the morning. It has no driver, no owner sitting in an office assigning jobs, and no dispatcher planning the day. It has a wallet.

The taxi drives a passenger to the airport. 28.40 USDC flows into its wallet. It drives to the nearest charging station—not because someone sent it there, but because its battery management agent calculated that prices at the station on Industriestraße are currently 22 percent below the daily average. 4.80 USDC for 35 kilowatt-hours. The charging station sends a receipt—not by email, but as a confirmed transaction on the blockchain.

At the end of the day, the taxi has earned $340. Now its accounting begins. $80 flows to the owner as a lease payment. $12 to the tire service—a sensor registered that tread depth is at 73 percent, so the maintenance agent books an appointment for next week and wires the deposit. $5 goes into a reserve fund for the next inspection. The rest is parked in a stablecoin pool where it earns interest until it's needed.

This car is no longer a mode of transportation. It's an economic agent. A business on four wheels that generates revenue, optimizes expenses, builds reserves, and manages its own balance sheet. And its operating currency is stablecoins.

This isn't science fiction. Bosch demonstrated with the moveID project on the blockchain platform peaq how vehicles receive their own digital identity^[56]^ and autonomously settle with charging stations and parking lots. Peer-to-peer. No intermediary. DIMO, a decentralized network with over 180,000 connected vehicles^[57]^, goes even further: the cars sell their driving data—location, diagnostics, driving behavior—directly to insurers and urban planners, and owners are rewarded in tokens.

The question is no longer whether machines will get a bank account. The question is what happens when three hundred million connected vehicles simultaneously start doing business on their own.
:::


---

::: {.infobox}
### The Drone and the Pad Thai

Wednesday, 12:30 PM. You tell your AI assistant: "I'm hungry—something Thai."

In the old days, you'd open an app, scroll through menus, read reviews, enter your credit card info, and maybe spend half an hour deciding between Pad Thai and Massaman Curry. Your assistant does it in four seconds.

It queries three Thai restaurants nearby via x402. Not by phone call—by HTTP. The query costs 0.001 USDC per restaurant, paid the moment the server responds. The restaurants have their own AI systems that check in real time: What's in stock? How busy is the kitchen? How long will preparation take? What can arrive at the customer's location in 25 minutes?

Two offers come back. The assistant picks the Pad Thai from Siam Corner: 16.50 USDC, including a modification because it knows you're allergic to peanuts. It pays immediately.

But here's where it gets interesting. Your assistant doesn't just pay for the food. It pays for the logistics. An autonomous delivery drone is booked—2.80 USDC for the 2.3-kilometer flight. The drone pays 0.003 USDC in airspace fees to the city en route and 0.001 USDC to a weather service for real-time wind data on its route.

Seven transactions. Three physical service providers. Zero human approvals. Your Pad Thai arrives in 22 minutes.
:::

> *What happened here deserves a second look. Not because of the technology—drone deliveries and AI assistants already exist. But because of the economics behind it. In the old world, this transaction needed a platform like Lieferando, which takes a 30 percent commission. A payment processor that charges 2.9 percent plus 30 cents. A contractual framework between restaurant, platform, and delivery service. Bank accounts, credit cards, monthly statements.*
> *In the new world, there are only agents and stablecoins. Every agent has a wallet. Every transaction settles in milliseconds, for fractions of a cent in fees. No middleman takes 30 percent—the value stays with the restaurant and the customer.*
> *That's machine money in the physical world. Not just bits paying for bits. Agents paying for atoms.*

---


### The City as a Machine Marketplace

::: {.szene}
2035. A major city. Millions of devices—cars, drones, buildings, charging stations, traffic lights, sensors, robots—have their own wallets. They trade with each other. Around the clock. Without human oversight.

The traffic light at the intersection of Schillerstraße sells its traffic data to passing robotaxis. 0.0001 USDC per data point: current red phase, projected green start, queue length in every direction. The taxis use the data to optimize their routes. They pay the traffic light—or more precisely, the decentralized infrastructure network that operates it.

A delivery drone books a landing pad on the roof of an office tower. 0.15 USDC for three minutes. The building earns more from that than from the parking space on the ground floor, which has been empty anyway since nobody drives to work anymore.

The solar array on the neighboring rooftop produced more electricity today than the house consumed. The surplus flows automatically to three neighbors, paid in USDC through a decentralized energy grid. The heat pump in the basement detects a 12 percent efficiency drop, commissions a diagnostic agent for 0.50 USDC, gets the result in four seconds—refill the refrigerant. And books a technician from the nearest decentralized contractor network. Appointment: Thursday 2 PM. Advance payment: 45 USDC.

In this city, there's not a moment when machines aren't trading with each other. The road charges the car a usage fee per kilometer—not as a flat tax, but in real time, based on weight, time of day, and road condition. The trash robot pays the elevator for the ride to the underground garage. The autonomous warehouse orders supplies from a factory 200 kilometers away. And the factory commissions an autonomous truck for delivery.

All of this doesn't happen alongside the human economy. It happens within it. The machines aren't a separate economy. They're a layer that slides beneath the human economy—invisible, efficient, permanent. Just as the internet slid beneath commerce without us still saying "online shopping," the machine economy will slide beneath daily life without us noticing it.
:::

DIMO is taking the first step today: 180,000 vehicles with blockchain identities that sell their own data. peaq hosts 6 million machine addresses across 60 decentralized infrastructure projects in 22 industries.^[58]^ The building blocks are there. The city I just described won't emerge all at once. It will grow together piece by piece—one car, one drone, one charging station at a time.

But it will emerge. Because the economic logic is inescapable: machines that can act autonomously will act autonomously. Machines that can pay independently will pay independently. And once they start, there's no reason to stop.

### Bitcoin as Anchor, Ethereum as Operating System

In this economy, Bitcoin and Ethereum play fundamentally different roles. Understanding both is important because confusing the two leads to bad investment decisions.

Bitcoin is not a payment system for microtransactions—on the base layer, it's too slow and too expensive for that. The Lightning Network makes Bitcoin payments fast and cheap, but Bitcoin's primary role in the machine economy is different: it's the hardest money ever created.

21 million units—not inflatable, not confiscatable, controlled by no government, and manipulable by no central bank.

In a world where AI massively boosts productivity and creates deflationary pressure, while governments simultaneously print money to maintain social stability and fund universal basic income, a scarce, neutral store of value becomes the anchor of the system. Bitcoin is digital gold—but gold you can send around the world in seconds, that fits on a USB stick, and that belongs to no government.

Ethereum positions itself as the operating system of the decentralized economy. It's the platform on which smart contracts run, DeFi protocols operate, and AI agents settle their transactions. Ethereum isn't scarce like Bitcoin—it has no fixed cap—but since the Merge to Proof-of-Stake, it trends deflationary: during high network usage, more ETH is burned than created.

The competition is real. Solana is faster. Arbitrum is cheaper. New Layer-1 blockchains emerge constantly. But Ethereum's network effects and security are hard to match. Most DeFi protocols, most NFT projects, most AI agent implementations run on Ethereum or Ethereum-compatible networks today. In technology markets, the best technology doesn't always win—the one with the biggest ecosystem does.

Solana deserves a closer look in this context because it plays a complementary role to Ethereum, not a competing one. If Ethereum is the operating system where the big applications run, then Solana is the high-frequency layer for everything that needs to be fast and cheap. Transactions on Solana cost fractions of a cent and confirm in under a second. For the machine economy, that's critical: when AI agents conduct hundreds of microtransactions per minute, every millisecond and every fraction of a cent in fees matters.

Particularly exciting are the so-called DePIN projects on Solana—decentralized physical infrastructure networks. Helium has built a global IoT and mobile network^[64]^ where every participant provides coverage and gets paid in tokens. Render allows GPU owners to rent their unused graphics processors to a network^[65]^ that distributes rendering tasks for film, architecture, and AI training. Both projects show a pattern: machines and infrastructure trade autonomously with each other, and the payment system runs on Solana.

The stablecoin numbers underscore the relevance. Stablecoins processed roughly $27.6 trillion in transaction volume in 2024. For comparison: Visa, the world's largest card payment network, handled about $13 trillion. A large and growing share of this stablecoin volume runs on Solana, because the low fees and high speed are especially attractive for automated and high-frequency payments. If you're wondering whether crypto payment systems are "just a gimmick": a network that moves twice the volume of Visa is no longer a gimmick.

---

### The Core Thesis: Machines Need Machine Money

When machines become economic actors—when they autonomously buy, sell, negotiate, and invest—they need a monetary system built for machines. One that doesn't require human identity. One that works around the clock. One that enables microtransactions for fractions of a cent. One that's programmable—where rules exist as code, not as statute.

The traditional financial system can't deliver that. It was built centuries ago for humans and is administered by institutions optimized for human timeframes, human amounts, and human identities.

Crypto isn't the perfect solution. The technology is young, regulation unclear, volatility high for many tokens. But it's the only existing infrastructure that meets the fundamental requirements of the machine economy: digital-native, permissionless, programmable, global.

That's why this book isn't just an AI book or a crypto book, but a book about the convergence of both. AI without crypto is an engine without fuel. Crypto without AI is fuel without an engine. Only together do they create something that actually transforms the global economy.

> *Machine money. The money machines need to function in a world they're helping to create.*

As for what this machine money might concretely look like, there are now initial designs. Emad Mostaque proposes a dual currency system in^[66]^ *The Last Economy*: "Foundation Coins" for the physical world of scarce resources. This currency is only minted when verifiable, useful compute is performed for an open knowledge commons. Money tied to crystallized intelligence, not gold or sovereign debt. Alongside that, "Culture Credits" for the digital world of abundance. With built-in decay—a kind of demurrage—ensuring the money flows rather than accumulates. The credits reward creativity, culture, and human connection.

You don't have to consider Mostaque's specific design the final word. But the core idea is compelling: an economy where machines produce abundance while humans live in a world of scarcity probably needs more than one currency. Bitcoin as digital gold, stablecoins as the payment layer, and possibly something entirely new for the machine-to-machine economy. The architecture of machine money won't be designed by a single thinker. It will evolve from practice—from millions of transactions between agents that use what works.

And that practice has already begun. In the world of tokenized assets.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ Machines need their own money because the traditional banking system excludes them.
>
> ◆ Crypto and smart contracts are the native payment channel for autonomous agents.
>
> ◆ The machine-to-machine economy is already functioning today.

## Chapter 4: When Everything Becomes a Token


---

> *"In cryptography we trust."*
>
> — Free after "In God We Trust"

---


![From the physical to the tokenized world](images-en/abb-09.png)


The Democratization of Assets—and Its Limits. Imagine you could buy a piece of the Burj Khalifa. Not a multimillion-dollar condo, but one ten-thousandth of the building. For a hundred dollars. On your smartphone. At 3 AM. And tomorrow, if you need the money for something else, you sell your share. In seconds.

That's not fiction. In Dubai, the Land Department launched a pilot^[67]^ in late 2025 in which real estate is tokenized exactly like that. Sold out in hours.
