Now multiply that principle. Not just real estate. Everything. Company shares. Patents. Farmland in Iowa. Rental income from an office building in Frankfurt. The rights to an Ed Sheeran song. A Picasso hanging in a vault in Geneva. A wind farm in the North Sea. A venture capital fund investing in the next OpenAI.

Tokenization means: every asset in the world is broken into small, tradeable, programmable digital units. On a blockchain. With a smart contract that defines the rules. No notary, no broker, no custody fees, no question of whether your bank approves the transaction.

The previous chapter explained why machines need crypto. This chapter explains how the entire real world gets onto the blockchain. And why this might be the biggest shift in the history of investing. Or the biggest disappointment.

### What's Already Happening: Numbers Most People Don't Know

The market for tokenized real-world assets surpassed the $36 billion mark by the end of 2025.^[68]^ Excluding stablecoins. That sounds like a lot but is a rounding error compared to global wealth. The world's total financial markets represent over $900 trillion. The $36 billion is 0.004 percent.

But the growth rate is noteworthy. Since 2020, the market has grown by over 2,200 percent.^[69]^ The forecasts from the big consulting firms read like science fiction: McKinsey expects $2 to $4 trillion by 2030. Boston Consulting Group says $16 trillion. Standard Chartered forecasts $30 trillion by 2034. Cathie Wood's^[238]^ Ark Invest projects over $11 trillion.^[70]^

The spread alone—from $2 to $30 trillion—shows that nobody really knows how fast this goes. Still, capital is moving in this direction. And the growth curve won't be linear but exponential, like every other technology in this book.

### What's Being Tokenized Today?

::: {.infobox}
### How BlackRock Is Tokenizing Finance

Government bonds are the gateway drug for institutions. BlackRock's BUIDL Fund^[71]^, a tokenized money market fund backed by US Treasuries, grew from $40 million at its launch in March 2024 to nearly $3 billion by end of 2025. In less than two years. Franklin Templeton runs its BENJI Fund on the Stellar blockchain. Ondo Finance offers tokenized Treasury yields via USDY to anyone with a wallet. Over $9 billion already sits in tokenized US government bonds.
:::

Real estate has the classic problem tokenization solves: illiquid, expensive, hard to divide, and geographically bound. RealT sells shares of US rental properties starting at $50 per token.^[72]^ Weekly rental distributions directly to your wallet. No property manager, no paperwork. In Dubai, Prypco Mint ran a pilot with the Land Department whose token tranches sold out within hours. The goal: 7 percent of Dubai's entire real estate market tokenized by 2033—that's $16 billion.^[73]^

Private equity has traditionally been reserved for the ultra-wealthy. Minimum investment: $5 million. Waiting list. Exclusive club. Hamilton Lane, one of the world's largest PE managers with $920 billion under management, tokenized funds through the Securitize platform and lowered the minimum investment to $20,000.^[74]^ Securitize also tokenized KKR funds. Private equity for the dentist from Düsseldorf instead of just the family office manager from Zürich.

Private credit—corporate loans outside the banking system—is being tokenized through platforms like Centrifuge and Maple Finance. DeFi investors can invest in real corporate loans and earn yields significantly above government bonds. Maple Finance has originated over^[75]^ $3 billion in loans cumulatively.

And then the niches that together are no longer a niche. Tokenized gold via Paxos and Tether Gold—over $1 billion combined. Music royalties via Royal.io, where fans can buy shares of their favorite artists' earnings. Farmland via AcreTrader. Patents via IPwe on the IBM blockchain. Fractional blue-chip art via Masterworks.

### When the Giants Come In

Crypto startups selling tokenized assets is one thing. BlackRock, JPMorgan, and Goldman Sachs doing it—that's another thing entirely.

BlackRock manages over $10 trillion.^[76]^ When Larry Fink says,^[77]^ "tokenization will be the next generation of markets," that's not a marketing statement from a crypto enthusiast. It's a strategic announcement from the most powerful man in the global financial system.


![Who's tokenizing what? Wall Street giants enter the game](images-en/abb-10.png)


BUIDL, BlackRock's tokenized money market fund, launched in March 2024 on Ethereum. Within a year, it expanded to seven blockchains. And then, in February 2026, something happened that would have been unthinkable five years ago: BUIDL was listed on Uniswap.^[78]^ Uniswap—the world's largest decentralized exchange. BlackRock, the epitome of Wall Street, entering DeFi.

It's as if Deutsche Bank had announced it would sell its funds at a flea market. Except this flea market has a daily trading volume of several billion dollars and is open 24/7.

JPMorgan built its own blockchain platform with Onyx. Kinexys (formerly JPM Coin) settles over $2 billion in daily transactions.^[79]^ The Tokenized Collateral Network transfers money market fund shares as collateral in real time—a process that takes days in the traditional system. In late 2025, JPMorgan announced an Ethereum-based tokenized money fund.

Goldman Sachs built GS DAP, a platform for tokenized bonds.^[80]^ The European Investment Bank has processed several digital bond issuances through it. In July 2025, a partnership with Bank of New York Mellon followed for tokenized money market fund shares.

HSBC launched tokenized gold tokens that are tradeable 24/7. Franklin Templeton runs BENJI on Stellar and Polygon. Fidelity is developing tokenized money market products. And Citibank projects that tokenization will reach $4 to $5 trillion by 2030.^[81]^

### What Does It Mean When These Firms Get Involved?

It means legitimacy. No CFO gets fired for investing in a BlackRock product—even if it runs on a blockchain. The reputational barrier falls.

It means capital. These firms manage dozens of trillions of dollars combined. If even a fraction flows into tokenized assets, the scale shifts.

And it means regulatory momentum. BlackRock and JPMorgan have more lobbyists in Washington than the entire crypto industry combined. If they want tokenization, regulators will follow. Not out of conviction—out of pragmatism.

### Legislation: Why a Token Without Legal Rights Is Just a Receipt on a Napkin

> Here's the hardest point. And the most important.

A tokenized share of a building is worth exactly as much as the legal right behind it. If you buy a token that claims to give you 0.01 percent of an office building in Frankfurt, but no court will enforce that claim, then you've bought a receipt on a napkin. Not an investment.

Tokenization only works with legislation. The technology is the easy problem. The hard problem is: does the legal system recognize a token as proof of ownership? Can I sue with it in court? Do investor protection rules apply? Who's liable if the smart contract has a bug?

The answer in 2026 is no longer a uniform "no." But it's not a uniform "yes" either. What we're seeing is a global race at three speeds. The US Under Trump: From Enemy to Enabler. The reversal of American crypto regulation under the Trump administration is one of the most remarkable policy pivots in recent financial history.

Under SEC Chairman Gary Gensler (2021–2025), the philosophy was "regulation by enforcement": no clear rules, but lawsuits against anyone who dared to offer digital assets. The industry operated in a regulatory gray zone where every innovation was a legal risk.

Under the new SEC Chairman Paul Atkins, that has radically changed. Instead of suing, the SEC now writes rules.

In July 2025, Trump signed the GENIUS Act—the first comprehensive federal law for stablecoins.^[82]^ A nationwide framework for stablecoin issuers with clear requirements for reserves, audits, and financial integrity. That sounds technical but is fundamental: stablecoins are the payment layer on which tokenized assets are traded. Without regulated stablecoins, no regulated tokenization.

In December 2025, the SEC granted the Depository Trust^[83]^ Company—the heart of American securities settlement—a no-action letter for a three-year pilot to tokenize securities custody on blockchains. That's as if the Bundesbank had told Deutsche Börse to settle stocks on Ethereum. The implications can hardly be overstated.

In January 2026, the SEC published its^[84]^ "Playbook for Tokenized Securities": a joint statement from three divisions clarifying how existing securities laws apply to tokenization. The core message: new rails, same rules. As long as the token represents a real security and investor rights are clear, the SEC is open to innovative bookkeeping via blockchain.

And then Nasdaq. In January 2026, the world's largest electronic exchange applied^[85]^ to the SEC for permission to trade tokenized stocks. Not someday. Now.

There is, however, a massive catch—and it goes to the very question this chapter raises. The SEC has made no effort to relax the so-called "Accredited Investor" rules. In the US, most tokenized securities can only be sold to investors who can prove either annual income above $200,000 or net worth above $1 million. That covers about 13 percent of American households.

For the remaining 87 percent, the door to the most lucrative investments remains locked—even with tokenization. The technology democratizes. The regulation doesn't. At least not yet. Europe: Thorough but Slow. Europe regulates. That surprises no one. How Europe regulates, however, is remarkably nuanced.

MiCA—the Markets in Crypto-Assets Regulation—has been fully in effect since December 30, 2024.^[86]^ The world's first comprehensive regulatory framework for crypto assets, valid across all 27 EU member states. Unified rules for stablecoins, crypto exchanges, and wallet providers.

But here's the nuance most people miss: MiCA regulates crypto assets. Tokenized securities—tokens representing stocks, bonds, or fund shares—still fall under existing financial market rules. MiFID II, the Prospectus Regulation, the AIFM Directive. MiCA and traditional financial regulation are two different worlds only now beginning to converge.

The DLT Pilot Regime is the attempt to connect these worlds. A regulatory sandbox that allows tokenized securities to be traded and settled on blockchains under controlled conditions. The sandbox was extended through 2026. And in December 2025, the EU Commission proposed a massive upgrade: larger volume limits, more asset classes, and a path from sandbox to permanent infrastructure.

Germany was a pioneer here. The eWpG (Electronic Securities Act) has been in force since June 2021^[87]^ and enables purely digital securities without a physical certificate. Bonds and fund shares can be issued as crypto-securities on a blockchain, regulated by BaFin. By 2026, this is no longer an experiment: many RWA deals in Germany are already structured as electronic securities on licensed infrastructure.

Switzerland and Liechtenstein are even further ahead. Switzerland's 2021 DLT Act created "ledger-based securities" that are directly enforceable on-chain. The SIX Digital Exchange (SDX) is operational. Liechtenstein, with its Token Container Model, has one of the world's most advanced tokenization laws.

Europe's problem isn't a lack of laws. It's speed. While the US SEC approved a pilot for tokenized securities settlement within months and Nasdaq applied to trade, Europe is still debating sandbox extensions. The laws are more thorough, the implementation slower. The pattern we described in the chapter on geopolitics repeats itself with tokenization.

### Asia: The Pragmatic Laboratory

While the US accelerates and Europe deliberates, Asia builds.

Singapore launched Project Guardian—the world's most ambitious tokenization project.^[88]^ A partnership between the Monetary Authority of Singapore and a who's who of global finance: JPMorgan, DBS Bank, HSBC, BlackRock, Goldman Sachs. By 2025, over 15 tokenization trials had been conducted across 6 currencies, in fixed income, foreign exchange, and fund management.

Project Guardian's significance extends beyond Singapore. In November 2025, MAS and the German Bundesbank signed a Memorandum of Understanding for cross-border tokenization.^[89]^ The British Investment Association joined. In December 2025, Project Guardian published an operational roadmap for tokenized funds. Singapore isn't just building for Singapore. It's building the global infrastructure.

Hong Kong positions itself as the gateway between the Chinese market and the rest of the world. The Hong Kong Monetary Authority has conducted multiple digital bond issuances in various currencies. A stablecoin law took effect in August 2025, with the first licenses being issued in early 2026. Between Hong Kong and Singapore, a regulated token corridor is quietly emerging—a model for cross-border trade in tokenized assets.

Japan is the quiet pioneer. The Japan Financial Services Agency has created a clear framework for Security Token Offerings. SBI Holdings, Nomura, and MUFG are active. And—here's the key point—Japan allows tokenized real estate STOs for^[90]^ retail investors starting at a few hundred dollars minimum. While the US locks out small investors, Japan lets them in. With rules, but without the million-dollar threshold.

Dubai is in a league of its own. The Virtual Assets Regulatory Authority agreed on a nationwide framework with the national financial regulator in August 2025. The Dubai Land Department is running a real estate tokenization pilot whose token tranches sold out. The goal: 7 percent of the entire real estate market tokenized by 2033—approximately $16 billion. The Dubai International Financial Centre launched its own tokenization sandbox for regulated financial instruments in 2025.

## Chapter 5: Stablecoins, Dollar Dominance, and the Debt Bomb

Before we talk about the future of tokenization, we need to talk about something nobody likes to talk about. The foundation.

Tokenization needs a settlement layer. Whether you're buying a fraction of an office building in Munich or an AI agent is trading a Treasury bond, at the end of the day, something has to settle the account. And that something, today, is 99 percent dollar stablecoins. USDT, USDC, and a handful of smaller clones. Over $300 billion in combined market capitalization. Trending steeply upward.

> *That's not a coincidence. That's strategy.*


---

### Stablecoins as a Digital Dollar Export

A number that surprises most people: Tether and Circle together hold more US government bonds than Saudi Arabia. The IMF documented this in its July 2025 External Sector Report.^[91]^ Tether alone sits on roughly $130 billion in short-term Treasury bills.^[92]^ This is no longer a crypto experiment. It's a systemically important buyer of American government debt.

Washington understood this. On July 18, 2025, President Trump signed the GENIUS Act—the first comprehensive stablecoin law in the US. The rules: every licensed stablecoin issuer must back their tokens 1:1 with liquid reserves, preferably US dollars or short-term government bonds. Monthly disclosure. Insolvency protection for holders. The message between the lines: we want stablecoins to buy American government debt. A lot of it. And we'll give you a legal framework for that.

JPMorgan estimates that stablecoins could generate additional dollar demand of $1.4 trillion by 2027.^[93]^ Standard Chartered sees the stablecoin market at $2 trillion by the end of 2028^[94]^, which would mean roughly $1 trillion in new Treasury bill demand. These aren't fantasy numbers. They're the logical consequence of what's already happening.

Because stablecoins are already a shadow banking dollar system. 500 million users, Tether claims.^[95]^ Verifiable? Hard to say. But the direction is clear: in Argentina, Nigeria, Turkey, Vietnam, and dozens of other countries, people use USDT not to speculate but to survive. Their local currency loses value every month. Banks are unreliable or simply unreachable. A dollar stablecoin on a phone is more stable for them than an account at the national bank.

And that's where it gets geopolitical.

Every person holding a dollar stablecoin generates demand for US government bonds. Every transaction in USDT or USDC is a transaction that doesn't happen in yuan, rupee, or euro. Stablecoins export the dollar into regions where the traditional banking system never penetrated. Whether intended or not, they are the most powerful instrument for extending dollar hegemony since the petrodollar system of the 1970s.

### 

### The Counteroffensive: BRICS, the Digital Yuan, and mBridge


The other side of the chessboard isn't sitting still.

The BRICS nations—Brazil, Russia, India, China, South Africa, and by now a dozen additional members—have declared de-dollarization one of their core objectives. The motive is obvious: whoever trades in dollars is vulnerable. The sanctions against Russia after its invasion of Ukraine demonstrated that with brutal clarity. Within days, Russian foreign exchange reserves worth billions were frozen. SWIFT access was severed. For any country that can imagine a similar confrontation with Washington, that was a wake-up call.

China's digital yuan, the e-CNY, has been in pilot operation since 2020 and is now usable in over 25 Chinese cities. But the real leverage isn't the digital yuan itself. It's mBridge: a platform for cross-border central bank payments co-developed by the Bank for International Settlements (BIS). China, Hong Kong, Thailand, the United Arab Emirates, and Saudi Arabia are participating. The idea: settle central bank digital currencies directly against each other, without the detour through dollars and SWIFT.

In early 2026, Russia, China, and India began preparing their CBDCs for interoperability. BRICS Pay, a pilot project for cross-border payments in local currencies, is being tested. "The Unit," an experimental settlement system that completed its 100-unit pilot in October 2025, is designed to enable cross-border trade without dollar intermediation.

Sounds threatening for the dollar? Yes and no.

De-dollarization is easier announced than implemented. The dollar's share of global central bank reserves has fallen to 56 percent—the lowest in 30 years.^[96]^ But it hasn't been replaced by any single alternative. The euro holds 20 percent, the yen 6, the pound 5, the yuan a mere 2 percent. The dollar's losses are spread across a dozen smaller currencies. That looks more like slow erosion than a regime change.

And there's the problem with the BRICS alternative: the members don't trust each other enough. India doesn't want to peg its rupee to the yuan. Brazil has no interest in a system Beijing controls. Russia is in it out of necessity, not conviction. Indonesia is pursuing a pragmatic dual strategy: on one hand, the country is expanding bilateral currency agreements with China, Japan, and South Korea; on the other, Jakarta remains cautious enough not to openly provoke Washington.^[97]^ Trump's threat to impose 100-percent tariffs on BRICS countries has made additional members nervous.

De-dollarization isn't an event. It's a process. And it's paradoxically slowed by stablecoins: when people in developing countries voluntarily choose dollar stablecoins, that strengthens dollar dominance from below—even as their governments try to undermine it from above.

But there's a dimension of programmable money that gets short shrift in the euphoria over stablecoins and tokenization: control.

Programmable money is a double-edged sword. The same technology that allows an AI agent to autonomously pay an invoice also allows a state to attach rules to money. Money that only works in certain stores. Money with an expiration date that must be spent before it lapses. Money that can be frozen at the push of a button when the holder is on a list.

That's not theory. China's e-CNY already has these features built in. In pilot programs, digital yuan was issued with expiration dates to stimulate consumption. The technical architecture allows the People's Bank of China to see every transaction in real time and freeze wallets when needed. Western CBDCs are planning similar transparency layers—though with stricter privacy promises. Promises that can be diluted quickly in a crisis.

The critical difference: Bitcoin and decentralized stablecoins on public blockchains are censorship-resistant. No state can reverse a Bitcoin transaction. CBDCs are the opposite: fully controllable by the issuing central bank. And stablecoins like Tether occupy a gray zone. Tether has repeatedly frozen wallets at the request of US authorities. That makes USDT freer than a bank account but less free than Bitcoin.

For investors, this matters because it refines the thesis: not all programmable money is created equal. The question isn't just whether money goes digital—it will. The question is who programs the rules. And whether you, as the holder, have the right to reject them.

### 

### Europe's Third Way: The Digital Euro


Europe is trying, as it often does, to find a middle path. The European Central Bank has completed its preparation phase for the digital euro and is working on the next stage. If EU lawmakers pass the regulation in the course of 2026, a pilot could launch by mid-2027. Rollout no earlier than 2029.

That sounds late. It is late. While Tether claims to serve 500 million people with dollar stablecoins and China is testing its digital yuan in dozens of cities, Europe is still debating privacy requirements and holding limits. Thoroughness has its merits. But in a race for the future of money, it's a luxury you have to be able to afford.

MiCA imposed strict requirements on stablecoins. And here's an irony: Tether partially withdrew from the European market because compliance costs were too high. The result? European users switched to USDC. Another dollar stablecoin. Europe's regulation protects consumers but paradoxically strengthens dollar dominance in its own single market.

---


### The Control Instrument — Why CBDCs Are a Threat to Freedom

Up to this point, I've written about CBDCs as geopolitical instruments. As chess pieces in the currency war between dollar, yuan, and euro. But there's a dimension that gets short shrift in sober analysis—one that concerns me as an entrepreneur and as a human being.

CBDCs are the most effective control instrument ever invented.

Why this isn't a conspiracy theory but a sober analysis of the technical architecture:

Cash is anonymous. When I spend a fifty-euro bill at a store, no authority knows about it. That's not a bug. It's a feature. Anonymous payment is a cornerstone of individual freedom in a democracy. Not because you have something to hide, but because the absence of surveillance is the prerequisite for free action.

A CBDC eliminates this anonymity. Every transaction is recorded by the central bank. That's not a side effect—it's the design. The ECB speaks of "controlled anonymity" for offline payments below certain amounts. But "controlled anonymity" is an oxymoron. Whoever has control has the data. And whoever has the data has the power.

China's foreign exchange authority SAFE stated openly in October 2023: the e-CNY can be "programmed with expiration dates or restricted to specific purposes." Money that expires. Money that can only be spent on certain things. Money the government can steer at will. Combined with China's Social Credit System, that means: misbehave, and your money expires faster. Or can only be used for basic groceries. Or gets frozen.

That sounds like dystopia. It's already reality. China is testing it.

If you think this is a purely Chinese problem, remember February 2022. When Canadian truckers protested vaccine mandates, the Trudeau government froze over 200 bank accounts under the Emergencies Act—totaling $7.8 million. No trial. No verdict. A political decision, and the accounts were locked. GoFundMe additionally froze donation accounts—$8.4 million—under government pressure. You can feel however you want about the truckers' cause. But the mechanism should give everyone pause: a phone call was enough to lock citizens out of their own money.

And that was with the old system. With commercial banks as intermediaries who could at least theoretically push back. A CBDC eliminates that intermediary. The central bank has direct access. No bank manager asking questions. No detour. One click.

The Human Rights Foundation, which has operated a CBDC Tracker since 2024, puts it bluntly: CBDCs give governments "new instruments of power for comprehensive surveillance, restriction of financial activities, economic disruption, and corruption." That's not the assessment of crypto anarchists. That's the Human Rights Foundation—the same organization that has advocated for human rights in authoritarian regimes for decades.

134 countries are working on CBDCs. 49 have pilot projects. That's 98 percent of global GDP. And most of these countries are not liberal democracies. When I look at the list—China, Russia, India, Nigeria, Saudi Arabia, the UAE—I don't see the promise of innovation. I see the promise of control.

---


## Chapter 6: The Toxic Trinity — CBDC, UBI, and Digital Feudalism

But it gets worse. Because CBDCs don't exist in a vacuum. They converge with two other megatrends this book describes at length: mass unemployment caused by AI and robotics, and the inevitable rise of Universal Basic Income.

::: {.szene}
Imagine the world in 2032. AI agents and robots have taken over 30 to 50 percent of knowledge work and a significant portion of physical labor. Governments worldwide, under pressure from social unrest, have introduced some form of basic income. The money is paid out in CBDCs.

Now pause for a moment and think that through.

If your only income comes from the state, in a currency the state programs, through a system the state controls—what freedom do you have left? Theoretically, all of it. Practically, none.

The government can put an expiration date on your UBI: spend it within 30 days or it expires. Saving becomes impossible. Wealth-building becomes impossible.

The government can dictate what you may spend your UBI on: groceries yes, plane tickets no. Certain products yes, others no. Too much carbon consumption? Your money gets restricted.

The government can cut or freeze your UBI if you "misbehave": attended a protest? Wallet frozen. Social media post the government doesn't like? Purchasing power restricted. This isn't fantasy. China is already practicing parts of this with its Social Credit System.

The technical capabilities are built into the architecture of every CBDC. Not every government will use them. But every government will have them.
:::


---

### The Economic Forced Logic — Why CBDCs Aren't Optional

And here it gets really uncomfortable: it's not just that governments could use CBDCs as control instruments. They will have to. Because they simply don't have the money.

The raw math: a UBI of $1,000 per month for every US adult costs roughly $3.1 trillion per year. For all residents including children: $4 trillion. That's approximately equal to total US federal tax revenue for an entire year. For Europe, Japan, and most industrialized nations, the math isn't any better.

These countries can barely afford their existing social systems as it is. The US stands at $37.6 trillion in debt, with an annual deficit of $1.7 trillion and interest payments consuming the single largest line item in the budget. The global debt-to-GDP ratio stands at 94.7 percent—and rising. And now these governments are supposed to provide 30 to 50 percent of the population made unemployed by AI and robots with a comfortable basic income?

That's mathematically impossible. Unless you do one of three things: Print money—which leads to massive inflation and destroys the UBI's purchasing power, as seen in Venezuela, Turkey, and Argentina. Massively raise taxes—which is politically nearly impossible and triggers capital flight; anyone paying 70 to 80 percent taxes emigrates, physically or digitally, to jurisdictions with lower taxes, into Bitcoin, into stablecoins. Or—and this is the most likely scenario—cut the UBI to the absolute minimum and use CBDCs as a rationing tool.

If the money isn't enough for a "Universal High Income," all that's left is a Universal Survival Income. Enough to survive, not enough to live. And to keep people from complaining, from rebelling, from revolting, the government needs control over every cent. Expiration dates to keep the economy moving. Categorical restrictions so the scarce budget isn't spent on "luxuries." Real-time dynamic adjustment. And once cash is abolished and everything runs through CBDC, there's no black market, no escape.

The logical consequence of economic arithmetic: governments won't have enough money for a comfortable UBI. So they'll combine minimal UBI with maximum control. CBDCs are the perfect instrument for that. Not because governments are evil, but because they'll have no other choice.

During the pandemic, we saw how quickly governments worldwide can synchronously implement drastic measures when they deem it necessary. Within weeks, freedom of movement, freedom of assembly, and economic freedom were restricted. The mechanisms were improvised—lockdowns, curfews, vaccine mandates. With CBDCs, they're no longer improvised. They're built in.

Emad Mostaque's "Digital Feudalism^[194]^" becomes very concrete here: a population dependent on a government basic income, paid in programmable money that logs every transaction and can be restricted or confiscated at will. What emerges is a digital two-class society. The Provided For—60 to 70 percent of the population—live on programmable UBI, can't save, can't make independent financial decisions. They are de facto subjects. The Owners—10 to 30 percent—own assets: stocks, Bitcoin, real estate, company shares. They have their own income, can afford self-custody. They are free.

Digital serfdom. And the only difference from the Middle Ages: back then, a serf could flee, hide, start a new life under a false name. In a world of CBDC, digital identity, and pervasive surveillance, there's no forest left to hide in.

---


### Trump's Counter-Design — Brilliant but Not Selfless

Against this backdrop, Trump's stablecoin strategy needs to be reassessed. The Executive Order of January 23, 2025, categorically banning CBDCs for the US. The Anti-CBDC Surveillance State Act, passed by the House in July 2025. The GENIUS Act, which regulates and legitimizes private stablecoins. The US is the only major country in the world actively opposing CBDCs. That alone should give you pause.

But I'm not naive enough to believe this comes from pure love of liberty.

Trump's stablecoin strategy is a brilliant chess move that kills two birds with one stone. First: the US avoids the CBDC surveillance problem—at least formally. Second, and this is the real coup: private stablecoins export US debt to the entire world. Tether and Circle together hold more US government bonds than Saudi Arabia. JPMorgan estimates additional Treasury demand from stablecoins at $1.4 trillion by 2027. Every person on Earth holding a dollar stablecoin on their phone is financing American government debt. And the GENIUS Act ensures this happens in a regulated, transparent, and scalable way.

That means: the US can continue increasing its debt without interest rates exploding, because demand for US Treasuries is kept artificially high through stablecoins. They can maintain the American standard of living while the rest of the world foots the bill. Social unrest in the US becomes easier to avoid than in Europe or Asia, where governments don't have the luxury of exporting their debt through private digital dollars. It's the petrodollar system 2.0. Only more elegant.

But—and this is the critical point—private stablecoins aren't guaranteed freedom either. USDT and USDC are centralized. Tether can freeze wallets, and has done so in the past. Circle too. In an extreme case, quick legislation—an Executive Order during a crisis—could turn private stablecoins into de facto CBDCs: freeze wallets, restrict transactions, tighten KYC requirements. The control instrument is built in there too. It's just not being used today. Yet.

The difference between a government CBDC and a private stablecoin is real, but it's a matter of degree, not kind. A CBDC gives the government control by design. A stablecoin gives a private company control—and the government can reach in whenever it wants. In both cases, the citizen holds the short end of the stick.


---

### What This Means for the Machine Economy

And here the circle closes back to this book's thesis.

When AI agents become economic actors—when they autonomously buy, sell, and negotiate—they need a payment system that can't be programmed by a central bank. An agent whose wallet can be frozen by a government isn't an autonomous agent. It's an instrument.

Bitcoin is the only existing monetary system that solves this problem: no expiration dates, no programmability by third parties, no central authority that can freeze wallets. 21 million units^[61]^ controlled by no government. Bitcoin isn't perfect. But it's the hardest money ever created—and the only one that offers both humans and machines true financial sovereignty.

Self-custody—the personal storage of cryptocurrencies—becomes, in a world of CBDCs and programmable money, not a technical detail but a human right. If you don't hold your own money, you don't really own it. You have permission to use it. Permission that can be revoked at any time.

The Convergence Thesis has a dimension that goes beyond investment opportunities. The convergence of AI, robotics, and crypto doesn't just determine who gets rich. It determines who stays free.

The Debt Elephant in the Room

Now comes the part that holds everything together. And that calls everything into question.

::: {.infobox}
### Why the US Can't Pay Its Own Debts

The United States is roughly $39 trillion in debt. 122 percent of GDP.^[98]^ Debt is growing by $6.12 billion per day.^[99]^ Interest payments alone on this debt exceeded $1.2 trillion in fiscal year 2025.^[100]^ More than the US spends on Medicare or national defense. The Congressional Budget Office projects that interest payments will double to $2.1 trillion by 2036. A total of $16.2 trillion in interest over the next ten years.^[101]^

Read that number again. $16.2 trillion. Just interest. Not a single dollar of that builds a bridge, pays a teacher, or cures a disease.

The US isn't alone. Japan leads the global debt league at over 250 percent of GDP. Italy stands at 137 percent, France at over 110 percent, the UK at 100 percent.^[102]^ Global government debt is approaching $100 trillion. IMF Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva warned in^[103]^ Davos: "The debts that weigh on our shoulders and approach 100 percent of global GDP will be a very heavy burden."

The Committee for a Responsible Federal Budget published^[104]^ a report in January 2026: "What Would a Fiscal Crisis Look Like?" The answer: it doesn't have to be a sudden crash. It can be a slow suffocation. Higher interest rates, slower growth, less fiscal room to maneuver, rising taxes, spending cuts. Death by a thousand cuts.

And why is this relevant for stablecoins and tokenization? Because everything is connected.
:::

### The Stablecoin-Debt Paradox

Here's a paradox most commentators miss.

Stablecoins support dollar dominance by generating demand for US government bonds. That helps Washington finance its national debt. But those very debts are gradually undermining the trust in the dollar that stablecoins need as their anchor.

Think it through: Tether holds $130 billion in Treasury bills. Those bills are only worth as much as the US government's promise to repay them. That promise is based on America's ability to service its debt. And that ability gets more fragile every year.

Stablecoins buy government bonds. That lets the government take on more debt. More debt means higher interest rates. Higher rates mean more debt. At some point, the cycle breaks. The only question is when.

The Federal Reserve Bank of Dallas^[105]^ examined the consequences of rising government debt on interest rates in a working paper and warned: if confidence in US Treasuries declines, so does demand for stablecoins backed by those Treasuries. The settlement layer of the tokenization economy is only as stable as the creditworthiness of the world's largest debtor nation.

### Future Scenarios: Three Paths


**Scenario 1: Dollar-Stablecoin Hegemony Holds (40 Percent Probability)**

The US manages to at least stabilize its debt without triggering an acute crisis. The GENIUS Act becomes the model for global stablecoin regulation. Dollar stablecoins grow to $2 trillion, then $5 trillion, and become the de facto settlement layer for the entire tokenized economy. The BRICS alternatives remain fragmented and never achieve critical mass. The dollar goes digital and becomes stronger than ever—not despite, but because the traditional banking system fails in so many countries. The machine economy settles in digital dollars. For investors, that means: the infrastructure providers of the dollar stablecoin ecosystem are the winners. Circle, Fireblocks, the Layer-1 blockchains.


**Scenario 2: Fragmented Multi-Currency System (45 Percent Probability)**

The US debt crisis escalates gradually. Interest payments consume an ever-larger share of the budget. Confidence in Treasury-backed stablecoins erodes slowly. Meanwhile, alternatives gain ground: the digital euro becomes reality from 2029, mBridge connects Asian CBDCs, regional stablecoin ecosystems emerge in Latin America and Africa. There's no "dollar moment," no sudden collapse. But the 99-percent dollar dominance in stablecoins crumbles to maybe 60 to 70 percent. The tokenization economy becomes multi-denominated: Treasury bonds in USDC, Bunds in digital euros, Asian real estate in regional stablecoins. For investors: multi-chain, multi-currency—and anyone betting only on the dollar misses half the world.


**Scenario 3: Crypto-Native Alternatives (15 Percent Probability)**

The debt crisis in developed nations becomes so severe that confidence in all government-backed currencies suffers. Bitcoin actually becomes "digital gold" and a global unit of account, as its advocates have claimed for years. Next-generation algorithmic stablecoins—not based on government bonds but on diversified commodity and crypto baskets—gain market share. The machine economy develops its own measures of value in which neither dollar, yuan, nor euro plays a privileged role. For investors, this would be the most radical shift: Bitcoin and decentralized protocols as safe havens, and the question "Which government bond should I buy?" becomes irrelevant.


My honest assessment: we'll see a mix of Scenario 1 and 2. The dollar stablecoin retains its dominance for the next five to ten years, but the cracks become visible. The debt problem can't be ignored forever. And the world won't trust indefinitely that a country spending $6 billion more per day than it takes in will provide the global store of value.

### What This Means for the Convergence Thesis


Back to the topic. AI, robots, and crypto are converging. The machine economy needs a settlement layer. Today, that's dollar stablecoins. Tomorrow, maybe not exclusively.

The machine economy won't settle in a single currency. AI agents will use whichever stablecoin is most efficient for the given transaction. An agent buying a tokenized office tower in Frankfurt will pay in digital euros. The same agent buying compute on a decentralized marketplace will use USDC. And for microtransactions between machines, Bitcoin's Lightning Network—or an alternative not yet invented—might make the most sense.

Unlike humans, machines won't have patriotic attachment to a currency. They'll make the most rational choice. And that's what makes the debt question so explosive: when the most rational choice is no longer the dollar, the machines will notice first. Not the central bankers. Not the politicians. The algorithms.

That may be the deepest irony of the Convergence Thesis: the technologies strengthening the dollar today—stablecoins, tokenization, AI—could be the very ones that replace it someday. Not out of malice. Out of efficiency.

### The Big Question: Will the Little Guys Finally Get In?

Now we come to the question that brought me to this chapter—one I can't answer with a simple yes or no.

Will tokenization open access for small investors to the world's most lucrative investments? Will ordinary people finally be able to participate in private equity, venture capital, trophy real estate, and infrastructure projects that currently require minimum investments of millions?

Or will the rich and powerful find ways to keep the new opportunities for themselves?

The honest answer: both. Simultaneously. And I'm not sure which side will ultimately outweigh the other.

### What Gets Better

Tokenization dramatically lowers minimum investments. This isn't theory—it's already happening. Hamilton Lane brought private equity funds from $5 million to $20,000 minimums. RealT sells real estate shares starting at $50. In Japan, retail investors can invest in tokenized real estate from $500.

Tokenization creates markets where none existed before. A share of a Basquiat painting was simply untradeable before Masterworks. The rental income of an apartment building in Detroit was unreachable for an investor in Munich. Music royalties were a black-box business of record labels.

Dividends are distributed automatically through smart contracts instead of middlemen. Settlement takes seconds instead of days. Costs fall because intermediaries disappear. And an investor in Manila can buy shares of an office building in Munich without ever seeing a German notary. Around the clock.

### What Stays the Same

The most lucrative deals will continue to be distributed to the big players first. When a Tier-1 VC fund is tokenized, the best terms don't go to the small investor putting in €500 through an app. They go to the institutional investor bringing $50 million. Tokenization changes the denomination, not the power distribution.

Regulatory hurdles are real. In the US, Accredited Investor rules exclude 87 percent of the population from most tokenized securities. In Europe, a public offering of tokenized securities above €8 million^[164]^ requires a full prospectus—a process that easily costs €500,000 and takes months.

Liquidity isn't automatic. Just because an asset is tokenized doesn't mean anyone wants to buy your token. The Canton Network Report of 2026 documents price differences of 1 to 3 percent for identical tokenized assets on different blockchains^[106]^ and friction costs of 2 to 5 percent for cross-chain transfers. Fragmented markets, fragmented liquidity.


And then the new gatekeepers. BlackRock BUIDL requires KYC and accredited investors. The "permissioned DeFi" movement—Aave Arc, Compound Treasury—creates separate pools for institutions that retail investors can't access. The same TradFi giants who built the old barriers are now building the new ones. With blockchain technology instead of paper forms, but with the same effect.

### The Robinhood Lesson

Remember Robinhood? The trading app that "democratized" stock trading? Commission-free trading. Fractional shares. Millions of new retail investors flooded the market.

What happened? Retail investors bought GameStop at $400 and lost billions. Payment for order flow meant orders weren't executed at the best market but at the highest-paying market maker. Gamification turned investing into an addictive game with confetti animations.

Access without education and without protection can cause more harm than no access at all.

I don't want to sound cynical here. Tokenization will improve many things for many people. But I also want to be honest: the notion that a new technology alone will upend the power dynamics of the financial system has never been borne out by history. What has worked is legislation. And legislation is made by people who need to understand what's at stake.

My metaphor: tokenization is like the printing press. Before Gutenberg, knowledge was reserved for monasteries. After Gutenberg, everyone could read. That was an enormous advance. But those who owned the presses became more powerful than the monks. The publishers, the press barons, the media conglomerates—they controlled what was printed, who read it, and what it cost.

More people will be able to participate in more asset classes. That's real. But the architects of the new system—the BlackRocks and JPMorgans—will profit disproportionately. They own the new printing presses.

### When Machines Invest: Tokenization Meets the Machine Economy


Now it becomes relevant for this book's core thesis.

In the previous chapters, we saw that AI agents need crypto as a native payment channel and that smart contracts are the contracts machines understand. Tokenization is the logical extension: it makes not just money but all assets machine-readable.

An AI agent managing a portfolio—and such agents already exist on platforms like Autonolas and Virtuals Protocol—can buy a tokenized Treasury bond, collect yield, post the token as collateral for a loan, and reinvest the proceeds in a tokenized real estate fund. Automatically. Without human intermediaries. In seconds.

In the traditional financial system, that would be impossible. The agent would need a bank account, an identity, a broker, a custodian, a notary for the real estate—and each of these steps requires a human clicking "Approved."

In the tokenized world, the agent needs only a private key and a smart contract.

Think further. An autonomous taxi network that tokenizes its revenue and distributes yield tokens to investors. A robot whose insurance policy is a smart contract, with premiums paid automatically from its earnings. A network of AI agents that purchases its own compute on a decentralized marketplace and pays with tokenized microtransactions.

That is the machine economy this book describes. And tokenization is the missing piece. Crypto gives machines money. Tokenization gives machines access to all the world's assets.

An interesting side effect: AI plus smart contracts plus tokenization could make regulation not harder but easier. The AI checks whether an investor meets the requirements. The smart contract enforces the rules. The blockchain documents everything immutably. The regulator can verify in real time whether everything is running correctly. No quarterly reports, no after-the-fact audits. Transparency as the default setting instead of the exception.

Whether lawmakers will recognize this is another matter entirely.

---


### What This Means for Investors

If you take this thesis seriously, tokenization has three implications for your investment strategy.

Infrastructure providers are the picks-and-shovels play. Not individual tokenized assets will be the biggest winners, but the platforms on which they're traded. The blockchains on which it all runs: Ethereum, Solana, Avalanche. Securitize, infrastructure providers like Fireblocks, compliance infrastructure. As in the Gold Rush, the shovel seller earns most reliably.

Access to new asset classes becomes real. If you want to invest in private equity or tokenized real estate today, platforms already exist that make it possible—at minimum investments that were unthinkable five years ago. What matters is which platforms are regulated, secure, and liquid enough.

Watch for regulatory arbitrage. Japan and Singapore are open for retail investors. The US remains closed for most. Europe is slow but thorough. Those who understand the regulatory landscape can seize opportunities others can't access.

The tokenization of all the world's assets isn't a question of if. It's a question of when, how, and above all: for whom. The next ten years will decide. And I have an uneasy feeling the answer will be more complicated than optimists hope and pessimists fear.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ Tokenization democratizes access to previously illiquid assets.
>
> ◆ BlackRock, JPMorgan, and Goldman Sachs are already active—this is no longer crypto hype.
>
> ◆ Self-custody becomes a human right in a CBDC world.


## Chapter 7: When Machines Start Companies


---

> *"The net never sleeps."*
>
> — Kevin Kelly

---


![Anatomy of a machine company (DAO)](images-en/abb-11.png)


---

### Smart Contracts, DAOs, and the End of the CEO
I want you to imagine a car. An ordinary electric car, parked on a street in Munich. It has no owner. At least not in the traditional sense.

The car owns itself.

It has a digital identity on a blockchain, a wallet with stablecoins, a smart contract defining its business rules, and an AI deciding where it drives.

At 6:30 AM, the car receives an API request: someone in Schwabing needs a ride to the airport. The AI calculates route, travel time, energy consumption. The smart contract prices the trip, compares it to the current market, accepts the booking. The passenger gets in, gets driven to the airport, gets out. 34.50 USDC is automatically transferred from their wallet to the car's wallet.

From the $34.50, the smart contract immediately deducts: $2.80 for electricity drawn from a charging station last night. $0.40 for the day's insurance premium. $0.15 for the proportional reserve funding the next tire change. $0.05 in network fees. The remainder—$31.10—is distributed proportionally to the token holders who financed this car two years ago.

No Uber, no accountant—everything runs on code.

Sounds like science fiction? It still is. Partially. But every single building block needed for it already exists. And that's exactly what this chapter is about.

---


### Smart Contracts: When Code Keeps Promises

In the previous chapter on crypto, I explained what smart contracts are: programs on a blockchain that execute automatically when certain conditions are met. Pay X when Y happens. Transfer ownership when payment is received. Escalate to an arbitrator when a deadline expires.

In this chapter, I go a step further. Because smart contracts as they exist today are dumb. I don't mean that pejoratively—I mean it technically. A smart contract on Ethereum executes exactly the logic its programmer wrote into it. Nothing more, nothing less. It can't improvise, can't categorize new situations, can't learn.

That's changing right now.

The combination of AI and smart contracts creates something that didn't exist before: adaptive contracts. A smart contract with an AI agent as its decision layer can respond to situations its programmer never foresaw. The AI analyzes market data, weather conditions, user behavior, machine status. The smart contract executes the decision.

An example: a tokenized solar farm in Andalusia. The smart contract governs revenue distribution to token holders. But the AI behind it does more. It reads weather data and predicts tomorrow will be cloudy. So tonight, it sold the electricity at peak spot market prices instead of storing it. It notices an inverter showing unusual voltage fluctuations—probably a wear issue—and automatically commissions a technician through a DePIN marketplace nearby. It spots that the electricity price in Portugal is currently higher than in Spain and reroutes capacity through the cross-border grid.

The smart contract keeps the promise: revenues are distributed, costs deducted, rules followed. The AI makes the promise intelligent.

This has a consequence too few people are thinking about: if the smart contract is the law and the AI is the decision-maker, does the solar farm still need a CEO? A board? A supervisory board?

The answer is: theoretically, no. Practically—and this is the exciting question in this chapter—the world isn't ready for that yet. DAOs: The Company Without a Boss. DAO stands for Decentralized Autonomous Organization. The term sounds like a computer science seminar, but the idea behind it is radical: an organization governed by code instead of people. No hierarchy, no office, no CEO. Just rules written into a smart contract, and members who decide by token vote.

Vitalik Buterin, co-founder of Ethereum, developed the concept in 2013.^[107]^ He was 19 at the time. His idea: if we can make money programmable, why not organizations?

The first major DAO launched on Ethereum in 2016.^[108]^ Called simply "The DAO," it raised $150 million—the largest crowdfunding in history at the time. Investors bought tokens and voted on which projects to fund. No VC, no board, no pitch deck. Direct democracy on the blockchain.

It ended in catastrophe. A hacker found a bug in the smart contract and stole a third of the funds. The Ethereum community decided on a hard fork—a kind of time-travel on the blockchain—to reverse the hack. The crypto world split into Ethereum and Ethereum Classic. It was a disaster. But it was also proof that the idea can work—when the code is clean.

Since then, much has changed. DAOs today manage billions of dollars. Uniswap, the decentralized exchange where BlackRock's BUIDL fund is traded, is a DAO. MakerDAO governs the DAI stablecoin. Lido manages over $30 billion in staking. Aave is a DAO that enables lending without banks.

But here's the point relevant to the Convergence Thesis: all these DAOs are still governed by humans. Humans write the proposals, humans vote, humans execute. The decentralization refers to the distribution of power, not to automation.

That's changing now.

Three days before I'm writing these lines, on February 21, 2026, Vitalik Buterin published a new proposal^[109]^ that addresses exactly this. He argues that DAOs suffer from a fundamental problem: nobody has time to read all the proposals and vote in an informed way. Participation rates are abysmal. Those who do vote are mostly the whales—holders with tokens worth millions. Direct democracy devolves into oligarchy.

Buterin's solution: personal AI agents as voting proxies. Every DAO member has an AI agent that knows their values and preferences, reads the proposals, analyzes them, and votes. Not autonomous in the sense of uncontrolled, but as an extension of the member who simply doesn't have time to read through every proposal.

He calls the concept "AI Stewards." And he says something I find remarkable, coming from someone who built Ethereum: "AI becomes the government is^[110]^ dystopian. But AI used well can be empowering."

AI as government is the nightmare. AI as a tool for better self-governance is the opportunity. The line between them is thin. But it exists. The Machine Firm: Beyond a Thought Experiment. Now connect the pieces.

AI agents that can act autonomously (Chapter 2) + cryptocurrency as their payment system (Chapter 3) + tokenized assets they can buy and sell (Chapter 4) + smart contracts as their business rules + DAOs as their corporate structure.

What emerges when you put this together? Companies that don't need humans to function.

I know how that sounds. And I'd be the first to say: be skeptical. Keep your eyes open. But look at what's already being built in 2025 and 2026.

peaq is a blockchain specifically developed for the machine economy—not for traders or NFT collectors, but for machines, robots, and vehicles. On peaq, machines can create their own digital identities, sign transactions, and offer services. There's already a growing ecosystem of DePIN projects—from energy networks to mobility platforms to robotics.

XMAQUINA launched the first DAO on peaq focused on owning and governing autonomous machines. MachineDAO, as it's called, holds physical machine assets, stakes in robotics firms, and DePIN investments. Members vote on acquisitions and operational decisions. The members are still humans. But the architecture is built so that AI agents could take over governance tasks.

Olas (formerly Autonolas) launched Pearl v1 in November 2025^[111]^—what CoinDesk called "the first decentralized AI agent app store." Users can own, operate, and monetize AI agents. The agents execute DeFi strategies, manage DAOs, and coordinate services across multiple blockchains. And they do it autonomously, around the clock, without anyone clicking "Approved."

Fetch.ai, merged with SingularityNET and Ocean Protocol into the ASI Alliance^[112]^, is building a network of autonomous agents that offer and purchase services from each other. An agent that delivers traffic data sells it to an agent that optimizes transport routes. Payment in crypto, automatically, via smart contracts. It's a small market today. But the architecture scales.

The DePIN sector overall^[113]^—Decentralized Physical Infrastructure Networks—now encompasses around 300 projects with over 21 million connected devices. Helium for IoT connectivity, Filecoin for decentralized storage, Render Network for GPU compute, Hivemapper for decentralized mapping. These are the physical infrastructures on which machines will operate.

::: {.infobox}
### Wyoming and the First Machine Companies

Wyoming became the first US state in 2021 to pass laws allowing DAOs to register as LLCs.^[114]^ A DAO can be a legal entity, with a smart contract as its operating agreement. Tennessee and Vermont followed. The costs: $15,000 to $50,000. Duration: four to eight weeks.

That's the first bridge between code and law. Still wobbly, still narrow. But it exists.
:::

### The Self-Driving Car: A Case Study

Let's come back to our car from the opening. Because it's tangible enough that I want to walk through it as a case study. Not as a future vision, but as an engineering problem whose individual parts have already been solved.

The hardware: Waymo, Cruise, Tesla, and Baidu operate autonomous vehicle fleets in multiple cities today. Level 4—fully autonomous driving in defined areas—is technically solved in San Francisco, Phoenix, Beijing, and Wuhan. Not future music.

The identity: On peaq or similar blockchains, a vehicle can receive a Machine DID (Decentralized Identifier). Not a human identity, but a machine one. The vehicle can authenticate itself, sign transactions, offer services.

The financing: The car is issued as a tokenized asset on a blockchain. 10,000 tokens at €5 each. 500 investors finance a vehicle for €50,000. Like RealT for real estate, only for a car.

The business logic: A smart contract defines the rules. Revenue distribution, cost deductions, reserve building, maintenance planning, insurance premiums. All automatic, all transparent, all verifiable on the blockchain.

The AI: The agent drives the car, accepts bookings, optimizes routes, decides on charging times based on electricity prices. It learns from driving data which routes are profitable and which aren't.

The legal structure: A DAO LLC in Wyoming. The smart contract is the operating agreement. The token holders are the members. Voting by token on fundamental decisions: should the car operate in a different neighborhood? Should the fleet expand to include a second vehicle?

Every single building block exists. What's still missing is the integration. And the regulatory green light. No regulator in the world has yet approved a vehicle with no human owner. But the building blocks are there, and the question is no longer whether someone will put them together, but when.

My estimate: a working prototype of a self-managing vehicle fleet as a DAO sometime between 2028 and 2031. Scaling from 2031.

### The Questions Nobody Has Answered Yet

If you've read this far, you're probably thinking about problems. Good—because so am I. Here are the biggest ones.

Liability.

When an autonomous car that owns itself hits a pedestrian—who's liable? The DAO? The token holders? The developer of the smart contract? The manufacturer of the AI? Today's liability law isn't built for this question. It knows natural persons—humans—and legal persons—companies. A third category—a machine person—doesn't exist.

The Wyoming DAO LLC comes closest. But even there, members—meaning token holders—are liable under certain circumstances. What happens when a thousand token holders are scattered across the globe and nobody is physically within reach? The law doesn't have an answer yet.

Taxes.

Does an autonomous machine company earn income? Probably yes. In which country is it taxed? Where the machine is physically located? Where the smart contract is deployed? Where the token holders reside? In a world where machines operate across borders and their "company" is code on a blockchain that doesn't physically exist in any country, tax authorities will have to get creative.

Control.

DAOs are hard to stop. A smart contract on Ethereum runs as long as Ethereum runs. Nobody can shut it down. That's a feature and a risk. When the AI of an autonomous vehicle starts making decisions nobody likes—when it chooses a route that disturbs residents, when it sets prices so high only the wealthy can afford rides—who pulls the plug? At a normal company, you call the CEO. At a DAO, you vote. But what if the AI agents are steering the vote?

Inequality.

And here's the theme that runs through this entire book. When machines start companies and make money, token holders profit. Who can afford tokens? Tokenization (Chapter 4) lowers the barriers to entry, yes. But the initial allocation—the best deals, the earliest tokens—will flow to those with capital and connections here too. The mechanic who gets paid an hourly wage to repair the car. The token holder who financed the car earns passive income. In their sleep.

I don't have solutions to these problems. I don't think anyone does who's being honest. What I do have is the conviction that these questions need to be asked now. Not in ten years, when the first machine companies have created facts on the ground.

### Timeline: When Does This Become Real?

I'm cautious with predictions. Anyone in the tech space quoting exact years is either lying or selling something. But you can identify approximate phases.

We're now, in early 2026, in the foundation phase. AI agents are operating on blockchains. DePIN networks are growing. DAO legislation exists in three US states. Machine identities are being standardized. Olas has an app store for AI agents. Academic papers are arriving weekly. The foundation is being poured.

Over the next two to three years—through about 2028—we'll see the first genuinely autonomous services. Tokenized energy installations that operate under AI management and automatically distribute revenues. AI agents that function as independent economic units, accepting contracts and paying in crypto. DeFi strategies that run as DAOs without a single human making a decision.

Between 2028 and 2031, as Level 4 and Level 5 autonomous driving becomes broadly available and robotics arrives in the physical world, the first physical machines will emerge as independent economic actors. Self-managing vehicle fleets, delivery robot networks, autonomous warehouses.

From 2031, the machine economy will be large enough to measure. Not as a fringe phenomenon, but as its own sector with its own GDP. That's when regulators won't be able to look away anymore either.

That's my assessment. Maybe I'm too optimistic. Maybe it goes faster. Technological developments have a habit of arriving slower than enthusiasts hope and faster than skeptics believe.

---

### What Does This Mean for Investors? Three Thoughts.

Invest in the infrastructure, not in the machines themselves.

Blockchains like Ethereum and Solana, on which the smart contracts run. Platforms like peaq that enable machine identities. Agent networks like Olas and Fetch.ai. Custody and identity providers. These are the picks and shovels of the machine economy. Whether a particular autonomous car turns a profit, nobody knows. That millions of machines will need infrastructure is certain.

Watch the regulation. Wyoming took the first step. When other states and countries follow—recognizing DAOs as full legal entities with clear liability and tax rules—that's the signal that the machine economy is transitioning from experiment to mainstream. Regulation always lags technology. But when it arrives, the big money follows.

And think about second-order effects. When autonomous machines start companies, earn money, and pay taxes—what happens to the labor market? To the tax system? To the question of who owns the productivity? These aren't academic exercises. They're investment questions. Because a society that answers them well will be more stable and prosperous than one that ignores them. And you'd rather invest in stable societies.

We've now built the entire thesis. Machines that think. Machines that have money. Machines that can trade all the world's assets. And now: machines that run their own companies.

In the next part of the book, we leave the thesis behind and look at what this means for the real world. Geopolitically, energy-wise, socially. Because an economy in which machines are economic actors changes everything. And "everything" is not an exaggeration.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ DAOs enable companies without human management.
>
> ◆ Smart contracts + AI agents = autonomous economic units.
>
> ◆ Token holders profit—but the access question intensifies inequality.


# PART II: THE LANDSCAPE

## Chapter 8: The Geopolitical Dimension — America, China, and Europe's Silence


---

> *"Whoever controls the chips controls the future."*
>
> — Freely after Jensen Huang

---


![The AI world map—USA dominates, China rivals, Europe regulates](images-en/abb-12.png)


Investments don't happen in a vacuum. The AI revolution has a geopolitical dimension you need to understand to make smart decisions. And the geopolitical reality is uncomfortable—especially for Europeans.

There are exactly two AI superpowers: the United States and China. Period.

Anyone who doesn't accept this will make bad investment decisions. So let me draw the map as it is, not as we'd like it to be.


---

### America First in AI: The US Ecosystem
The United States dominates the AI revolution in a way that is virtually unprecedented in technology history. Not in a single area, but in every relevant one simultaneously.

Fundamental research: OpenAI, Anthropic, Google DeepMind, Meta AI—the four most important AI labs in the world are all in the US. Their models define the state of the art. Their publications set the agenda of global AI research. Their researchers, often recruited from the world's best universities, represent the largest concentration of AI talent on the planet.

Hardware: NVIDIA controls over 80 percent of the AI training chip market. NVIDIA's revenue exploded from $27 billion in FY2023 to over $130 billion in FY2025^[115]^—a fivefold increase in two years, driven by the insatiable demand for AI compute. NVIDIA's H100 and Blackwell chips are the foundation on which the entire AI revolution runs. Without NVIDIA, no modern language models, no image AI, no agents, no robot intelligence.
AMD and Intel are trying to catch up but remain far behind NVIDIA. And then there are the custom chip initiatives from the hyperscalers: Google with TPU, Amazon with Trainium and Inferentia, Microsoft with Maia. They're all trying to reduce their dependence on NVIDIA—which paradoxically drives total investment in AI hardware even higher.

Infrastructure: AWS, Azure, and Google Cloud together control over 65 percent of the global cloud computing market.^[116]^ The cloud is the factory of the AI era—this is where models are trained, where inference workloads run, where the services used by companies worldwide are delivered. The cloud is the gateway to AI.

Capital: In 2024 alone, over $100 billion flowed into American AI startups.^[117]^ That's more than the total venture capital investments of most European countries—across all industries. OpenAI was valued at over $150 billion. Anthropic at over $60 billion. xAI, Elon Musk's AI company, raised $6 billion in a single round. Capital is flowing at a scale historically comparable only to the Manhattan Project or the Apollo program—except this time it's private capital, not government money.

The concentration is breathtaking. And it reinforces itself: the best researchers go where the most money is. The most money flows where the best researchers are. The best models come from the best researchers with the most money. And the best models attract the most users, which generates the most data, which makes the next generation even better.

This is not a cycle you break out of easily.

---


### China: The Underestimated Rival

China is the second AI superpower, and anyone who underestimates it is making an expensive mistake.

The Chinese AI strategy differs fundamentally from the American one. The US dominates in foundation models—the massive language models that power everything. China dominates in applications and the hardware supply chain.

ByteDance, the parent company of TikTok, runs one of the most advanced AI systems in the world—not for language processing, but for recommendation engines. The algorithm that decides which video you see next on TikTok is an AI system of breathtaking complexity and effectiveness. Baidu, Alibaba, and Tencent are investing billions in their own foundation models.

And then came DeepSeek—the moment China showed it can compete not just in applications but in the fundamentals. Building a model with more efficient architecture and less compute that can hold its own against the best American models wasn't just a technical breakthrough. It was a strategic signal: export controls won't stop China. They'll force China to find its own path. And that path might end up being more efficient than the American one.

In robotics, China is already the largest market and increasingly the leading producer. Unitree delivers humanoid robots under $20,000—a fraction of what Figure AI or Tesla will charge for Optimus. China has something Western robotics startups lack: decades of experience in mass-producing electronics, established supply chains for motors, sensors, and batteries, and a government that considers robotics a strategic priority and subsidizes accordingly.

BYD, now the world's largest automaker by unit sales, is integrating AI and robotics into its production lines at a speed that puts Western industrial giants to shame. While German automakers are still debating "digitalization strategies," BYD already has factories where AI-controlled robots autonomously handle large parts of production.

Key insight for investors: The AI competition between the US and China is not a zero-sum game. Both markets will grow. But the paths diverge: the US dominates software and models, China dominates hardware and mass production. Anyone building a diversified AI portfolio needs to keep both sides in view.


---

### Europe: A Continent Regulating Itself Into Irrelevance
I say this as a European entrepreneur who feels the consequences daily.

Europe hasn't produced a single leading foundation model. No European company is in the top 20 most valuable AI companies. Europe's biggest contribution to the AI revolution so far is the bureaucracy that slows it down.

The EU AI Act is the most ambitious AI regulation in the world—and possibly the most damaging. The detailed analysis of how it's reshaping the industry, who benefits, and who loses follows in Chapter 11. The short version: Europe regulates not with industry but against it. Compliance costs disproportionately burden small companies and cement the dominance of US tech giants, which can absorb them effortlessly.

The brain drain compounds the problem. Europe's best AI researchers work in San Francisco, New York, or London—not in Berlin, Paris, or Munich. Not because they dislike Europe, but because the labs are there, the capital is there, the opportunities are there. Yann LeCun, one of the fathers of deep learning, is French—he works for Meta in New York. Demis Hassabis, founder of DeepMind, is British—he works for Google in London (technically still Europe, but post-Brexit and funded by Silicon Valley capital). The list goes on.


For investors: An AI-focused portfolio should be predominantly invested in US and selectively in Asian assets. European stocks can be part of a diversified portfolio—European industrial companies like Siemens or SAP will use AI and benefit from it—but Europe is not where the returns of the AI revolution are being generated. The innovation is happening elsewhere.

### Europe's Regulation as an Own Goal: Three Personal Experiences

I'm not a theoretical critic of regulation. I live it.

First example: My company accessibleAI develops AI-powered software for accessibility compliance. We wanted to use Meta's open-source model Llama—self-hosted, on European servers, with full control over the data. In July 2024, Meta announced it wouldn't release its multimodal AI models in the EU.^[167]^ The reason: "the unpredictable nature of the European regulatory environment." In September 2024, the restriction was written into the license. EU citizens and EU companies are prohibited from using Llama 3.2's vision models. When Llama 4 launched in April 2025—fully multimodal—all versions were blocked for the EU.

We couldn't find a European hosting provider that could legally run the model. The most capable open-source AI in the world, developed by a US company that offers it for free, is unavailable to European businesses. Not because it's dangerous or doesn't work, but simply because Meta doesn't want to take the regulatory risk.

Apple did something similar. Apple Intelligence was delayed in the EU, citing the Digital Markets Act. Google rolled out AI features later for the EU. A pattern is emerging: US companies treat Europe as a second-class market because the compliance costs don't justify the effort.

Second example: I held stablecoins. USDC, Circle's dollar stablecoin. Until November 2024, Coinbase paid me 4.7 percent annual yield on it. Passive income on digital dollars, fully automatic. On December 1, 2024,^[168]^ that was over. Coinbase discontinued the USDC Rewards program for all customers in the European Economic Area. A Coinbase spokesperson explained that MiCA required the discontinuation. Article 50 of the Markets in Crypto-Assets Regulation prohibits paying interest on e-money tokens.

My American friends still get their 4.7 percent. I get zero. Not because I was taking more risk or because Coinbase operates less securely in Europe, but because European regulators decided that stablecoins should be payment instruments, not savings vehicles. The intention may be good. The result is that European citizens are systematically disadvantaged compared to Americans.

The same is happening with Tether, the world's largest stablecoin. Tether hasn't applied for a MiCA license. Several European exchanges have delisted USDT. As of July 2025, USDT is effectively banned on regulated European exchanges. A stablecoin with a market capitalization of over $139 billion, which accounted for 58 percent of European crypto transaction volume, is no longer tradable in Europe.

Third example, and the most frustrating one: I had the idea of giving open-source contributors on a project tokens. No speculation, no pump-and-dump. Simply a way to reward people who contribute code, write documentation, report bugs. Tokens as recognition and as a governance tool so the community can help decide the project's direction.

Under MiCA, anyone who issues tokens must create a crypto-asset whitepaper meeting Article 6 requirements. The issuer is liable for accuracy. The whitepaper must be submitted to the national supervisory authority 20 days before publication. For asset-referenced tokens, you need a regulatory license with minimum equity requirements of €350,000.

For an open-source project with volunteer developers, that's not feasible. The compliance costs exceed the value of the entire project. The danger of stepping into MiCA's minefield is too great. So you drop the idea. And with it dies a concept that would be perfectly implementable in the US or Switzerland.

The Europe Paradox is real: regulation meant to protect citizens disadvantages them. Europeans are "protected" from multimodal AI models—but can't use the most powerful open models. Europeans are "protected" from unregulated financial products—but lose 4 to 5 percent annual yield. Europeans are "protected" from dubious token issuances—but innovative projects leave. Nobody doubts the good intentions behind this regulation. But is Europe regulating itself out of the future?

---


### The Chip War

Semiconductors are the new oil. Whoever controls the chips controls AI. And AI determines the economy of the 21st century.


::: {.infobox}
### Taiwan — The Underestimated Risk

The entire AI revolution depends on a surprisingly fragile supply chain. NVIDIA designs the chips but doesn't build them. Production lies almost entirely with TSMC—the Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Company—in factories on an island 80 miles off the Chinese coast that the People's Republic considers part of its territory.

TSMC produces over 90 percent of the world's most advanced semiconductors^[122]^—the sub-7-nanometer chips needed for AI training and inference. There is no second company on the planet that can deliver chips of comparable quality and volume at scale. Samsung comes closest but is well behind. Intel is trying to catch up but has lost touch with the leading edge.


![The Chip War—TSMC's bottleneck in the global AI supply chain](images-en/abb-13.png)


And then there's ASML: the Dutch company that builds the lithography machines without which TSMC can't produce its chips. ASML's EUV machines^[123]^ (Extreme Ultraviolet Lithography) cost over $300 million each and are so complex that only a handful of engineers in the world can maintain them. ASML has a de facto monopoly on this technology. No ASML, no TSMC. No TSMC, no NVIDIA. No NVIDIA, no AI revolution.

The geopolitical implications are obvious and alarming. If China militarily threatens or even occupies Taiwan, the global chip supply shuts down. The US has responded by getting TSMC to build factories in Arizona and pledging billions in subsidies to Intel through the CHIPS Act. But building a chip fab takes years, and TSMC's engineering know-how can't be copied like software.

For investors, the Chip War is a double-edged sword. On one hand, NVIDIA, TSMC, and ASML offer extraordinary investment opportunities—they sit at the tightest chokepoint of the entire value chain. On the other hand, that very concentration is a systemic risk. An earthquake in Taiwan, a war in the Taiwan Strait, or even just tightened export controls could shake the entire industry.


:::

---

### The Chip War Gets Harder
The battle for supremacy in semiconductor manufacturing has intensified since the CHIPS Act. And the results are more sobering than expected.

TSMC is building the most expensive chip fab in history in Arizona. Total investment has ballooned from an initial $12 billion to $165 billion.^[124]^ Six factories, financed with $6.6 billion from the CHIPS Act. The first factory has been producing 4-nanometer chips since early 2025. But costs are 30 to 50 percent higher than in Taiwan.
