American technicians have less experience with the complex manufacturing processes. TSMC's notorious 12-hour shifts ran into cultural resistance. The factory works, but it's expensive.

Intel, once the undisputed chip king, has become the patient. CEO Pat Gelsinger was pushed^[125]^ to resign in December 2024—one week after Intel received the largest single CHIPS Act grant: $7.86 billion. His successor Lip-Bu Tan cut another 20 percent of the workforce, including 10,000 foundry workers, without severance. Intel's foundry division generated roughly $4.5 billion in revenue in 2025 against $2.5 billion in losses. The future of the entire manufacturing strategy is in question.

Samsung is stumbling too. Despite being the first manufacturer to mass-produce 3-nanometer chips, Samsung's yield rate sits at about 50^[126]^ percent. TSMC's is over 90. Every other chip on a Samsung wafer is defective. Qualcomm, Nvidia, and Google have all switched to TSMC. Samsung's foundry market share has fallen to 9.3 percent; TSMC stands at 60. A surprising exception: Elon Musk chose Samsung with a $16.5 billion contract^[127]^ for AI chips—presumably because he couldn't get the control over the manufacturing process he wanted at TSMC.

China is trying to close the chip gap and is running into physics. Huawei and SMIC produce chips at the 7-nanometer node, but without access to ASML's EUV lithography machines, they can't go further. Instead, they use DUV multi-patterning—a method that's slower, yields worse results, and hits physical limits at around 5 nanometers. Huawei's Kirin 9030 chip in its latest Mate smartphone is an improved 7-nanometer variant, not a true 5-nanometer as claimed. China is 3 to 5 years behind TSMC, and the gap is closing more slowly than Beijing hopes.

For the thesis of this book, the Chip War matters for one reason: control over AI starts with the chips. And that control lies with TSMC in Taiwan—an island that China considers its own territory. Sixty percent of the world's advanced chip contract manufacturing runs through a single company on an island at the center of the most dangerous geopolitical conflict in the world. That's not a risk you can ignore.

### 

### India: The Quiet Third Player


In AI geopolitics, the conversation usually centers on two poles: the US and China. India rarely comes up. That's a mistake.

India has 100 million weekly active ChatGPT users.^[118]^ More than any country except the US. Half the messages come from 18- to 24-year-olds. India has over 4 million software engineers^[119]^—the second-largest pool after the US—and produces 500,000 new IT graduates per year. The median age is 28. In the EU, it's 44.^[120]^

In March 2024, the Indian government launched the IndiaAI Mission with a budget of $1.24 billion^[121]^ over five years. That sounds like a lot but is a rounding error compared to the hundreds of billions US tech companies pour into AI infrastructure annually. More interesting than the budget is the approach: India is building multilingual AI models for 22 official languages. Krutrim, India's first AI unicorn, is working on models that understand Hindi, Tamil, Bengali, and 19 more languages. No Western model can do that.

India's role in the AI value chain is already enormous, if invisible. Hundreds of Indian companies annotate the data that large language models are trained on. Indian workers label images, transcribe audio, categorize text. Data annotation—the human handwork behind machine intelligence—is to a significant degree Indian.

Whether India becomes the third AI pole depends on what kind of pole you mean. A standalone AI research ecosystem on par with OpenAI or DeepSeek? Probably not. The compute resources and fundamental research aren't there. But an AI application market of 1.45 billion people, a talent exporter staffing the world's research labs, and an AI services sector building the industry's infrastructure? That's India already.

For investors, India is a theme worth watching. Not because of any single company, but because of the demographics. Five hundred thousand new engineers per year, in a country with a median age of 28, where 100 million people use AI weekly. If the US builds the AI models and China controls them, then India will apply them—with a breadth and depth no other country can match.

---


### Crypto as a Geopolitical Hedge

In this geopolitical landscape, crypto has a property often overlooked: jurisdictional independence.

Bitcoin belongs to no country. Ethereum is controlled by no government. Stablecoins can be used by any person and any machine in the world, regardless of which government is imposing which sanctions, which trade blocs are facing off, or which currency is currently devaluing.

> *That's not a bug. That's a feature.*

If the US and China are waging a technological cold war and Europe is busy regulating, then a financial system that belongs to none of these blocs is more than an ideological position for investors—it's a pragmatic hedge. Whoever owns Bitcoin and Ethereum owns assets that no single government can confiscate, devalue, or sanction.

In a world splitting into technological blocs—a US ecosystem, a Chinese ecosystem, possibly further regional systems—a neutral, cross-border store of value doesn't become less important. It becomes more important.

That doesn't mean you should put your entire portfolio into crypto. It means that crypto serves a function in a geopolitically diversified portfolio that no other asset can fulfill: hedging against the risk that the rules of the game change. Changed by players whose interests don't align with yours.

But even the best hedge asset is useless without energy. AI systems consume electricity at a scale that challenges our entire infrastructure. If you want to understand the machine economy, you need to understand where its fuel comes from.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ The US dominates the AI ecosystem: hardware, cloud, venture capital.
>
> ◆ China is catching up; Europe is regulating itself into irrelevance.
>
> ◆ TSMC in Taiwan is the geopolitical chokepoint of the entire AI revolution.

## Chapter 9: Energy — The Invisible Bottleneck


---

> *"Civilization is essentially a race for energy."*
>
> — Frederick Soddy

---


Much is written about AI. About the chips that power it. About the models that make it intelligent. About the robots that give it a body.

Startlingly little is written about the electricity all of this devours. And that's a mistake, because energy is the invisible bottleneck of the AI revolution—the critical chokepoint on which the entire vision will succeed or fail.

---

### Why AI Has an Insatiable Hunger for Energy

Artificial intelligence needs electricity—not a little, not moderately, but in quantities that alarm even energy experts.

Training a large language model—the process in which the model learns from billions of text documents—consumes as much electricity as a small town over several months. Training GPT-4 consumed an estimated 50 gigawatt-hours.^[128]^ For context: the average American household uses about 10,500 kilowatt-hours per year. Training a single AI model consumes as much electricity as roughly 4,800 households use in an entire year.

And training is just the beginning. Inference—the ongoing operation of the model when millions of users simultaneously send requests—consumes more energy over time than training. Every question to ChatGPT, every code generation by Copilot, every image creation by DALL-E consumes compute. And therefore electricity. Goldman Sachs estimates a single ChatGPT query uses roughly ten times as much electricity as a Google search.^[129]^

Data centers, which handle the bulk of this computation, already consume an estimated^[130]^ 4.4 percent of total US electricity. The International Energy Agency projects that data center energy consumption will more than double by 2030.^[131]^ This projection is based on current AI workloads—primarily text generation and image processing for enterprise applications.


![AI's energy hunger — data centers consume more and more power](images-en/abb-15.png)


Now scale that: What happens when AI agents work autonomously around the clock? When millions of robots are controlled in real time? When (as we'll see in the BCI chapter) AI generates personalized, physics-simulated 3D worlds for millions of users simultaneously? The energy requirements don't grow linearly. They grow exponentially.

OpenAI has called on the US government to build 100 gigawatts of additional power generation capacity per year[^132]—the equivalent of 100 nuclear power plants annually. That sounds like an absurd demand. It's the logical consequence of the numbers.

### Why Solar and Wind Aren't Enough


Here's where I regularly lose people. The moment I say nuclear energy is key to powering the AI revolution, I hear: "But solar and wind have gotten so much cheaper!" And that's true. Solar construction costs have fallen over 80 percent in the last decade. Onshore wind is competitive with natural gas. Based on the so-called Levelized Cost of Energy (LCOE—the total cost per megawatt-hour generated over an installation's lifetime), solar costs $38 to $78 per megawatt-hour, onshore wind $27 to $73, natural gas $48 to $107, while a new nuclear plant costs $141 to $220.

### Case closed? Renewables win?

No. Because these numbers are misleading. Not intentionally, but they omit something crucial: renewable energy produces electricity when the sun shines and the wind blows. Not when you need it. And an AI data center needs electricity 24/7, 365 days a year, with 99.99 percent reliability. No pause, no weekend, no "sorry, it was cloudy today."

The capacity factor makes the difference. A nuclear power plant has a capacity factor of 92.5^[133]^ percent—delivering over 22 hours per day, over 340 days per year. Solar sits at 23 to 25 percent, onshore wind at 33 to 35 percent. A 1-gigawatt nuclear plant generates about 8,100 gigawatt-hours per year. To generate the same amount of energy with solar, you'd need three to four times the installed capacity. And even then, not at the right time.

The sun doesn't shine at night. That sounds trivial. But for a data center running a training run over several weeks, it's a deal-breaker. You can't pause an AI training run because clouds roll in. You can't shut down an inference service for 200 million users because the wind dies down in the evening.

And in northern climates, there's the phenomenon of what Germans call *Dunkelflaute*—literally "dark doldrums," days or weeks in winter with almost no sun and no wind simultaneously. Germany experienced this in January 2024: for over a week, solar and wind production combined ran at under 10 percent of installed capacity. Coal and gas plants had to step in. An AI data center running solely on renewables would have had a serious problem that week.

The area requirement compounds the problem. A nuclear plant needs about 1.3 square miles for 1 gigawatt of power^[134]^. A solar farm needs 45 to 75 square miles for the same energy output—35 to 60 times more. A wind farm needs 260 to 360 square miles—200 to 280 times more. In a densely populated country like Germany or the Netherlands, renewables hit physical limits long before costs become relevant.

What about storage? The idea of storing excess solar power in batteries during the day and using it at night sounds logical. But the math doesn't work out.

Gordon Hughes, energy economist at the University of^[135]^ Edinburgh and former World Bank advisor, calculated in a detailed cost analysis what it would take to power a 1-gigawatt data center entirely with renewables at 99.99 percent reliability. His result: 1.8 GW offshore wind, 1.5 GW solar, and 8 GWh of battery storage. Total cost: $13.3 billion. For comparison: the same supply with a conventional nuclear plant plus gas backup costs $9.85 billion.

And even that $13.3 billion doesn't fully solve the problem. Eight hours of battery storage covers one night. But what happens during a *Dunkelflaute* lasting five days? Or a three-week maintenance period? Battery storage for 35 days would cost over $60 billion. For a single data center.

Hughes said of LCOE comparisons: "The only use of LCOE comparisons between different technologies is lobbying and PR." He has a point. When you add the so-called firming costs to solar's raw generation costs—storage, grid expansion, and gas backup for the dark hours—you land at $90 to $150 per megawatt-hour. Suddenly nuclear doesn't look so expensive after all.

Don't get me wrong: renewables are an important part of the energy mix. For household electricity, for industry, for charging electric vehicles. But for the permanent, uninterruptible baseload that AI data centers need, they alone are not the solution. That's not ideology. That's physics.

Google, the world's largest private buyer of renewable energy, understood the problem. Despite hundreds of solar and wind deals, not a single Google data center has achieved 100 percent carbon-free energy. Not on a single day of the year. The reason: the long-term power purchase agreements—the so-called PPAs—with solar and wind farms are essentially accounting exercises. The wind farm is in Texas, the data center is in Virginia. The solar power is fed into the grid at noon; the data center needs it at three in the morning. On paper, the math works. Physically, the power at night comes from the gas plant next door.

That's exactly why Google, Microsoft, Amazon, and Meta are now turning to nuclear energy. Not out of nostalgia, not because they've suddenly become nuclear fans—but because it's the only carbon-free technology that delivers power around the clock. In sufficient quantities. On a small footprint.

---

### Why Tech Giants Are Buying Nuclear Power Plants


![Tech giants become energy companies](images-en/abb-14.png)


This is where it gets relevant for investors. Because the tech companies haven't just recognized the energy problem—they've started solving it themselves. And the way they're solving it says a lot about the future of energy markets.

Microsoft signed a 20-year power purchase agreement with Constellation Energy,^[136]^ which is investing $1.6 billion to restart the Three Mile Island reactor in Pennsylvania. Yes, that Three Mile Island—the site of the worst nuclear accident in US history. The irony is obvious. But Microsoft's calculus is sober: Three Mile Island delivers reliable baseload power, zero carbon emissions, close to important data centers on the East Coast. The site's past is a PR problem. The physics are solid.

Amazon invested $20 billion in the Susquehanna nuclear plant^[137]^ and struck additional deals with utilities. Google ordered 500 megawatts from Kairos Energy,^[138]^ a startup developing Small Modular Reactors. Meta issued requests for 1 to 4 gigawatts of new nuclear generation.

These aren't footnotes. These are the largest companies in the world investing billions to secure their own power supply—because the market can't deliver fast enough what they need.

The critical insight for investors: These companies aren't buying power from utilities. They're buying the power plants. They're vertically integrating their energy supply, just as they've already vertically integrated their chip development, their cloud infrastructure, and increasingly their AI models.

This changes the investment landscape. Traditional utilities won't be the primary beneficiaries of the AI energy boom—at least not to the same degree as the companies controlling the technology. Value flows to the technology providers at the ends of the chain: the reactor builders, the uranium suppliers, and the tech companies themselves.

Small Modular Reactors are the concept redefining nuclear energy for the AI age. Instead of massive power plants that take decades to plan and billions to build, SMRs are compact, factory-prefabricated, and deployable near data centers.

NuScale Power received the first NRC certification for an SMR design^[139]^—the approval from America's nuclear regulator that opens the path to commercial production. Oklo, backed by Sam Altman, develops compact fission reactors designed specifically for data centers. Kairos Energy, which won the Google deal, is working on a molten-salt-cooled reactor design.

The advantages for AI data centers are obvious: SMRs deliver 24/7 baseload power, independent of weather or time of day. They have an extremely small physical footprint. They can be placed near data centers, reducing transmission losses. And they're scalable—a company can start with one module and add more as demand grows.

The timelines are optimistic but not unrealistic. The first commercial SMRs could deliver power by the late 2020s. By 2035, they could be a significant part of the energy mix powering AI infrastructure.

Investment implications: uranium, SMR manufacturers, energy infrastructure. For the investment portfolio we'll build in Part IV, the energy theme has concrete implications.

Uranium is the fuel of nuclear energy—and thus indirectly the fuel of the AI revolution. The uranium price has more than doubled since 2020,^[140]^ driven by the nuclear renaissance and demand from tech giants. Cameco, the world's largest publicly traded uranium producer, is a direct bet on rising uranium demand. Kazatomprom in Kazakhstan, the world's largest producer overall, is also investable—though with higher geopolitical risk.

SMR developers are classic asymmetric bets. NuScale Power (ticker: SMR—yes, really) is the most advanced publicly traded SMR developer. Oklo has been investable since its IPO via a SPAC—a Special Purpose Acquisition Company, a shell entity created solely to acquire another company and take it public. Both are pre-revenue companies with high risk, but in a scenario where SMRs become the standard for data center energy, the upside potential is enormous.

Existing nuclear plant operators like Constellation Energy are already benefiting. Constellation is the largest nuclear plant operator in the US and has signed long-term power purchase agreements with Microsoft and other tech companies. Unlike SMR startups, Constellation already has operating plants, regulated revenues, and positive cash flow. It's the more conservative bet on the nuclear renaissance.

### The Hidden Crisis: Why Uranium Is Running Short

The uranium price has tripled since 2020. From just under $30 per pound of U3O8 to around $88 in February 2026. And yet hardly anyone is talking about it.

Supply can't keep pace with demand. Global reactor demand stands at roughly 179 million pounds of U3O8 per year. Primary mine production covers only 140 to 150 million pounds of that. The gap—30 to 40 million pounds annually—has so far been filled by stockpiles, reprocessed material, and other secondary sources. But those sources are shrinking. From 14 percent of supply today to an estimated 8 percent by 2030.

The problem is compounded by the two largest producers cutting simultaneously. Kazatomprom in Kazakhstan, with 24 percent of world production the single largest producer, announced a^[141]^ 10 percent production cut for 2026. The reason: shortages of the sulfuric acid needed for uranium extraction. Cameco in Canada, with 14 to 20 percent market share the largest publicly traded producer, had to cut its production forecast^[142]^ by 19 percent in August 2025. Delays at the McArthur River mine.

Add geopolitical risks. Niger, a significant producing country, was rocked by a military coup in 2023. French uranium company Orano, which operates a substantial portion of its production there, faces an uncertain future. Together, Kazakhstan and Niger supply roughly half of global primary production. Both countries are politically unstable.

And then there's the bottleneck almost nobody talks about: uranium enrichment. Natural uranium must be enriched before it can be used as reactor fuel. Global enrichment capacity stands at a nominal 77 million SWU per year (SWU is the unit measuring enrichment work). Of that, Russia's Rosatom^[143]^ controls about 40 percent. Forty percent. In a single hand. The hand of a country the West is currently waging an economic war against.

The US sourced 24 percent of its enrichment services from Russia until recently. In May 2024, Biden signed^[144]^ the "Prohibiting Russian Uranium Imports Act." But Western capacity without Russia sits at only 37 to 40 million SWU. With rising demand from new reactors and SMRs, a structural deficit looms from 2030 onward. ARK Invest projects that SWU capacity "will become scarce by 2030." For the new Small Modular Reactors, it gets even more critical: HALEU fuel, which many SMR designs require, needs five to six times more enrichment work per kilogram than conventional fuel.

COP28 officially acknowledged the problem[^145]: over 20 countries committed to tripling nuclear capacity by 2050. Demand for uranium and enrichment will rise. Supply is growing more slowly. Prices have only one direction.

One detail illustrates nuclear's energy density: a single uranium fuel pellet, the size of a fingertip, generates as much energy as a ton of coal. That's why nuclear plants can operate on 1.3 square miles instead of an area the size of a small state.

### The Scale of Investment

We've seen the specific deals Microsoft, Amazon, Google, and Meta have struck for nuclear plants and SMRs. But the dimension goes far beyond that.

Meta plans $135 billion for AI infrastructure in a single year—2026. Microsoft is investing over $60 billion. Amazon roughly $100 billion. Google around $75 billion. Elon Musk's xAI has poured $18 billion into the Colossus supercomputer in Memphis alone—a facility with 555,000 GPUs consuming 2 gigawatts of power. Two gigawatts. That's enough to power a city of a million people.

Combined, the tech industry is investing over a trillion dollars in AI infrastructure in 2025 and 2026. And every single data center, every single GPU needs power. A lot of power.

What we're witnessing is vertical integration at a scale the tech industry has never seen. These companies aren't simply buying power on the market. They're buying the power plants. They're building their own solar farms. They're financing nuclear research. They're becoming energy companies, whether they want to or not.

The US needs to build an additional 30 to 50 gigawatts of capacity by 2030—just for AI data centers. That's equivalent to 30 to 50 large nuclear plants. Or tens of thousands of wind turbines. Or a solar panel array the size of a small state.

Whoever controls the energy controls the pace of the AI revolution. Jensen Huang no longer talks about "data centers" at CES 2026—he talks about "AI Factories." Factories need power. And the factories that produce AI need more power than anything humanity has ever built.

### The Bottleneck Nobody Has on Their Radar: Water

Data centers don't just need power. They need water. A lot of water. And this topic is ignored in nearly every analysis of AI infrastructure.

Servers generate heat. GPUs under full load run at 70 to 90 degrees Celsius. That heat has to be removed. Most large data centers use evaporative cooling: water is sprayed, evaporates, and cools the air in the process. The water that evaporates is consumed. Not contaminated, not recyclable. Gone.

A typical hyperscale data center consumes around 500,000 gallons of water per day. Per day. Google's data center in Council Bluffs, Iowa consumed a billion gallons in 2024 alone.^[146]^ That's enough to cover the drinking water needs of every household in Iowa.

Overall, Google consumed over 8 billion gallons of water^[147]^ for its data centers in 2024. Microsoft's water consumption rose 34 percent year over year. Meta consumed 813 million gallons, 95 percent of it for data centers.

AI makes it worse. GPU clusters for AI training have a power density of 40 to over 100 kilowatts per rack. Normal cloud servers run at 6 to 10 kilowatts. Ten to twenty times more heat per square foot means ten to twenty times more cooling demand.


::: {.infobox}
### Google's Water Problem in Oregon

The conflicts have already begun. In The Dalles, Oregon^[148]^—a small town of 16,000 residents—Google has operated multiple data centers since 2006. When the local newspaper "The Oregonian" investigated the water consumption, Google paid $100,000 to fund the city's lawsuit against the newspaper. Residents now call Google "Voldemort." Google is planning more data centers in the region. The city wants to source water from Mount Hood National Forest to meet growing demand. Environmentalists suspect: the water is primarily for Google, not for the population.

:::


In Mesa and Goodyear, Arizona, new data centers are going up in a region that already suffers from water scarcity. The Colorado River, which supplies the American Southwest, has carried less water than historical norms for two decades.

And then the question everyone asks: How much water does a single ChatGPT query consume? The answers vary. OpenAI officially cites 0.32 milliliters, but that measures only the direct water consumption at the data center itself. When you include the water consumption of electricity generation, a UC Riverside study arrives at 10 to 25 milliliters per query. A Washington Post study pegged the water consumption of a 100-word email via GPT-4 at 519 milliliters—about half a liter. With 200 million daily ChatGPT users, it adds up.

Solutions exist. Direct liquid cooling, where cooling plates sit directly on the chips, can reduce cooling energy needs by 50 percent. Immersion cooling, where servers are fully submerged in a dielectric fluid, saves even more. In Scandinavia and Iceland, data centers run on pure air cooling—no water at all. But these solutions cost more and aren't yet standard.

Google and Microsoft have pledged to be "water positive" by 2030—meaning they'll return more water than they consume. So far, the opposite has happened: consumption at both companies keeps rising year over year.

Water is an underestimated factor in the AI debate. Electricity can be generated—from fossil sources if necessary. But water in a desert in Arizona is water in a desert in Arizona. You can't build a gas plant that produces water. The tech giants will increasingly make location decisions not only based on power availability but on water access. That's a factor that barely figures in investment calculations yet.

The AI revolution isn't just a software revolution. It's a physical revolution, with consequences for energy markets, raw materials, and infrastructure that extend far beyond the technology sector. Anyone investing only in software is missing half the story.

But there's another half that can't be measured in gigawatts and cubic meters: What happens to the people whose work the machines take over? The energy question is an engineering problem. The work question is a societal one.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ AI's energy consumption is growing exponentially.
>
> ◆ Nuclear energy is the only scalable solution for baseload demand.
>
> ◆ Tech giants are buying nuclear plants—uranium is becoming a strategic asset.

## Chapter 10: The Human Cost — Work, Meaning, and Societal Upheaval


---

> *"He who has a why to live can bear almost any how."*
>
> — Viktor Frankl

---


![Which jobs disappear, which emerge](images-en/abb-16.png)


### From Tribal Chiefs to CEOs: A Brief History of Work


![The history of work — four eras and the current break](images-en/abb-17.png)


Before we talk about the future of work, we need to understand how short the history of work as we know it actually is. What we consider "normal" today—the eight-hour day, the office, the résumé, the career ladder—is an arrangement younger than most trees in a forest.

For most of human history, "work" didn't exist, at least not in the form we know. Hunter-gatherers, who account for over 90 percent of human history, worked three to five hours a day. The rest was socializing, storytelling, craft, play. The agricultural revolution 10,000 years ago brought settlements, land ownership, laws, warriors. And with it came the feudal system. A lord owned the land. Peasants worked it. The structure was the same everywhere, from the pharaohs to the Russian serfs: whoever controlled the means of production controlled the labor.


Then industrialization came, and it needed something new: obedient, punctual, uniformly educated workers. The factory required a new type of human. A farmer worked to the rhythm of the seasons, at his own pace. A factory worker had to stand at a machine at six in the morning, twelve hours straight, in lockstep with the machine. The Prussian education system delivered exactly that: children sorted into age groups like factory shifts, sitting in rows like assembly lines, with the central learning objective of respecting authority. Horace Mann brought the model to the US in 1843; from there it spread worldwide. The system wasn't designed to foster independent thinking. It was designed to produce a workforce that functions in factories.

The terms^[150]^ "white collar" and "blue collar" are 20th-century inventions, barely a hundred years old. The idea of selling your time to an organization that pays you a fixed monthly sum has existed for less than 0.1 percent of human history.

Marx put it this way in 1848[^149]: "The modern bourgeois society has not done away with class antagonisms. It has but established new classes, new conditions of oppression in place of the old ones." You don't have to be a Marxist to recognize the structure: the feudal lord becomes the factory owner, then the CEO. The levy is no longer called a "tithe" but a "profit margin." But the basic structure—one class owns the means of production, another works with them—is the same.

### The New Feudal Lords

And now we face the next transition. The question is: Who will be the new feudal lords?

In the feudal system, whoever owned the land was in control. In industrial society, whoever owned the factories. In the knowledge economy, whoever owned the platforms and data. Google, Amazon, Meta—they are the feudal lords of the digital age, and their "peasants" are the billions of users who deliver their data as tribute without being paid for it.

### The Three Pillars of the Machine Economy

In the machine economy, control will belong to whoever owns three things:

- **The AI models** (intelligence)
- **The robots** (physical labor)
- **The crypto infrastructure** (the payment system)

These are the new means of production. And their owners will be the new feudal lords.

NVIDIA builds the hardware on which intelligence runs. OpenAI, Anthropic, and Google build the intelligence itself. Tesla, Figure AI, and Unitree build the robots. And the crypto protocols—Bitcoin, Ethereum, the stablecoin issuers—form the financial system in which the machines operate.

The parallel to feudalism is uncomfortably precise. In the feudal system, a peasant couldn't simply "stop being a peasant." He was bound to the land, and the land belonged to the lord. In the machine economy, a knowledge worker whose job is replaced by AI can't simply "stop being a knowledge worker." His human capital—his education, his experience—is tied to an economic structure that is becoming obsolete.

The difference from feudalism: this time there's an exit. You can own the new means of production. Not as a feudal lord—as an investor. Anyone who owns NVIDIA stock owns a share of the AI revolution's hardware. Anyone who owns Bitcoin owns a share of the machine economy's monetary system. Anyone who invests in robotics companies owns a share of the physical labor force of the future.

This is the deeper justification for this book's investment thesis: it's not just about making money. It's about being on the right side of the next structural transformation. The peasant in the 18th century couldn't invest in factories. He could only watch as the new world passed him by. The knowledge worker in the 21st century has that option. Stocks, ETFs, cryptocurrencies—all accessible, for everyone, with a smartphone and a brokerage account.

There will be new feudal lords. The question is whether you'll be among them or among their peasants.

The WEF said it openly: "You'll own nothing and be happy."

The feudal lords of the future consider your owning nothing a feature, not a bug. For them. Not for you.

Remember the causal chain:

Machines replace your labor. You lose your labor income. Without income, no capital. Without capital, no investments. Without investments, no stake in the machine economy.

The result: you consume what others produce. You rent what others own. You wait for transfers from those who can still pay taxes, or from a state that itself becomes ever more dependent on the few who control the means of production.

That's not a dystopian novel. It's the logical consequence of passivity in a world where the machines are coming.

But it doesn't have to be your fate.

### The School System Question

And the school system? The system designed 200 years ago to produce factory workers is still training children for a world that no longer exists. Punctuality, obedience, standardized knowledge, timed exams: all of these were requirements of industrial society. In a world where AI can retrieve any factual knowledge in seconds, where creativity, critical thinking, and the ability to collaborate with machines are the relevant competencies, our school system is an anachronism.

But that's a debate for another book. For this book, one insight is enough: the way we work is not a law of nature. It was invented—by humans, for specific purposes—and it's being reinvented right now. Understanding that gives you a head start.

### Which Jobs Disappear, Which Emerge

With this historical context, we can look at the current upheaval more soberly. We're not mourning an eternal order. We're watching the next in a long series of transitions.

The history of technology is full of predictions about mass job loss that turned out to be overblown. The Luddites smashed looms. Bank tellers feared the ATM. Bookkeepers feared the spreadsheet. And every time, more new jobs emerged than old ones disappeared.

But there's a concrete difference from earlier waves of automation:

The crucial difference: previous automation waves replaced physical routine work. The loom replaced the weaver. The assembly line replaced the craftsman. The ATM replaced the bank teller. In every case, human work shifted upward—from physical to cognitive, from routine to creative, from execution to planning.

AI breaks this pattern because it automates precisely the cognitive, creative, planning work that humans fled to in previous waves. There's no obvious "up" anymore. Unless you define "up" as "owner of the machines that do the work."

A McKinsey study from 2024 estimates that by 2030, 400 million jobs worldwide will be affected by AI automation.^[151]^ "Affected" doesn't mean "gone"—it means a significant portion of the tasks will be automated. Some jobs will be fully replaced, some transformed, some only marginally changed.

Most affected: customer service and call centers, basic bookkeeping and data processing, standard legal advice, translation and basic copywriting, entry-level positions in software development, parts of financial analysis.

Less affected, for now: trades with high variability (plumbing, electrical work), professions with a strong human relationship component (therapy, counseling), leadership positions with a strategic dimension.

Newly emerging: AI training and supervision, prompt engineering and AI workflow design, robot maintenance, AI ethics and governance, and roles we can't even name yet.


::: {.infobox}
### Harari's "Useless Class"

Yuval Noah Harari^[261]^^[211]^^[152]^ goes further. In "Homo Deus" in 2016, the Israeli historian coined a term more uncomfortable than anything the WEF reports formulate: the "useless class." Not unemployed. Useless. Harari's argument isn't that these people are lazy or incapable. His argument is that the economy simply no longer needs them. Not as workers, not as soldiers, not even as consumers—if machines handle consumption among themselves.

:::


That's harsher than the official forecast. McKinsey talks about transformation. Harari talks about obsolescence. The question isn't "what new job will I get?" but "does society even have a use for me?" That's a question no retraining program can answer. The WEF's number of 170 million new^[153]^ jobs may be accurate. But it doesn't answer whether the people who lose their old jobs are the ones who'll get the new ones.

Perhaps the most honest confirmation comes from the AI developers themselves. Anthropic, one of the leading AI companies, published a study^[154]^ in early 2026 that doesn't ask what AI could theoretically do, but what it actually does—measured against real usage data from their own system, Claude. The new metric is called "observed exposure," and it fills a gap that all previous forecasts left open: the difference between potential and reality.

The result is a massive gulf. In computer and mathematical occupations, 94 percent of tasks could theoretically be handled by AI. But only 33 percent actually are. Adoption lags dramatically behind potential in nearly every occupational category. That means two things simultaneously: we're still at the very beginning of the automation curve. And much more is coming.

But where AI is actually being used, it hits precisely the layer that felt safest: more female, more white, more highly educated, with 47 percent higher salaries than average. The white-collar world. Thirty percent of all workers have zero exposure. Cooks, mechanics, bartenders. The revolution is happening in the office towers, not in the workshops.

Even more alarming: while overall unemployment in AI-exposed occupations has so far remained stable, young workers between 22 and 25 are already being hired 14 percent less frequently than before the AI boom. Older workers don't show this effect. Companies hire fewer before they start firing. That's the canary in the coal mine.


---


### Knowledge Workers as the New Peasants
*The bitterest irony: this revolution hits exactly the class that felt safest.*

In recent decades, knowledge workers were told they were the future. Learn to code. Get your MBA. Factory workers may have lost their jobs to machines, but you're safe—your work requires creativity, judgment, human interaction.

AI challenges that narrative directly. When an AI system analyzes legal documents faster than a lawyer, creates financial analysis more precisely than an analyst, writes marketing copy that converts better than a copywriter—what exactly is the competitive advantage of human knowledge work?


The honest answer: it's shrinking. It's not disappearing overnight—AI has weaknesses, makes mistakes, needs oversight. But it shrinks with every model update, every new benchmark result, every company that discovers it can replace three employees with one employee plus AI.

Knowledge workers are the middle class in most Western societies. They are the consumers, the taxpayers, the voters. When this class comes under pressure, the consequences extend far beyond the labor market.

They will become, in the worst case, the new peasants: people whose human capital—their education, their experience—is tied to an economic structure that is becoming obsolete. Just like the peasants of the 18th century, who were experts in a world that was vanishing.

### Why Our Prosperity Compass Is Broken

There's a deeper problem that overarches the debate about jobs and basic income: we're measuring prosperity with an instrument that registers progress as catastrophe.

GDP—the number that governments, central banks, and markets orient themselves by—has a structural weakness: it's blind to abundance. When Wikipedia makes encyclopedia knowledge freely available, GDP registers the publishers' revenue loss but not humanity's knowledge gain. When an AI agent does a lawyer's work in minutes at a fraction of the cost, GDP shrinks while actual utility explodes.

Emad Mostaque puts it succinctly in^[162]^ "The Last Economy": "We are about to achieve post-scarcity in the realm of intelligence, and our scarcity-based economic system is going to process this abundance as poverty."


That's not an abstract problem. It has real consequences. When AI drives the cost of legal advice, diagnoses, translations, financial analysis, and programming down to a fraction, that will show up as declining revenue, declining salaries in those fields, declining GDP contributions. Politically, it will be read as a crisis. The reflex will be regulation, slowdown, protectionism. But the real crisis isn't that things are getting cheaper. The crisis is that our measurement instrument can't distinguish between decline and progress.

At the same time, GDP counts things as growth that nobody would call progress: natural disasters requiring reconstruction, divorces keeping lawyers busy, diseases generating treatments. The more that breaks, the better the number. The more abundance that emerges, the worse the number. That's not a bug in the formula. It's a design flaw that went unnoticed in a world of scarcity and becomes a fundamental orientation problem in a world of AI-driven abundance.

Mostaque goes further and describes what he calls "non-metabolic labor": AI and robots need no sleep, no food, no health insurance, no pension. They need electricity. In a purely economic calculus that measures only productivity per cost unit, the value of human labor can turn negative—because the costs of insurance, labor law, sick days, and error rates exceed the output a human can still deliver compared to a machine. That sounds brutal. It is brutal. What is the human cost when machines don't just work cheaper, but when the entire metric we use to measure work is rigged in their favor?


### What Research Actually Says About Basic Income
When work disappears, someone has to pay. The obvious idea is called Universal Basic Income, and it's being championed by exactly the people who profit most from automation. Sam Altman, CEO of OpenAI, has come out in favor of UBI. Elon Musk goes further and talks about a "Universal High Income." The question is: does it work?

*The research gives a nuanced answer.*

::: {.infobox}
### Finland's UBI Experiment

Finland ran the most well-known national UBI experiment from 2017 to 2018. Two thousand randomly selected unemployed people received €560 per month, unconditionally, tax-free. The result surprised both sides of the debate. The recipients didn't become lazy. They actually worked six days more than the control group during the measurement period. But they didn't become entrepreneurs or innovators either. The real effect was psychological: less depression, less loneliness, more life satisfaction. UBI worked not as an employment program but as a health intervention.

:::

Sam Altman's own study,^[159]^ funded through his project OpenResearch, was the largest in US history. Three thousand participants in Illinois and Texas received $1,000 per month for three years. The result: recipients worked 1.3 to 1.4 fewer hours per week. Not dramatic, but measurable. The researchers had hoped the financial cushion would enable participants to find better jobs. That didn't happen. What did happen: people used the time for what they considered more important than work. Whether you see that as progress or a problem depends on whether you view work as a moral value or a means to an end.

In Stockton, California,^[160]^ the opposite emerged. Mayor Michael Tubbs gave 125 residents $500 per month for two years. There, full-time employment actually rose. The financial cushion enabled participants to quit bad jobs and find better ones. In a city that had gone bankrupt in 2012, people needed stability first before they could act at all.

In Kenya, the world's largest long-term experiment has been running since 2016. Roughly 6,000 people in rural villages receive just over $20 per month, accompanied by MIT Nobel laureate Abhijit^[161]^ Banerjee. The most surprising finding: a lump sum of $500 worked better than $20/month over two years, even though the total was identical. Five hundred dollars at once provides startup capital for a small business. Twenty dollars a month covers daily costs but changes nothing.


The Gulf states are the closest real-world example of a functioning resource rent. Saudi Arabia, Kuwait, the Emirates finance generous transfers to their citizens from oil revenues. Free healthcare, subsidized housing, direct cash payments. Poverty among citizens effectively doesn't exist. But the governments themselves see the problem: citizens have become passive. Saudi Arabia's Vision 2030 is actively trying to transform citizens from "recipients" into "entrepreneurs." The material wealth is there. The drive is not.

What does this tell us about the machine economy? Three things.

First: UBI doesn't make people lazy. Multiple independent studies confirm this. But it doesn't automatically make them more productive or entrepreneurial either. It removes existential anxiety—and that's worth a lot.

Second: the structure of the payment matters more than the amount. Startup capital can change lives. Small monthly payments can ease daily life but can't enable leaps. Sam Altman's most radical proposal—"Universal Basic Compute"—points in this direction: instead of money, every person gets a share of AI compute that they can use, sell, or pool. AI compute becomes currency. It sounds crazy. It might work.

Third: the Saudi lesson. Material wealth doesn't solve the meaning question. And the meaning question is the real problem.


*But there's a deeper problem that the UBI discussion overlooks.*
*UBI creates dependency. On whoever pays.*

Whoever draws their income from the state can't make truly independent decisions. You're grateful, not sovereign. You wait instead of creating. You live on what others give you, not on what you built yourself.

"You'll own nothing and be happy"—that's the formula of the new feudal lords. Universal Basic Income is the ration you give the peasants so they don't revolt.

*I don't want a ration. I want a stake.*

The answer to the machine economy isn't hoping for transfers. The answer is owning the machines. Or at least a share of them.

*That's the difference between survival and prosperity.*

*What Happens When Work Stops Providing Meaning*

Viktor Frankl, the Austrian psychiatrist and^[157]^ Holocaust survivor, called it the "existential vacuum": the feeling of meaninglessness that arises when a person has no purpose. Frankl's core thesis was simple. Man is not primarily driven by pleasure, as Freud claimed, nor by power, as Adler claimed. He is driven by the search for meaning. Take away the meaning, and he gets sick.


Psychological research confirms this with impressive breadth. The long-term unemployed have a significantly higher risk of depression, substance abuse, and suicide. Not because of the lack of money alone. Even people who are financially secure but have no purpose suffer. Studies on "retired husband syndrome" show that men become measurably sicker after retirement if they can't find an alternative occupation.

Anne Case and Angus Deaton,^[155]^ both Princeton economists and Deaton a Nobel laureate, documented the phenomenon of "Deaths of Despair." In the US, life expectancy for white Americans without a college degree has been declining since the late 1990s—not rising. Declining. In one of the richest countries in the world. The causes: opioid addiction, alcoholism, suicide. What the affected have in common: they lost their economic function. Their jobs were automated or outsourced. Their communities collapsed. Their meaning vanished.

Japan's Hikikomori phenomenon^[156]^ shows a different variant. More than a million young Japanese have withdrawn from society. They don't leave their rooms, don't work, maintain no social contacts. The causes are complex, but one factor stands out: in a society that defines identity strongly through professional achievement, failure in work means failure as a human being. Those who can't find an acceptable place in the system withdraw entirely.


When AI and robotics actually take over a significant portion of knowledge work and physical work—which is the thesis of this book—we face a meaning crisis of unprecedented dimensions. Not because people will go hungry; UBI, transfers, social safety nets can solve the material problem—but because humans need a purpose, something that challenges them, that makes them feel competent, that sets the alarm clock in the morning.


There are positive scenarios. A renaissance of creativity. People freed from routine work discover art, music, craft, community, care. The Greek polis drew no distinction between "work" and "life." Work was what slaves did. Free citizens philosophized, debated, created art. Perhaps the AI revolution is the moment when humanity returns to that state—without the slavery, because the machines take the role of the slaves.

Perhaps. But the evidence so far is less optimistic. The people who lost their jobs to automation—in Ohio, in Northern England, in the French banlieues—didn't experience a renaissance. They experienced opioid epidemics. They voted for Brexit. They voted for Trump. Not out of stupidity. Out of despair.


The meaning crisis is relevant to this book because it represents a risk to the entire investment thesis. A society suffering from a massive meaning crisis is not a stable society. And unstable societies are bad environments for investments, no matter how impressive the technology. That's why societal instability is listed as an independent risk in the risk chapter.


---


### Political Radicalization: Luddites 2.0

History shows that technology-driven unemployment leads to political radicalization. Not maybe. Reliably.

The Luddites of the early 19th century smashed machines. The political movements of industrialization ranged from the labor movement to Marxism to violent revolutions. Globalization over recent decades has fueled the rise of populism: Trump, Brexit, AfD, Le Pen.

What happens when AI and robots replace millions of jobs in developed countries? Anti-technology movements. Populist parties promising "robot taxes." Nationalist tendencies declaring foreign tech companies the enemy. Redistribution demands that skim corporate profits.

For investors: societal stability isn't background noise. It's a risk factor.


---

> *Universal Basic Income: The New Tribute*
> *When machines take over the bulk of the work, society must find a new mechanism to distribute income. Work as the primary distribution mechanism stops functioning when there isn't enough work for everyone.*
> *That sounds like utopia. I consider it inevitability. A society in which 30 percent of the population stands without income and without prospects isn't a functioning society. Some form of basic security is coming.*

---


### The Winner-Loser Divide

The convergence of AI, robotics, and crypto will create wealth—enormous wealth. The productivity gains are real. But this wealth will be distributed more unequally than ever before.

Those who own the infrastructure of the new world—the chips, the models, the robots, the crypto protocols—will be among the wealthiest people and institutions in history. Those who use this infrastructure will also benefit. And those who neither own nor effectively use it will fall behind.

This book is an investment book, and its goal is to help you end up on the right side of this divide. A medieval peasant couldn't buy land. A modern investor can buy NVIDIA stock and Bitcoin. Access to the means of production of the future is open. You just have to use it.

That's the critical difference from every previous transformation.

The loom belonged to the factory owner. The railroad belonged to the barons. The land belonged to the feudal lord. If you weren't born into the upper class, you had no chance of being on the right side.

Today is radically different.

With a smartphone and a few hundred dollars, you can start. NVIDIA stock. Bitcoin. Ethereum. Robotics and AI ETFs. These aren't secrets of the elite. They're public, accessible, for everyone.

The window is open. But it won't stay open forever.

The causal chain is already running: machines replace labor. If you don't act today, you'll have nothing to invest tomorrow. And if you invest nothing, in ten years you'll be on the wrong side of the greatest wealth transformation in history.

That's not fearmongering. That's math.

### What This Means for Professions: The Banking Example

The abstract trends in this chapter become immediately tangible when you project them onto a specific industry. Banking is particularly well suited because it's simultaneously highly regulated, data-intensive, and personnel-heavy. What happens there will, in modified form, hit every industry where humans work with information.

Let's start with the jobs that disappear. Not maybe. Certainly.

A loan officer who reviews credit applications spends his day matching income documents, checking credit scores, evaluating collateral, and working through a standardized decision tree. An AI system does this in seconds and makes fewer errors. Not because AI is smarter than the loan officer, but because the task is rule-based, and AI processes rule-based tasks faster than any human. The same applies to routine processing of account openings, address changes, standing orders, and the entire range of standard operations that still require human hands today.

Basic investment advice faces the same problem. A bank advisor who recommends a mix of savings accounts and a global ETF to a customer with $50,000 in savings does nothing a robo-advisor can't do better, cheaper, and without conflicts of interest. Advisory that consists of querying a risk profile and then pulling the matching product off the shelf is algorithmically trivial. The bank branch that offers this kind of advice as its core product won't exist in ten years. In Scandinavia, it barely exists today.

Then there are the jobs that change radically without disappearing.

Relationship banking—serving wealthy or complex clients—remains human, but the tools change completely. A corporate banker discussing the financing of a new production facility with his client will be supported by an AI that synthesizes market data, industry risks, the client's balance sheet history, and the current interest rate environment in real time. The human brings the relationship, the trust, the judgment in gray areas. The machine brings the data and the analysis. Whoever masters both will be more valuable than ever. Whoever can only do the relationship but doesn't understand the tools becomes obsolete.

Compliance and risk management face a similar transformation. Anti-money laundering today consists largely of rule-based transaction monitoring that produces absurdly many false positives. In some banks, compliance teams must dismiss 95 percent of automated suspicious activity reports as false alarms. AI can dramatically improve the hit rate by detecting patterns that rule-based systems miss. But the final decision—whether to file a suspicious activity report with authorities—stays with the human. The compliance department gets smaller, but each individual employee needs more technical expertise than today.

And then there are jobs that are only just emerging. They have names nobody knew five years ago.

AI supervision in banking will become its own discipline. When an AI makes credit decisions, someone has to ensure it hasn't learned discriminatory patterns. That it doesn't systematically disadvantage applicants from certain zip codes. That it makes explainable decisions that can withstand regulatory scrutiny. This requires people who understand banking, statistics, and the limitations of machine learning simultaneously.

Data quality sounds unsexy but becomes the critical bottleneck. AI is only as good as the data it was trained on. A bank with 30 years of legacy IT architecture, three merged legacy systems, and customer data in 14 different formats has a massive data problem. People who can clean, standardize, and make this data usable for AI systems will be in desperate demand.

Prompt engineering and AI workflow design are the bridge between the business side and the technology. A credit analyst who understands how to instruct an AI system to perform a complex corporate analysis is ten times more productive than one doing everything manually. And ethical AI governance becomes a board-level responsibility. When an AI system makes a wrong credit decision costing millions, who's liable? The board needs to be able to answer that.

The major banks understood this long ago. JPMorgan invests $17 billion per year in technology and has over 2,000 AI applications deployed.^[163]^ Jamie Dimon tells his people openly: if you don't understand AI, you have no future with us. DBS Bank in Singapore, Asia's largest bank by assets, goes further. It hasn't just converted its internal processes to AI—it's pioneering the tokenization of assets. Bank deposits, bonds, even real estate rights are traded there as digital tokens on a blockchain. That merges traditional banking with the crypto infrastructure we described in Chapter 3.

What does this mean concretely for someone working in the banking sector today—or looking to enter it?

First: learn to work with AI tools. This doesn't require a computer science degree. A weekend with Claude or ChatGPT, an online course on prompt engineering, experimenting with an AI-powered analytics tool—that's enough to start. Second: specialize in what AI can't do. Complex client relationships, regulatory judgment in gray areas, industry-specific knowledge that exists in no training dataset. Third: understand the intersection of banking and technology. Whoever can do credit risk and Python simultaneously is sitting on gold. Fourth: don't stand still. The half-life of banking knowledge is shortening with every model update. Continuing education isn't a luxury anymore—it's a survival tactic.

The question of how societies handle this transformation inevitably leads to regulation. And that's where it gets political.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ 30 to 50 percent of knowledge work could be automated.
>
> ◆ The meaning crisis is the greater risk than mere job loss.
>
> ◆ Adaptability and AI competence become survival skills.

## Chapter 11: Regulation — The Elephant in the Room

"Regulation is the attempt to solve tomorrow's problems with yesterday's tools."


![Regulatory speeds—USA, China, and Europe compared](images-en/abb-18.png)


No technology exists in a vacuum. Every transformation powerful enough to rewrite the economy summons the state. That was true for railroads, for electricity, for the telephone, for the internet. And it will be no different for AI.

Regulation is coming. That's certain. The question is how it comes, and what that means for investors.


---

### EU AI Act: The Detailed Analysis
The EU AI Act took effect in its first stages in 2024 and is the most ambitious AI regulation in the world. Let me take it seriously—more seriously than most tech commentators who reflexively reject it, and more seriously than the EU officials who reflexively defend it.

The basic principle is a risk-based approach. AI systems are classified into four categories:

Unacceptable risk—banned. This includes social scoring (as in China), manipulative techniques, real-time facial recognition in public spaces (with exceptions for law enforcement). That's reasonable. Few seriously argue against it.

High risk—strict requirements. This covers AI in critical infrastructure, education, employment, credit scoring, law enforcement, immigration. Extensive documentation, risk assessments, dataset quality checks, human oversight, and transparency reports are required. The principle is sound, but implementation is the problem. Compliance costs are substantial, definitions are broad, and the question of what constitutes "high risk" leaves enough room for interpretation to keep legal departments busy for years.

Limited risk—transparency obligations. AI-generated content must be labeled as such. Chatbots must disclose that they're not human. Also sensible in principle.

Minimal risk—no special requirements. This covers the majority of AI applications, from spam filters to recommendation algorithms.

So much for theory. Practice looks different. The AI Act regulates a technology that Europe doesn't control. The foundation models come from the US and China. It's as if Europe were writing traffic rules for highways but building no cars.

At the same time, compliance costs disproportionately hit small and medium-sized businesses. For OpenAI, €200,000 in annual costs is pocket change. For a European AI startup with 20 employees, it's existentially threatening. And the definitions are so broad that the question "When is an AI system high risk?" will occupy courts for years—with the result that cautious companies will forgo AI rather than take the regulatory risk.

### The Casualties of the AI Act: Europe's AI Exodus

The problem with the AI Act isn't theoretical. I can show it with concrete cases.

Aleph Alpha^[169]^ was Germany's great AI hope. A startup from Heidelberg building its own large language model. In 2023, it raised $500 million, funded by Bosch, SAP, and the Schwarz Group. A year later, the dream was over. In September 2024, Aleph Alpha announced it was abandoning the development of its own language models and pivoting to AI consulting. Not because of the AI Act alone—that would be too simple. But the AI Act didn't make it easier. When a startup with 150 employees has to sink €200,000 per year into compliance instead of engineers, that's not paperwork. That's a lost competitive advantage.

Mistral AI from Paris survived—but only through massive political lobbying. Mistral's key lobbyist, former Secretary of State Cédric O, used his direct line to Macron to rally France, Germany, and Italy into a united front against the strictest rules for generative AI. It worked—the rules were watered down. But the irony is glaring: Europe's flagship AI startup survived the European AI Act not through innovation but through political influence. That's exactly the system the AI Act supposedly aims to fight.

The numbers are sobering. In 2025, roughly $215 billion in AI venture capital flowed into the US. In Europe, it was $37 billion.^[165]^ Ratio: nearly 6 to 1. A quarter of Europe's most promising AI startups are considering moving to the US, according to a Sifted survey. Forty-six CEOs of Europe's largest companies demanded in July 2025 that implementation of the AI Act be paused for two years.^[166]^ The EU Commission initially refused. In November, it caved and postponed the high-risk rules to 2027.


Europe created a regulatory framework with the AI Act that burdens its own industry more than foreign competition. An AI startup in San Francisco faces no comparable regulation. An AI startup in Munich does. The result is predictable: innovation migrates to where it's less impeded.

### USA: From Light Touch to Laissez-Faire

The American regulatory landscape is the polar opposite.

President Biden issued an executive order on AI in 2023 that emphasized transparency, safety, and responsible use but created no binding regulations. The order relied on voluntary commitments from the tech industry and directed federal agencies to develop guidelines.

Under the subsequent Trump administration, even this tentative approach was largely rolled back. The new line: AI is a competitive advantage the US must not jeopardize through regulation. Regulation should intervene only where concrete, demonstrable harm has occurred—not preemptively.


The result: the US has de facto the least regulated AI landscape among major economic powers. For innovators, that's a blessing. For the societal debate about AI safety and ethics, it's a risk. For investors, it's clear: US companies operate with fewer regulatory constraints than their European competitors, which translates into higher innovation speed and lower compliance costs.

But "lightly regulated" doesn't mean "free from pressure." In the spring of 2026, the US government showed it's perfectly willing to discipline AI companies—just not through regulation. Through contracts.

Anthropic, the company behind Claude and by its own account the most safety-conscious player in the AI market, refused to cooperate with the Pentagon on autonomous weapons systems and mass surveillance. CEO Dario Amodei had drawn red lines—publicly and unequivocally. The response came via executive order: Anthropic was classified as a "National Security Supply Chain Risk," a category previously reserved exclusively for foreign companies. On the same day, OpenAI signed a comprehensive Pentagon contract.

The message to the AI industry was unmistakable: cooperation with the state isn't an invitation. It's an expectation. Say yes, and you get contracts. Say no, and you're declared a risk.

That's not classical regulation. It's subtler and in some respects more effective. No law was passed, no paragraph written. But every CEO of an AI company understood where the lines are. The US doesn't regulate AI the way Europe does—through compliance forms. It regulates through market power, through access to contracts, through the question of who's in and who's out.

---


### China: Regulation as Industrial Policy

China pursues a third path that corresponds to neither the European regulatory approach nor the American laissez-faire: regulation as industrial policy.

The Chinese government regulates AI, but selectively and strategically. It bans what's politically undesirable—AI-generated content criticizing the regime, deepfakes of political figures, algorithms that foster social instability—and aggressively promotes what's economically and militarily useful.

China's "New Generation AI Development Plan" sets the explicit goal of becoming the world's leading AI nation by 2030. The means: billions in state subsidies, preferential access to data (China has 1.4 billion people and far more lax data protection laws than Europe), targeted promotion of AI applications in key industries, and a state-orchestrated ecosystem of universities, companies, and military research.

The result: China regulates more than the US but differently than Europe. The regulation doesn't slow down—it steers. It doesn't prevent innovation—it channels it in directions the state considers useful.

---

### Crypto Regulation: MiCA, SEC, and the Battle for the Future of Money

In the crypto space, the regulatory landscape is even more fragmented.

Europe, with MiCA (Markets in Crypto-Assets Regulation), created the world's first comprehensive regulatory framework for cryptocurrencies. MiCA establishes legal certainty for crypto companies, which is fundamentally positive. It defines what a crypto asset is, how to obtain a license, what reserve requirements apply to stablecoins. For an ecosystem that previously operated in a regulatory gray zone, that's progress.

The flip side: MiCA is conservative and restrictive. Requirements for stablecoin issuers are strict. KYC obligations for crypto exchanges go beyond what traditional banks must provide for comparable services. And the regulation of DeFi—decentralized finance protocols with no central operator—is an open question MiCA doesn't answer.

In the US, the SEC under Gary Gensler took an aggressive anti-crypto stance and filed numerous lawsuits against crypto companies. The approval of Bitcoin spot ETFs in early 2024 was a turning point—forced by a court ruling against Gensler's stated wishes. Since then, hundreds of billions of dollars have flowed into Bitcoin ETFs, and the regulatory mood has shifted. The current administration is significantly more crypto-friendly.

For the machine-to-machine economy this book describes, the regulatory question is existential: Will machines be allowed to autonomously execute crypto transactions? Who's liable when an AI agent enters a smart contract? Does an AI agent need KYC verification? These questions are unanswered. And their answers will determine the speed at which the machine economy grows.

The Robot Tax: Who Taxes the Machines? In February 2017, Bill Gates said something in an interview^[170]^ that startled the world: "If a human worker in a factory creates $50,000 worth of value and is taxed on it, then a robot doing the same work should be taxed at a similar level."

The reactions were predictable. Lawrence Summers,^[171]^ former US Treasury Secretary and Harvard economist, called the proposal "profoundly misguided." The tech industry argued a robot tax would stifle innovation. Economists asked: What exactly is a robot? Is a spreadsheet a robot? A chatbot? An AI agent that does bookkeeping?

Robert Shiller, Nobel laureate in economics, took a more nuanced view.^[172]^ He wrote a few weeks after Gates that a moderate, possibly temporary robot tax would be "a natural and obvious component of any policy to combat growing inequality."

The European Parliament debated the question the same month. Luxembourgish MEP Mady Delvaux had submitted a report proposing a robot tax and basic income. The overall report was adopted by 396 to 123 votes. But the robot tax paragraph was struck by amendment. The center-right coalition of ALDE, EPP, and ECR rejected it. Delvaux expressed disappointment: the conservatives refused to "consider the possible negative effects on the labor market."

Then South Korea went a step further. In 2018, it became the first country in the world to implement a form of robot tax.^[173]^ Not as a direct levy but by reducing tax incentives for automation investments. Large companies that previously received 7 percent tax savings on automation investments got only 3 percent. The effect was measurable: a study by the Information Technology & Innovation Foundation from February 2026 found that affected industries cut robot installations by 28 percent.^[174]^ Less automation. More jobs. But also: less productivity gains.

That's the dilemma. A robot tax slows automation. That protects jobs short-term. Long-term, it weakens competitiveness against countries without such a tax. South Korea has the highest robot density in the world. It could afford this experiment. Europe or the US, which already lag behind China and South Korea in automation, would have less room to maneuver.

The debate has evolved since then. Bernie Sanders demanded a robot tax on large companies using AI to replace jobs in October 2025. The Brookings Institution published a framework in January 2026 modeling various approaches: robot tax, compute tax on GPU hours, token tax on AI-generated outputs, digital tax on AI-as-a-Service revenues. The academic discussion has shifted from "robot tax yes or no" to "what kind of taxation for what kind of AI."

For the thesis of this book, the question is central. When machines increasingly take over value creation, when AI agents execute tasks and robots work in factories, the payroll tax shrinks as the funding base of the welfare state. At some point, society must find an answer for how to tax the machine economy. Not whether—how. The only question is whether that answer is smart and competitive, or whether it stifles innovation the way the AI Act threatens to.

But taxing machines doesn't change the underlying dynamic.

Whether the robot is taxed or not, it still replaces the work. The question is only who gets the tax revenue and who distributes it.

And here's the problem: whoever pins their hopes on government redistribution is betting on a game they don't control. Governments change. Taxes change. Programs change.

What doesn't change: ownership.

Whoever owns the machines sits on the right side—regardless of tax policy.

Who benefits from regulation? Here's the irony many miss: regulation isn't automatically bad for the regulated industry. Often it's the opposite.

Regulation creates compliance costs. Compliance costs are fixed costs—they apply regardless of company size. An AI startup with 10 employees and a tech giant with 100,000 employees must meet the same documentation requirements, undergo the same audits, file the same reports. For the giant, that's peanuts. For the startup, it's a third of the budget.

The result: regulation raises barriers to entry. It protects incumbents from challengers. It cements existing market positions. NVIDIA, Microsoft, Google, Amazon—they can all absorb the EU AI Act and MiCA without flinching. Their compliance departments are larger than most European AI startups.

That's not a conspiracy theory. It's the economic logic of regulation, known since George Stigler's^[247]^ "Theory of Regulation" in the 1970s: regulation is often welcomed and shaped by the very companies it supposedly constrains—because it protects their market position.

### Autonomous Weapons: The Elephant in the Elephant's Room

There's a topic that gets surprisingly little airtime in the AI debate, even though it's the most urgent of all: AI-controlled weapons.

The Ukraine war is the first large-scale laboratory for autonomous warfare. Ukraine procured thousands of FPV drones with machine vision and AI targeting in 2024. In 2025, mass production began of the Vyriy-10, a drone with an AI navigation module that continues flying autonomously when Russia jams the GPS signal. The cost: $500 to $2,000 per drone, versus $2 to $10 million for the tank it destroys. That cost ratio makes traditional military spending questionable.

Russia responds with the Lancet-3, a loitering munition with autonomous target recognition running on NVIDIA processors. China is experimenting with drone swarms and has unveiled a military vehicle that provides autonomous combat support based on DeepSeek. The US runs Project Maven, which aims to deliver "100 percent machine-generated intelligence" to combat commanders by June 2026. Palantir holds a contract worth over a billion dollars for it. The Pentagon's Replicator initiative targets mass production of autonomous drones by the thousands to counter China's numerical advantage.

The defense-AI industry is growing faster than almost any other sector. Palantir is worth over $160 billion—a 330 percent stock price increase in one year.^[176]^ Anduril, founded by former Oculus founder Palmer Luckey, was valued at $30.5 billion in June 2025.^[177]^ Peter Thiel's Founders Fund invested a billion dollars in a single round—the largest single check in the fund's history. Shield AI, whose drones are already deployed in Ukraine and with the US Marines, stands at $5.6 billion.^[178]^


::: {.infobox}
### Lavender — AI Selects Targets in Gaza

The most disturbing case isn't Ukraine—it's Gaza. In April 2024, the Israeli magazine +972 revealed a system called Lavender.^[175]^ An AI database that marked 37,000 Palestinians as potential targets. According to sources within the Israeli military, Lavender's output was "treated practically as a human decision." Review time per target: about 20 seconds. A second system called "Where's Daddy?" located the marked individuals and suggested strikes when they were home at night—with their families. UN human rights experts condemned the use. Human Rights Watch documented the cases.

Lavender is the first documented case in which AI was used at scale for target selection in an armed conflict. It raises a question no legal system in the world has answered: Who is responsible when an AI marks a wrong target and a civilian dies? The programmer? The commander who spent 20 seconds looking at a recommendation? The state? The AI itself has no legal personhood. It can't be punished. The result is an accountability gap that widens with every generation of more autonomous systems.

:::


The UN General Assembly voted 166 to 3 in December 2024 for a resolution on autonomous weapons systems. Only Russia, North Korea, and Belarus voted against. Ukraine abstained—it uses the systems itself. But a resolution isn't a ban. Since 2014, the UN has been negotiating a binding agreement—the so-called LAWS negotiations. So far without result. Individual states are blocking. In the meantime, the arms race accelerates.

This has consequences for the thesis of this book. The convergence of AI and robotics doesn't just affect factories and logistics centers. It affects battlefields. The technologies that enable a humanoid robot to sort packages in a warehouse also enable it to operate on a battlefield. The difference lies in the software, not the hardware. And software is copyable.

The speed of autonomous decisions makes human control increasingly impractical. An AI reacts in milliseconds; a human in seconds. In a drone swarm attack, there's no time for a call to the commanding officer. That means: the more autonomous the systems become, the less control humans have over their decisions. And the less control humans have, the wider the accountability gap.

There's no easy solution. A ban on autonomous weapons is desirable but unrealistic as long as the US, China, and Russia refuse. What remains is the uncomfortable recognition that the AI revolution—whose investment opportunities this book describes—also has a military dimension I'd rather not have but can't ignore.

For investors, the conclusion is clear: in a regulated environment, the big win. NVIDIA, Microsoft, Google are not just technological winners—they're regulatory winners. This strengthens the investment thesis for the infrastructure heavyweights in the safe core of the portfolio we'll build in Part IV.

What can European investors do concretely? The regulatory landscape sounds discouraging for Europeans. But it's no reason to sit on your hands. There are concrete paths to act despite MiCA and the AI Act.

Bitcoin and Ethereum ETPs are tradable in Europe on regulated exchanges. CoinShares, VanEck, and 21Shares offer products listed on the Deutsche Börse. That's not a regulatory loophole—that's the intended path. MiCA complicates the direct purchase of certain stablecoins, but it doesn't prohibit investing in the infrastructure of the machine economy.

For crypto investments beyond Bitcoin and Ethereum, there are regulated exchanges like Bitstamp or Kraken that hold or have applied for MiCA licenses. Decentralized protocols like Uniswap or Aave are technically accessible from anywhere in the world, but Europeans should take their tax documentation obligations seriously.

For AI stocks, there are no Europe-specific restrictions. NVIDIA, Microsoft, Alphabet, and all the other companies mentioned in this book are tradable through any German brokerage account. The regulatory asymmetry that disadvantages European startups is actually an argument for European investors: invest where the innovation happens, not where it's regulated.

With that, we've surveyed the landscape. The technology is real. Geopolitics sharpens the race. Energy limits the pace. Regulation determines who plays and who watches. And the human cost will be high—higher than most suspect today.

But the landscape also shows: the transformation can no longer be stopped. Not by regulation, not by energy scarcity, not by societal resistance. The only questions left are how fast it comes. And who stands on which side.
