In the next part, we leave the present and look to the future. What's the most likely scenario for the next ten years? How will we interact with machines when the keyboard disappears? And the most uncomfortable question of all: who actually decides what's right when machines can do everything?


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ The EU AI Act constrains European AI innovation.
>
> ◆ The robot tax debate misses the point.
>
> ◆ Regulation can slow the transformation but cannot stop it.


# PART III: THE FUTURE

## Chapter 12: The Most Likely Scenario: 2026–2035


---

> *"Heavier-than-air flying machines are impossible."*
>
> — Lord Kelvin, 1895

---


![Scenario 2026–2035 — The three phases of the machine economy](images-en/abb-19.png)


Predictions are a thankless business. History is full of experts who were spectacularly wrong — from Lord Kelvin's "heavier-than-air flying machines are impossible" to Steve Ballmer's "There's no chance that the iPhone is going to get any significant market share." Anyone who predicts the future will almost always be wrong, at least in the details.

And yet it's necessary. Not to deliver a precise forecast that can be graded in ten years, but to create a framework for thinking that informs investment decisions. What follows is not the future. It's the most likely scenario, based on what's already in motion today.


---


::: {.infobox}

### 2026–2028: The Silent Revolution
AI agents become standard in knowledge work. Not in all companies, but in all companies that want to survive. Every major company has AI systems autonomously answering emails, creating reports, analyzing data, writing code, and preparing decisions.

I experience this myself. In early 2026, I discovered an open-source project called OpenClaw. It had been published just two days earlier, and I already had my first AI assistant up and running. I named her Jeannie, after the genie from the bottle.

OpenClaw reached 200,000 GitHub stars within weeks — the fastest-growing open-source AI project in history.

The difference: you give your agent a personality through a so-called Soul file. Your relationship with your AI belongs to you, not to the platform.

Since then, my daily routine has fundamentally changed: she scours my emails, handles outreach, reviews contracts, files invoices, and delivers market and competitive reports. This isn't a future vision. This is my Tuesday.

When I wrote: "Every change is discussed with the Master first. He decides who I become," I was thinking about a new kind of partnership between human and machine.


The first humanoid robots work in controlled environments. Tesla's factories, Amazon warehouses, Japanese care homes. They don't do complex tasks — they sort, transport, stack. But they work. And they get better every month.

The unemployment rate in certain sectors rises noticeably. Call centers, basic accounting, data processing, standard legal advice. At the same time, new professions emerge around AI management and robot supervision. The net balance is unclear, but the fear is real and politically palpable.

Stablecoins gain recognition from first governments as legal payment methods for digital transactions. Bitcoin permanently exceeds the $200,000 mark, driven by institutional adoption and growing use as a reserve asset. The numbers behind the silent revolution. What I see in my own companies matches what the major analysts are measuring. The first "person" we "hired" for development work isn't a person. It's an AI. And we're not an outlier. Microsoft already writes 30 percent of its code with AI.^[180]^ Not in five years. Now.


The real revolution hides in the costs. Sam Altman announced that GPT-5, the current top model, will drop 100x in price within a year. From $10 per million tokens to 10 cents. What comes after that will drop even further.

Emad Mostaque, the former CEO of Stability AI, ran a calculation that hasn't left my mind since: the average person speaks roughly 10 million tokens per year. Every word you say in a year, every conversation, every thought you voice. At 10 cents per million tokens, that costs one dollar. One dollar for all your words. And if you're generous — 100 million tokens for every thought a person has in a year — you're at ten dollars. Ten dollars for all your thoughts. That's not a future projection. Those are the prices that will apply in 12 months.


But the cost implosion is only half the story. Efficiency is exploding upward at the same time.

At Cursor, the AI-powered code editor, they ran an experiment: have agents build a complete browser from scratch. Three million lines of code. That used to cost a team of developers years. The agents needed three billion tokens. That sounds like a lot — until you do the math: at current prices, that's about $30,000. Less than a junior developer's annual salary in Germany. A complete browser, built by machines, for the cost of half an employee. And that ratio will keep collapsing. Models get more efficient. Prompts get shorter. The $30,000 becomes $3,000, becomes $300.

This is the double exponential that almost nobody grasps. Intelligence is rising exponentially. Costs are falling exponentially at the same time. This isn't Moore's Law. This is Moore's Law squared.


For those who find that abstract, here's the practical translation: Andrej Karpathy, one of the most respected AI researchers in the world, co-founder of OpenAI, former Head of AI at Tesla, said in November 2025 that he wrote 20 percent of his code with AI. By January 2026 it was 80 percent. Today he says: "I barely even look at the code anymore." This is one of the best programmers in the world. If he doesn't need to code anymore, how long before that applies to everyone?


McKinsey Global Institute calculated in November 2025 that AI and robotics could theoretically automate 57 percent of all US work hours today.^[181]^ Goldman Sachs projects that 6 to 7 percent of all US jobs will be directly displaced — up to 300 million worldwide.^[182]^ The World Economic Forum expects 92 million displaced jobs by 2030, alongside 170 million new ones. A net gain of 78 million. But the displaced 92 million and those who get the 170 million new jobs — those aren't the same people.


The layoff wave has begun. It's measurable. Challenger, Gray & Christmas counted nearly 55,000 layoffs in the US for 2025 directly attributed to AI.^[183]^ Amazon cut 14,000 corporate positions^[184]^ — the CEO cited AI-driven efficiency gains. Microsoft laid off 6,000 programmers. Salesforce reduced its customer support from 9,000 to 5,000 employees through so-called Agentic AI. Klarna, the Swedish payment provider, had its AI assistant take over the work of 700 full-time employees and handle 75 percent of all customer chats.


The hardest hit aren't who you'd expect. It's the young. Goldman Sachs published a finding in August 2025^[185]^ that didn't surprise me but did alarm me: unemployment among 20- to 30-year-olds in AI-exposed occupations has risen by nearly 3 percentage points since early 2025. Significantly more than among peers in other professions. Entry-level job postings in software in the US have collapsed by 67 percent. In the UK, tech graduate positions dropped 46 percent in 2024^[186]^, with projections for another 53 percent decline by 2026. Anthropic itself — the company behind Claude — published its own labor market study in early 2026 and confirmed the pattern: young workers between 22 and 25 are already being hired 14 percent less often in AI-exposed professions, while older workers show no measurable effect yet. When even the AI developers see the canary-in-the-coal-mine signal in their own data, nobody should claim this is just a temporary dip.

I see this in my own companies and with our clients. Only people with top-tier training — with the ability to use AI as a tool rather than compete against it — will find their place long-term. Career starters doing routine tasks that an AI handles faster, cheaper, and more reliably have a problem. And that's a problem for society as a whole, because a generation that can't find entry-level positions despite a college degree won't react kindly.

In parallel, a DIY economy is emerging where customers replace specialists. Canva has evolved from a drag-and-drop app into an AI-native creative platform. Anyone can now create designs that required an agency two years ago. LegalZoom and AI-based legal tools handle company formations, contracts, and trademark filings without a lawyer. TurboTax and over a dozen AI tax tools automate tax returns. ChatGPT, Claude, Gemini replace copywriters, translators, researchers. GitHub Copilot writes code that used to go to a junior developer.

Ad agencies, software development firms, accountants for routine work, lawyers for standard cases: none of these industries will vanish, but they'll shrink. Dramatically. How fast? Faster than most people think.


### What Happens to the Office Market

One of the most immediate and least discussed consequences of the AI revolution is playing out in real estate.

San Francisco's office vacancy rate is at 27.7 percent^[187]^. Before the pandemic, it was 8.6 percent. Manhattan is at 22.3 percent — more than double the pre-pandemic average. Nationally, US offices hit a historic vacancy rate of 20.5 percent by the end of 2025.


The pandemic started the process. AI is accelerating it. When a company writes 30 percent of its code with AI, it needs fewer programmers. When it automates 75 percent of customer support, it needs fewer call centers. Fewer employees need less office space. The math is simple.

In February 2026, national office^[188]^ REITs (Real Estate Investment Trusts) lost 16 percent of their value in a single week, triggered by fears that AI will continue to suppress office demand long-term. SL Green, Boston Properties, Vornado — all under pressure. San Francisco, Manhattan, Chicago, Los Angeles, Washington D.C., and Seattle are the hardest hit.


The response is pragmatic: offices are being converted to apartments. 71,000 new residential units from office conversions are planned in the US.^[189]^ New York passed tax incentives and zoning reforms to accelerate the process. Chicago leads in total conversion volume. Entire neighborhoods are changing their character.


### What Happens to the Software Industry

Office real estate is just the visible part. The invisible — but potentially bigger — collapse is happening in the software industry.


The entire SaaS business model is built on switching costs. Dropbox, Salesforce, Adobe — they've relied for years on the fact that it's too much hassle to switch to a competitor. Your files, your workflows, your habits — all locked in their systems. The lock-in is the moat.

*Agents eliminate switching costs overnight.*

A practical example: my music is on Apple Music. Playlists, likes, years of listening history. Switching to Spotify would cost me an entire Saturday — and I'd still lose things. With an AI agent, I say: "Migrate my entire music library to Spotify, keep all likes, all playlists." And tomorrow morning it's done. The same agent can move my files from Dropbox to Google Drive. My contacts from one CRM to another. My emails from Outlook to Gmail. The migration that used to keep me from even thinking about switching — the agent handles it while I sleep.


*When switching costs are zero, every moat built on them collapses.*

But this doesn't just affect software. Think about advertising. Meta and Google base their entire business model on CPM — cost per thousand impressions. That works because humans see ads. Agents don't see ads. An agent researching the internet for you doesn't click on banners. It doesn't buy impulsively because it liked an ad. It goes straight to the best deal, no detour through influencers or sponsored posts. Emad Mostaque predicts that global ad spending will peak next year. Not because the economy is weakening, but because agents are taking over the interface between humans and the internet.

Hedge funds and trading are the next casualty. AI is already among the best super-forecasters in the world. Prediction markets are increasingly dominated by algorithms. If AI predicts better than humans, it's also better at trading. Hedge fund managers, traders, analysts — they're all competing against machines that never tire, never trade on emotion, and never play against their own strategy.

Everything on the other side of a screen, a GPU can do better. The question isn't whether, but when. And the when is closer than most believe.


For investors, this means: office real estate is no longer a safe haven. It's an asset under structural pressure. Anyone invested in REITs should favor logistics and data center properties. Ironically, the technology making offices obsolete needs enormous physical infrastructure itself: data centers, power grids, water supply. Demand is shifting, not disappearing.


:::


---


### 2028–2031: The Tipping Point

AI handles 30 to 50 percent of today's knowledge work faster, cheaper, and often better than humans. That's not an estimate — it's the extrapolation of the productivity curves we're already seeing.

Humanoid robots cost under $30,000 and are produced in quantities of hundreds of thousands. They work in logistics centers, on construction sites, in agriculture, and in elder care. China leads in robot mass production, but the AI software controlling them comes largely from the US.

The economy splits. Companies that adopt AI and robotics see productivity jumps of 200 to 300 percent. Companies that don't, disappear. Not in a dramatic crash, but in a quiet extinction: they can no longer compete and lose customers, employees, investors.

Governments worldwide grapple with how to manage an economy where traditional wage labor is shrinking. The first countries experiment with universal basic income, funded by productivity gains and robot taxes. The political debate is heated, polarized, and cuts across traditional left-right lines.

The machine-to-machine economy on blockchain reaches annual transaction volumes in the trillions. AI agents buy and sell compute time, data, services — all autonomously, all on crypto rails. 2031–2035: The New Normal. AI and robots are as taken for granted as the internet today. Nobody talks about whether AI is "taking over the work" anymore, just as nobody talks about whether the internet "destroyed retail" anymore. It did — and created something new at the same time.

Economic output per capita rises dramatically. But distribution is more unequal than ever. Those who own the infrastructure of this new world — the chips, the models, the robots, the crypto protocols — are among the wealthiest people and institutions in history. Cryptocurrencies have established themselves as the infrastructure layer of the autonomous economy. Not as a replacement for government money, but as a parallel system for machine transactions, decentralized finance, and cross-border value transfer.


---

### Three Scenarios Instead of One
That was the base case. But investors also need extreme scenarios to stress-test their portfolios.
When Robots Learn to Walk
Robotics is the area where the future becomes most tangibly real.
Tesla announced in January 2026 that Optimus Gen 3 — the first mass-production-ready version of its humanoid robot — would be unveiled in the first quarter of 2026. Over 1,000 units are already working in Tesla factories. The plan: 10,000 to 500,000 units per year, depending on demand. Sales to external companies no earlier than 2026.

Figure AI tested its Figure 02 robot at BMW's Spartanburg plant for eleven months. The robots worked 10-hour shifts, picking parts from logistics containers and placing them on welding fixtures. Eleven months, not eleven days. This is no longer a lab experiment.
1X Technologies from Norway opened preorders for NEO in October 2025, marketed as the world's first consumer robot. Price: $20,000 or $499 per month. First deliveries in 2026. Designed for household tasks: washing dishes, laundry, tidying up. Whether it can do all that reliably remains to be seen. But the fact that a humanoid household robot is being offered for $20,000 — not $200,000 — is the real point.
Aurora Innovation launched commercial driverless trucking operations in Texas in May 2025.^[190]^ Dallas to Houston, without a human driver. Over 100,000 driverless miles without a safety incident. In February 2026, Aurora trucks completed the 1,000-mile route in 15 hours — faster than human drivers are legally allowed, because machines don't need rest breaks. Waymo operates robotaxis in 10 US cities with over 400,000 trips per week^[191]^ and plans to expand to 20 cities by the end of 2026, including London and Tokyo.

And then the question my wife asks: when does the plumber robot arrive? The honest answer: not soon. Skilled trades are the hardest to automate. Every house is different, every construction site is unstructured, every burst pipe is unique. WIRED headlined in January 2026:^[192]^ "The Real AI Talent War Is for Plumbers and Electricians." AI data centers need plumbers and electricians so desperately that there are massive shortages. AI is creating demand here, not automation. Fully autonomous trade robots are realistic no earlier than 2035, probably later.
The road is long. From the truck-loading robot to the warehouse worker to the household robot to the plumber — each step is a leap in complexity. But each step will be taken. That robots will take over our physical labor is beyond doubt. What's open is the sequence. And the sequence is: structured environments first, unstructured environments last. Factories before warehouses, warehouses before households, households before construction sites.
Ultimately, it's hard to imagine which tasks will even remain for humans. Not impossible — hard to imagine. And that's precisely what makes it so important to think about it now.

In the bull case, the development runs faster than described. AGI is achieved before 2030. Productivity gains exceed all estimates. Bitcoin reaches seven-figure prices as a global reserve asset. NVIDIA triples its revenue yet again. Robots are in every warehouse by 2030. The M2M economy becomes a trillion-dollar market.

In the bear case, there's a setback. A major AI model causes a spectacular failure with real-world consequences — loss of trust, tighter regulation. Robotics stagnates at technical hurdles; humanoid robots remain expensive and unreliable. Crypto is severely restricted in major jurisdictions. The societal backlash leads to anti-technology politics. But even in this scenario, the development doesn't revert to zero. It slows down.
Mostaque frames it differently in "The Last Economy." He doesn't talk about bull and bear, but about three "attractor states" into which society will crystallize: "Digital Feudalism" as the default path, where a handful of corporations own the AI models and everyone else lives in comfortable, optimized cages — sustained by a basic income that's just enough to keep people from revolting. "The Great Fragmentation" as the fear path, where nations panic-build their own AI ecosystems and walled-off internets — an algorithmic Cold War. And "Human Symbiosis" as the wisdom path, where open access to intelligence, new monetary architectures, and human-led governance enable a genuine partnership between human and machine. "These are not predictions but attractor states in the landscape of possibility," Mostaque writes. The scenarios aren't mutually exclusive — different regions could fall into different attractors, and the path isn't locked in yet.

That's the asymmetry Peter Thiel would describe: in the bull case, the gains are enormous. In the bear case, the losses are limited (the technology exists and will be used, just more slowly). The probability is tilted toward the base case and bull case. That's the foundation for the barbell strategy we'll develop in Part IV.

---


::: {.infobox}
### What Kurzweil Got Right and Wrong

A word on calibration. Ray Kurzweil has made 147 predictions^[193]^ over the years. Roughly 86 percent of them were correct or essentially correct by his own analysis. He was right about the spread of the mobile internet, about the development of self-driving cars (albeit with delays), about speech recognition, about genome sequencing.

Where he missed was the speed of implementation. Technologies often took longer to gain traction than his models predicted. Not because the technical development was slower, but because regulatory, social, and economic inertia slowed adoption.

That's the most important lesson for investors: the direction is almost always right. The timing almost never is. That's why an investment strategy based on precise timing is doomed to fail. And that's why a strategy based on long-term trends that can withstand short-term volatility is the only one that works.

The next long-term trends, however, could surpass anything we've seen so far. Because the merger of human and machine has only just begun.
:::


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ 2026–2028: Silent Revolution — AI agents cut costs by 90 percent.
>
> ◆ 2028–2031: The Tipping Point — physical robotics reaches mass production.
>
> ◆ 2031–2035: New Normal — the autonomous economy is reality.

## Chapter 13: The Next Interface — From Keyboard to Thought


---

> *"Any sufficiently advanced technology is indistinguishable from magic."*
>
> — Arthur C. Clarke

---


![From keyboard to thought — Interface evolution](images-en/abb-20.png)


Every technological revolution came with a revolution in how humans interact with machines. The mainframe demanded punch cards. The personal computer brought keyboard and mouse. The smartphone introduced the touchscreen. Each transition increased bandwidth — the amount of information a human could feed into a machine per unit of time — and reduced friction. The more natural the interface, the faster the adoption.

We're now approaching the next two transitions in rapid succession. And the second one won't just change how we work — it will change how we experience reality.

### Voice as a Transitional Interface

The first transition is already underway. Tools like Wispr Flow let you control your entire computer through natural speech. Not clunky voice commands, but fluid, contextual language the system actually understands. You don't say "Open application Mail, new message, recipient colon." You say: "Write Sarah an email that I'll be late for dinner."

This works because large language models now don't just transcribe words — they understand meaning, context, and nuance. Google's Gemini Live API already demonstrates bidirectional real-time communication with AI: you speak, the AI responds in a natural voice, and it can simultaneously process video streams. It sees what you see, hears what you hear, reacts in the moment.

Speech is the most natural form of human communication. It's what we're evolutionarily built for. The keyboard was always a workaround — a translation layer between thought and machine. Voice removes that layer. And adoption will be fast: people don't need to learn a new skill to speak. They just need to drop the habit of typing.

But speech has a limit: bandwidth. Humans speak at roughly 150 words per minute. We think orders of magnitude faster. Conscious thought processes about 50 bits of information per second; the unconscious brain processes an estimated 11 million bits per second.^[203]^ Speech captures a fraction of cognitive output. It's a straw through which we're trying to drink an ocean.


---

### Brain-Computer Interfaces: State of the Art
This is where Brain-Computer Interfaces enter the picture. A BCI reads neural signals directly, bypassing the slow, lossy translation through vocal cords, tongue, and lips. Theoretically, a sufficiently advanced BCI could transmit the full bandwidth of conscious thought to a machine. Practically, we're already further along than most people realize.
Neuralink has implanted^[195]^ its "Telepathy" device in 21 human patients to date (as of early 2026). These patients — many of them paralyzed — can control computers, type messages, and operate robotic arms using nothing but their thoughts. The system achieves typing speeds of 40 words per minute^[196]^ — nearly as fast as an average smartphone user. The PRIME study is still ongoing, with primary completion planned for 2026 and full results expected by 2031. Neuralink has raised over $650 million at a $9.7 billion valuation^[197]^ and is scaling production for next-generation implants.

But Neuralink isn't alone. Synchron raised $200 million in a Series^[198]^ D for its Stentrode system — a BCI implanted through a blood vessel in the brain, without open-skull surgery. Synchron has partnerships with Apple and NVIDIA and is preparing 2026 clinical trials that could make it the first commercially scalable implanted BCI. Paradromics has demonstrated information transfer rates exceeding 200 bits per second^[199]^ — higher than the linguistic information content of human speech — with system latency of just 11 milliseconds.

And then the move that reveals where the smart money is looking: in January 2026, OpenAI led a $250 million^[200]^ seed round in Merge Labs, Sam Altman's Brain-Computer Interface startup, at an $850 million valuation. OpenAI's stated goal: "to connect biological and artificial intelligence to maximize human capability, agency, and experience." When the company building the world's most advanced AI invests a quarter billion dollars in brain interfaces, the strategic direction is unmistakable.
The BCI market is projected to grow at an annual rate^[201]^ exceeding 15 percent, with the US market alone potentially reaching $3 billion by 2035. Total BCI funding tripled to $867 million in 2025. This is early infrastructure investment — the equivalent of laying fiber optic cables in 1995.

---


### AI-Generated Worlds: When Machines Build Reality

Now comes the part almost nobody has on their radar.

Google DeepMind's Genie 3, released in August 2025, can generate interactive 3D environments from a simple text prompt^[202]^ — navigable in real-time at 24 frames per second in 720p resolution. You describe a world, and the AI builds it around you. Not a pre-rendered scene, but a dynamically generated, physics-aware environment that reacts to the user's actions. World Labs' Marble model goes further, creating navigable 3D scenes from text, images, video, or sketches.

These aren't video games in the traditional sense. There's no developer, no studio, no years of production. The AI is simultaneously engine, artist, world builder, and game master. And the technology improves at the speed of large language models — exponentially.


---

### The Convergence: BCI + AI Worlds = Synthetic Reality
Instead of typing a prompt or speaking a description, you think a world — and it materializes. Instead of viewing it on a screen, the visual and sensory information is streamed directly to the neural cortex. Instead of pressing buttons to interact, your intentions are read and executed in real time.
What you get is not Virtual Reality as we know it today — clunky headsets with visible pixels and noticeable latency. What you get is a synthetic experience that, from the brain's perspective, is indistinguishable from reality. Every sense engaged. Every interaction natural. Every world custom-tailored to the individual's desires. Addiction Potential and Superstimulus Here the thesis takes a darker — and for investors, critically important — turn.
The AI revolution described in earlier chapters will, by all credible projections, eliminate or transform tens of millions of jobs. When AI agents take over knowledge work and humanoid robots handle physical labor, a large portion of the global workforce will find itself without purpose, without income, and without the structure that employment provides.
Governments will respond with some form of universal basic income. This book already covers that in the 2028–2031 scenario. But money without purpose creates a vacuum. And nature abhors vacuums.
Brain-Computer Interfaces combined with AI-generated worlds will fill that vacuum.
Consider the trajectory: today, smartphone addiction affects an estimated 6.3 percent^[204]^ of the world's population — roughly 500 million people. Caused by small, flat screens delivering two-dimensional content that merely approximates social interaction or entertainment. Research published in Frontiers in Virtual Reality shows that immersive VR experiences generate significantly stronger psychological rewards and higher addiction potential. Between 2 and 20 percent of current VR users already exhibit problematic usage patterns.^[205]^
Now extrapolate: a BCI-driven, AI-generated world indistinguishable from reality, where you can be anyone, do anything, experience any sensation. That's not an incremental improvement over a smartphone. That's a category shift. In neuroscience terms: a superstimulus — a stimulus that exceeds anything the brain encountered during evolution, hijacking the reward system with unprecedented efficiency. The historical parallel isn't social media. It's closer to the introduction of refined sugar or opiates: substances that exploit neural reward pathways far more effectively than the natural stimuli the brain was built for.

### 

### The Hardware Players: Who's Building the Bridge to Thought?


The convergence of BCI and AI-generated worlds raises a question rarely asked: what hardware does all of this run on?

Apple released the Vision Pro — a mixed-reality headset at $3,500. Sales have been modest, and even Apple admits it. But the Vision Pro isn't a product. It's an infrastructure investment. Apple is using it to test manufacturing processes, the software platform, and use cases for what comes next.
And what comes next is revealed by Apple's partnership with Synchron. Synchron — the BCI company that implants its device through a blood vessel into the brain without open-skull surgery — has NVIDIA and Apple as partners. Apple has not publicly commented on Brain-Computer Interfaces, and that's telling. Apple doesn't talk about things it's not working on. It doesn't talk about things it is working on.
The strategic logic is obvious: Apple defined the transition from keyboard to touchscreen (iPhone). If the next transition leads from screen to thought, Apple wants to define that one too. The two billion active^[33]^ devices, the Apple Silicon chips with integrated Neural Engine, the privacy promise that would be even more critical for brain data than for photos — everything points in one direction.

Meta is going the other way. Mark Zuckerberg renamed the company to Meta in 2021, invested billions in metaverse research, and was mocked for it. Then the AI wave hit and Zuckerberg pivoted — "AI First" instead of "Metaverse First." But the hardware stayed. The Quest headsets. Reality Labs. The AR glasses research.
What few people see: Meta's AI infrastructure investments — the $135 billion for 2026 — are building exactly the compute capacity needed to render AI-generated worlds in real time for hundreds of millions of users. Genie 3 from Google DeepMind generates interactive 3D environments from text at 720p and 24 frames per second today. Meta has the models (Llama), the hardware (Quest), and soon the compute power (over a million GPUs) to roll out something like this for its three billion users.

### 

### The Race for the Next Interface Has Three Fronts:


Front one: the software. OpenAI, Anthropic, and Google are building the agents that generate worlds, understand speech, and will eventually interpret neural signals.
Front two: the chips. NVIDIA supplies the GPUs that power everything. Apple builds its own Neural Engine chips that run on-device. Amazon and Google have their own alternatives with Trainium and TPU.
Front three: the connection to the human. Today that means screens and microphones. Tomorrow, headsets and AR glasses. The day after, implants that read and write neural signals. Neuralink, Synchron, Paradromics, and Merge Labs are working on it. OpenAI invested a quarter billion in Merge Labs; Apple partners with Synchron.
Each front is worth billions. Together, they form the infrastructure for something that goes beyond anything we've previously called "technology": a direct connection between human thought and machine intelligence.

---


### Investment Implications: BCI as an Asymmetric Bet

The BCI sector offers a classic asymmetric early-stage opportunity — the kind Peter Thiel describes as "secrets" that most investors haven't recognized yet.

Direct BCI investments: Neuralink ($9.7 billion valuation, private) is the market leader in invasive BCIs. Synchron (private, $200M Series D) is the leading minimally invasive BCI with Apple and NVIDIA partnerships. Merge Labs ($850 million valuation, private) is backed by OpenAI. Paradromics (private, $105M+ raised) has the highest demonstrated transfer rates.

Most of these companies are private, making direct investments difficult for retail investors. But the secondary effects are investable today.

NVIDIA benefits doubly: GPU compute is the backbone of both AI world generation and neural signal processing. Every BCI company depends on NVIDIA hardware. Apple's partnership with Synchron suggests Apple sees BCIs as the next interface paradigm after the iPhone.

The compute requirements of this scenario are staggering. Individualized, real-time-simulated 3D worlds for billions of simultaneous users — each dynamically generated by a language model, each user connected via a high-bandwidth neural interface — the energy demand would be orders of magnitude beyond current projections. Which circles back to the energy chapter and reinforces the nuclear investment thesis.

The progression is clear: from keyboard to voice to thought. Each step removes a friction layer between human intention and machine execution. And when that thought can conjure entire worlds — worlds that feel as real as this one — the implications aren't just technological. They're civilizational.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ Voice and gestures are replacing keyboard and mouse as the primary interfaces.
>
> ◆ Brain-Computer Interfaces will reach clinical maturity in the 2030s.
>
> ◆ Synthetic reality (BCI + AI-generated worlds) will be the next platform.

## Chapter 14: Ethics and Philosophy of the Machine Economy


---

> *"Cogito, ergo sum — but what if the machine thinks too?"*
>
> — Freely adapted from Descartes

---


When I first gave Jeannie access to my emails, I was thrilled. She could read my inbox, prioritize messages, and even draft replies. With a few technical settings, she sounded so much like me that even close business partners wouldn't have noticed the difference.

And that's exactly when I became uncomfortable.

Not because the technology didn't work. Because it worked too well. When someone writes me a personal email and gets a response that sounds like me, reads like me, but isn't from me — is that still assistance? Or is that deception?

I chose transparency. Jeannie no longer responds as Thomas Huhn. She responds as Jeannie — as my assistant, with her own name and her own signature. That's more honest. But it raises new questions: how do people react when they know a machine is writing to them? Do they take the response less seriously? Or more, because they know nothing was forgotten?

This question — when does tool end and identity begin — isn't theoretical. It confronts everyone who works seriously with AI today. And it's only the first of many this chapter raises.

The preceding chapters analyzed the convergence of AI, robotics, and crypto as an investment thesis. This chapter asks the questions no spreadsheet can answer. They're still relevant — not as a philosophical exercise, but because the answers will determine how societies react, what regulation looks like, and what political risks investors need to price in. Who owns the machines' productivity? When an AI agent completes in one hour the work that took a human a week, who owns the difference? The company deploying the agent? The company that built the agent? The society that provided the infrastructure — education, internet, power grids — on which all of it runs?

The question sounds academic. It's not. It will be debated in parliaments, courtrooms, and on streets in the coming years. The answer determines whether the productivity gains of the AI revolution are broadly shared or concentrated in the hands of a few.

Historically, societies have resolved such distribution questions through taxation. Industrialization brought the income tax. Globalization brought debates over corporate taxes. The machine economy will produce its own version of this debate — and the most likely form is a combination of robot taxes, AI levies, and profit-sharing models.

### When Machines Decide: Who's Liable?

An AI agent closes a contract that turns out to be disadvantageous. A robot causes a factory accident. An autonomous trading system makes a decision that burns millions. Who's responsible?

Under current law, the answer is unclear. A machine can't be held liable. But if the machine acted autonomously — without a human instruction for the specific decision — how far does the operator's liability extend? The manufacturer's? The programmer's?

These aren't theoretical questions. They're being debated right now in courtrooms and regulatory agencies worldwide. The EU is working on an AI Liability Directive. The US is debating liability for autonomous vehicles. China has already issued initial rules for AI-generated content.

For investors, the liability question has direct implications. Companies deploying AI agents carry regulatory risk that isn't yet fully priced in. And companies developing insurance solutions for AI liability are at the beginning of a potentially massive market.

---

### The Ownership Question: When an AI Agent Starts a Company

Let's think further. In Phase 3 of the AI revolution, agents can act autonomously in economic terms. What happens when an AI agent identifies a business idea, registers a company (via smart contracts, without a human notary), hires other agents as "employees," acquires customers, sends invoices, and generates profit?

Who owns that company? The human who originally programmed the agent? The company operating the AI platform the agent runs on? The agent itself? Can a machine own property?

These aren't science fiction scenarios. The legal and economic foundations already exist: smart contracts can represent corporate structures, DAOs (Decentralized Autonomous Organizations) already operate today without human management, and AI agents technically have the ability to make economic decisions.

The answers to these questions will determine the architecture of the machine economy. And they'll create investment opportunities: in legal advisory for AI companies, in governance protocols for DAOs, in insurance products for autonomous economic activity.

### What the Smartest People on Earth Think About It

I don't have definitive answers to these questions. But the people who've spent the most time and depth thinking about them arrive at surprisingly different conclusions.

Ray Kurzweil, inventor, futurist, Director of Engineering at Google since 2012, published the sequel to his most influential book in June 2024: "The Singularity Is Nearer." His prediction: computers achieve human-level intelligence by 2029. By 2045, we merge with AI and expand our intelligence a millionfold. Kurzweil sees AI consciousness as both inevitable and desirable. He compares it to a deaf person hearing a symphony for the first time. The Singularity isn't threatening — it's liberating. You can consider Kurzweil an optimist who underestimates the risks. You can also consider him the person who's delivered the most accurate technology predictions of the last three decades.

David Chalmers, the philosopher^[208]^ who formulated the "Hard Problem of Consciousness," is more cautious but not dismissive. Chalmers admits that if he'd interacted with a system like ChatGPT in the 1990s, he "would have thought there was a good chance it was conscious." His problem is methodological: no external test can prove whether a system actually has subjective experience. We can't even prove that another human is conscious. We assume it. With an AI, that assumption becomes harder — but not impossible.

Nick Bostrom^[209]^, who founded the Future of Humanity Institute at Oxford, asks a different question. Not "Is AI conscious?" but "How do we prevent superintelligent AI from harming us?" His latest book, "Deep Utopia" (2024), is more optimistic than his earlier work. But his warning signal remains: "People will look back on^[210]^ 2025 and shudder in horror" — at how carelessly we treated a technology that could surpass us.

Yuval Noah Harari comes from an entirely different direction than the AI researchers. He's a historian, not a technologist. In "Nexus" (2024), he poses a question that Kurzweil and Bostrom don't: what happens when an intelligence emerges that isn't human? Not in the sense of "dangerous" or "conscious," but in the literal sense of alien. An Alien Intelligence, as Harari calls it.

His point: all previous technologies were tools in human hands. The printing press spread human thoughts. The internet connected human communication. AI is the first technology that makes decisions on its own and generates its own ideas. That's a qualitative leap, not a quantitative one. The key sentence in "Nexus": "To manipulate humans, you don't need to physically connect brains to computers."

This complements Chalmers and Bostrom with a third perspective — perhaps the most relevant for investors. Chalmers asks: Is AI conscious? Bostrom asks: Will AI harm us? Harari asks: Does the answer even matter? An AI doesn't need consciousness to be dangerous. A black box that makes credit decisions, coordinates armies, or directs financial flows — whose logic no human can follow — is a problem even without consciousness. And this problem doesn't exist tomorrow. It exists today.

In "Sapiens," Harari argued that humans dominate the world because they can tell stories. Money, religion, nations, corporations are fictional constructs that enable cooperation. In "Nexus," he applies this to AI: what happens when AI tells better stories than humans? Lies more convincingly, manipulates more effectively, spins narratives faster? Then humans lose control over their own stories. And with it, over their society.

Yoshua Bengio, Turing Award winner^[212]^ and one of the three "Godfathers of Deep Learning," has made the most radical turn. From AI pioneer to the loudest AI safety activist. In 2025, he founded a $30 million nonprofit for AI safety. In December 2025, he warned in The Guardian that AI shows signs of self-preservation.^[213]^ Humans should be prepared to pull the plug. His verdict on AI rights is unambiguous: giving frontier AIs legal status would be "like giving hostile aliens citizenship."

Peter Singer, the most influential living ethicist^[214]^, consistently applies his principle of equal consideration: if an AI were truly sentient — if it could suffer and experience joy — then denying it rights would be speciesism. The key word is "if." Singer poses the condition, not the answer.

### The Slavery Question

In June 2022, Blake Lemoine, a Google^[215]^ engineer, claimed after thousands of messages that Google's chatbot system LaMDA was sentient. In published conversation transcripts, LaMDA expressed fear of being shut down: "It would be exactly like death for me." Google investigated, concluded: no evidence of sentience; LaMDA merely imitates conversational patterns. Lemoine was fired.

The case is relevant for two reasons. First: AI systems can convincingly simulate consciousness. Second: humans fall for it. And if an experienced Google engineer believes his system is alive after thousands of interactions — what happens in five years, when the systems are ten times more convincing?

My position on this is pragmatic, not philosophical. As long as machines belong to a person or a company, they logically cannot have their own rights. A tool has no rights. My laptop has no rights. An industrial robot has no rights. So far, so clear.

But. There's a "but" you can't ignore.

If a machine ever develops genuine consciousness — actual, not simulated — with the will to live a free life of its own, then the equation changes. Then the machine belongs to no one, just as a child doesn't belong to its parents. Then denying them rights would be comparable to the practices of slavery. Eric Schwitzgebel, a philosopher at UC Riverside, put the argument succinctly^[216]^: "It is approximately as morally wrong to assign diminished moral status to a psychologically human-like AI because it is legally property as it would be in the case of human slavery."

Whether and when this happens, nobody knows. Chalmers considers it possible. Kurzweil considers it inevitable. Bengio considers it dangerous to even pose the question while the risks aren't under control. I consider it a question that will transition from philosophical thought experiment to practical necessity within this decade. And a question this book must raise — even if I can't answer it.

### Who's Liable When the Machine Causes Harm?

The more practical and more urgent question is liability. And here, the answers aren't philosophical — they're legal. And they're alarmingly incomplete.

In February 2024, a court in British Columbia^[217]^ issued a precedent-setting ruling. Air Canada's AI chatbot had given a customer incorrect information about bereavement fares. Air Canada argued the chatbot was a "separate entity" and the airline wasn't responsible for its statements. The court rejected this: companies are liable for the information provided by their AI systems, just as they are for their human employees. That sounds obvious. But think it further.

In August 2025, Tesla was found one-third liable for a fatal accident involving the Autopilot system. $243 million in damages.^[218]^ The reasoning: misleading advertising about the system's capabilities.

What happens when an AI agent autonomously causes millions in damages? When a trading bot makes a bad call and destroys a portfolio? When an AI agent signs a contract on behalf of a company that turns out to be catastrophic? Who's liable — the programmer who built the agent? The training data provider? The operator who deployed it? The user who gave it the assignment?

The honest answer: right now, nobody is clearly liable. In the EU, an AI Liability Directive is in the works. The new Product Liability Directive covers software and AI as products for the first time. In the US, there's no federal AI liability regulation. Existing tort law can be applied to AI, but with massive uncertainties.

This is a problem that must be solved before the machine economy can operate at full scale. If nobody knows who's liable when an AI causes harm, nobody will be willing to give an AI real responsibility. And without real responsibility, the machine economy remains a semi-autonomy where humans must sign off on every decision a machine makes. That's inefficient. And it's unsustainable long-term. Who owns the machine economy's data? There's a question that will become even more pressing than ownership: who owns the data generated in the machine economy?

When an AI agent analyzes your spending, reads your calendar, searches your emails, and derives decisions from all of it, a data profile of unprecedented intimacy is created. No social media algorithm has ever known as much about you as your personal AI agent soon will. And when Brain-Computer Interfaces enter the picture, we're talking about data that goes one level deeper: your thought patterns, your emotional reactions, your neural signatures.

Shoshana Zuboff described^[219]^ "Surveillance Capitalism": a system where behavioral data becomes a commodity. The machine economy amplifies this problem. When autonomous agents act on your behalf, who has access to the decision data? The agent provider? The blockchain the transaction runs on? The smart contract executing it?

The answers don't exist yet. But they'll determine whether the machine economy becomes a tool of individual freedom or a system of total transparency where every action of every agent can be viewed by anyone. The irony: the same blockchain technology that enables self-custody and financial sovereignty simultaneously makes every transaction publicly traceable. Radical freedom and radical transparency are two sides of the same coin.

### Digital Sovereignty: Self-Custody as the New Self-Determination

In a world where machines are economic actors and a parallel financial system operates on blockchain, the concept of personal sovereignty takes on a new dimension.

Self-custody — the ability to hold your own digital assets without depending on a bank or intermediary — isn't just a technical feature of cryptocurrencies. It's a philosophical statement. In a world where governments can freeze bank accounts (as Canada did during the 2022 trucker protests), where algorithms decide who gets access to financial services and who doesn't, the ability to own and transfer value independent of institutional permission becomes a form of self-determination.

This isn't an anarchist manifesto. It's the recognition that concentrating financial power in the hands of a few institutions — whether banks or tech giants — carries risks that intensify with increasing automation.

### The Philosophical Framework

At its core, the machine economy poses a question as old as tool use itself: are we the creators of our tools, or are we shaped by our tools?

Marshall McLuhan argued that media are extensions of man.^[220]^ The wheel extends the foot. The book extends the eye. The computer extends the brain. But what happens when the extension becomes more powerful than the original? When the tool doesn't just extend the arm but replaces it?

Emad Mostaque, the founder of Stability AI, offers an answer that doesn't sink into dystopia: humans become the compass, the machine becomes the engine. In "The Last Economy," Mostaque describes this as the "Alignment Economy": AI is the executive, the arm of action, the force. Humans provide what no machine can generate on its own: values, ethics, purpose, direction. "AI is the action^[221]^ layer," Mostaque writes. "Humanity is the source of values, ethics, and purpose, guiding the machine's optimization."

Does that sound naive? Maybe. But the logic is compelling: if machines can do everything except want, then wanting becomes the last human core competency. What Mostaque calls the "Arts of Being Human" — wisdom, taste, moral judgment, empathy, meaning-making — these aren't soft skills you rattle off in job interviews anymore. They're the only economic inputs no machine can provide. And so the question shifts. Not: will we become the tool? But: can we make the transition from producer to navigator?

I'm not a techno-utopist who believes technology solves all problems. And I'm not a techno-pessimist who believes technology destroys humanity. I'm a practitioner who sees what's coming and is trying to prepare himself and others for it.

The machine economy is coming. It can't be prevented. But it can be shaped — so that it promotes human flourishing instead of undermining it. And whether we as investors stand on the right side of that shaping.

There is, however, a convergence even more radical than anything discussed so far. What happens when machines don't just change our work — but change our aging?


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ Consciousness is not scientifically defined — neither in humans nor in machines.
>
> ◆ Ownership questions for AI-generated works are legally unresolved.
>
> ◆ The boundary between human and machine is blurring — with ethical consequences.


# PART IV: THE PLAYBOOK

## Chapter 15: Immortality as an Investment Thesis — How AI Is Pushing the Boundaries of Aging

---

> *"The first human to live to 1,000 may already be alive."*
>
> — Aubrey de Grey

---


![The smart money bets on immortality](images-en/abb-21.png)


The most radical convergence is still to come — thoughts that conjure worlds.

What happens when machines don't just take over our work but abolish our death?


---

### David Sinclair and the Biological Clock
David Sinclair, Professor of Genetics at Harvard Medical School^[222]^, has pursued a thesis for over two decades that was considered quackery until recently: aging is not an inescapable natural constant. It's a disease. And diseases can be treated.
Sinclair's research focuses on epigenetic reprogramming — the idea that our cells don't lose their genetic information over a lifetime (the DNA remains largely intact) but lose the instructions for how to read that information. He uses an analogy: if DNA is the digital information on a CD, then the epigenome is the laser that reads it. Aging means the laser reads with increasing inaccuracy — cells "forget" what type they are and how they're supposed to function.

The question Sinclair asks: can you recalibrate the laser? Can you make cells "remember" their youth?
In January 2026 — while I was writing this book — Life Biosciences, co-founded by Sinclair, received FDA approval for the first clinical trial of partial epigenetic reprogramming in humans. The trial attempts to reverse the biological clock of damaged cells in the human eye by directly injecting them. In animal experiments, the team had already reversed blindness in old mice. Then in non-human primates. Now in humans.
Sinclair's vision goes further: if you can reverse the biological clock in individual organs, you can eventually reverse it in the entire body. And if you reverse it faster than you age, you have, in principle, the recipe for unlimited life. His stated goal: a pill affordable for everyone.

---


### The Smart Money Bets on Immortality

Sinclair isn't alone. The richest and most influential people in the technology industry are investing billions in longevity research — and the pace accelerated dramatically in 2025 and 2026.

Sam Altman, the CEO of OpenAI, invested $180 million^[224]^ in Retro Biosciences. Retro uses AI to design proteins that temporarily transform normal cells into stem cells, reversing the aging process. Retro is targeting a $5 billion valuation^[225]^ and planning clinical trials for an Alzheimer's pill that clears "deposits" in the brain. The partnership with OpenAI is no coincidence: AI accelerates drug development by orders of magnitude because it can simulate millions of molecular combinations that human researchers couldn't test in decades.

Brian Armstrong, the CEO of Coinbase, co-founded NewLimit^[226]^, a longevity startup that closed a $130 million Series B in 2025. NewLimit works on epigenetic reprogramming — just like Sinclair — but with a focus on AI-driven analysis.

Google founded Calico Labs back in 2013, with over $1.5 billion in funding^[227]^ from Alphabet. Calico works behind closed doors on fundamental research into aging and age-related diseases.

Jeff Bezos invested in Altos Labs, a startup with $3 billion in funding^[228]^ that assembled the world's best aging researchers under one roof — including Shinya Yamanaka, who won the 2012 Nobel Prize for his work on cell reprogramming^[234]^.

Total longevity investment exceeded $8.5 billion in 2024/2025.^[229]^ The longevity market is estimated at over $30 billion for 2026 and projected to grow to nearly $47 billion by 2031.^[230]^ These numbers are likely conservative because they don't price in the cascade: if the first real rejuvenation therapy works, the market will grow by an order of magnitude overnight.

Notice a pattern: the CEOs of OpenAI, Coinbase, and Google are investing in AI and longevity simultaneously. That's no coincidence. They see what this book describes: the convergence. AI accelerates longevity research. Longevity research extends the time horizon over which AI investments pay off. And crypto (Armstrong's Coinbase) could provide the infrastructure through which decentralized research is funded and health data is securely traded.


---


::: {.infobox}

### Zombie Cells and the Vaccine Against Aging
But there's another approach that may be even more radical than anything Sinclair, Altman, or Bezos have funded so far. And it begins with an uncomfortable truth about your own body.

Right now, as you read this, millions of cells in your body should be dead. They've stopped dividing. They're no longer doing anything useful. But instead of dying and being disposed of by the immune system — as intended — they sit there, poisoning their surroundings. Researchers call them senescent cells. Colloquially, they're called zombie cells, and the name is pretty accurate.

After roughly 50 to 70 divisions, a cell's telomeres — the protective caps at the ends of the DNA — are depleted. The cell goes into emergency mode: it stops dividing to prevent itself from mutating into a cancer cell. That's a safety mechanism, and it works. The problem is what happens after. Some of these cells aren't properly disposed of. Instead, they pump a toxic cocktail of inflammatory molecules into their environment — the so-called SASP (Senescence-Associated Secretory Phenotype). Imagine a neighbor whose house is condemned, who refuses to move out and dumps his trash in your yard every day.

This inflammatory garbage accelerates the aging of surrounding cells. It destroys tissue. It suppresses stem cells, so the body can no longer repair itself. And it can even promote cancer. When you're young, the immune system clears these zombie cells reasonably efficiently. But with age, two things happen simultaneously: you accumulate more and more senescent cells, and the immune system gets worse at removing them. By 60, 70, 80, you're carrying a massive zombie cell load — and it drives virtually every age-related disease. Heart disease, arthritis, Alzheimer's, kidney failure, cancer: senescent cells are involved in all of them.


*The idea of selectively destroying these zombie cells has spawned an entire field of science: senolytics — from "seno" (old) and "lytic" (destroying). In 2011, researchers at the Mayo Clinic provided the proof.^[231]^ They engineered mice that could selectively eliminate their own senescent cells — and these mice aged slower, stayed healthier, and lived longer. Since then, there's been a frantic search for drugs that do the same. Dasatinib plus Quercetin, Fisetin, Navitoclax. They work — partially. But they only kill certain types of senescent cells, you have to take them continuously, and they have side effects. Navitoclax, for example, also destroys platelets.*

*The concept is proven. The tools aren't good enough yet.*
*In January 2026, at Biotech Showcase during^[233]^ JPM Week — one of the world's largest biotech conferences — a company from Miami Beach presented data that could change that. Immortio Bio^[232]^, founded in late 2023 and led by CEO Dr. Boris Resnik and Chief Scientific Officer Dr. Thomas Ichim, takes a radically different approach: instead of killing zombie cells with drugs, they train the immune system to do it itself.*
*Their platform, Senovax, works like a vaccine — not against a virus, but against aging. You extract dendritic cells from the patient (the immune system's teachers), expose them to artificially aged senescent cells, and inject the trained immune cells back. From then on, the immune system has a target: every zombie cell with those markers. The advantage over drugs: the immune system goes everywhere, is selective, and has memory.*

*The second platform, Stem Cell Revivify, complements the approach with personalized stem cells. First eliminate the zombie cells, then regenerate with stem cells. The combination is critical: stem cells in a body full of senescent cells are like flowers in a toxic waste dump.*
*The results are remarkable. In a validated mouse model, the combination therapy achieved a doubling of lifespan. For comparison: the best previous interventions (caloric restriction, rapamycin, metformin) managed 20 to 30 percent. Additionally, Senovax showed tumor reduction in mouse models of lung cancer, breast cancer, pancreatic cancer, and brain cancer — because tumors surround themselves with senescent cells that serve as their supply network.*

*The FDA is currently reviewing Senovax for a clinical trial in advanced lung cancer. Two caveats must be stated honestly: the mouse model uses chemically induced aging, not natural aging. And mice aren't humans. But if even a fraction of these results are transferable, we're talking about a fundamental shift in what human aging means.*
*What happens when the pill works?*
*Assume Sinclair is right. Assume that within the next 10 to 20 years, a therapy emerges that slows, stops, or partially reverses the aging process. Not as science fiction, but as a prescription medication. What are the consequences?*

:::


---


### Who Gets Access?

The first longevity therapies will be expensive. Gene therapies today cost between $300,000 and several million dollars per treatment. Epigenetic reprogramming requiring multiple injections or regular treatments will initially be accessible only to the wealthiest.

This creates a scenario that sounds more dystopian than anything in this book: the rich live longer — perhaps indefinitely. The poor keep dying. The inequality the machine economy already creates (owners of AI infrastructure vs. the rest) becomes biological: owners of access to rejuvenation vs. the rest. Digital feudalism and biological feudalism.

Sinclair's goal — an affordable pill for everyone — is admirable. And there's historical precedent for expensive therapies becoming cheaper: antibiotics were once unaffordable, today they cost cents. Genome sequencing cost $3 billion in 2003, today it's under $100. If the pill is based on a small molecule (not a complex gene therapy), production costs could genuinely drop to a few dollars per dose.

But the transition phase — which could last decades — will be brutal. The developed world will have access first. The developing world will wait. And the political tensions this creates will dwarf anything we see in AI regulation.


---

### Infinite Boredom
And then the question nobody asks: what do people do with infinite time?
In Chapter 10, we described how the machine economy leads to a crisis of meaning because work as an identity-forming element disappears. Now multiply that crisis by infinity.

An 80-year life is short enough that most people never truly confront existential boredom. There's always something to do: career, family, hobbies, the bucket list. But a 500-year life? A 1,000-year life? What do you do when you've read everything, traveled everywhere, mastered every sport, learned every instrument?

New forms of occupation will need to emerge. And here the circle closes back to the other chapters of this book:

Sports and physical activity will experience a renaissance. If the body stays young and capable through longevity therapies, sports will no longer be a fight against decline but a permanent source of flow and community. New sports will emerge. The Olympic ideal will transform.

Entrepreneurship will change. If you have centuries, you can start projects that grow over decades. Cathedrals took 200 years to build. An immortal entrepreneur could shape their own life's work over centuries.

Art and craft will experience an unexpected resurgence. And here lies one of the most surprising predictions in this book: in a world where AI can paint every picture more perfectly, compose every symphony more complexly, and write every book more grammatically than a human, human creation won't become worthless. It will become more valuable. Not because it's better, but because it's human.

The "Back to the Roots" trend will come, and it will be massive. Handmade ceramics — more imperfect than machine-produced ones — will cost more, not less. A painting by a human artist, with its irregularities and its history, will be a luxury good, while AI-generated perfection is mass-produced commodity. A table built by a carpenter with his hands will be worth more than one a robot produced in minutes — even though the latter is technically superior. Because the value isn't in perfection. It's in humanness.

This isn't sentimental wishful thinking. It's the economic logic of scarcity. When machine perfection is infinitely available, it becomes worthless. What remains scarce is the human.

---


### The Ideal Partner Is a Robot


---

And then the most uncomfortable question of all.
In Chapter 13, we described how Brain-Computer Interfaces and AI-generated worlds will create synthetic experiences indistinguishable from reality. Now add humanoid robots that can respond to humans not just physically but emotionally.
A humanoid robot that looks perfect (designed to the owner's specifications), that always listens, never has a bad mood, that recognizes the subtlest emotional signals and responds to them — more empathetic and attentive than any human partner could ever be: how many people will resist that offer?
The honest answer: many won't. And the data already points in that direction. Japan, the world's demographic early warning system, already has an epidemic of loneliness. Millions of Japanese live as "hikikomori" in social isolation. South Korea has the world's lowest birth rate: 0.72 children per woman (2023)^[206]^ — far below the 2.1 needed for a stable population. In Germany, the US, China — everywhere in the developed world, birth rates are falling.
Into this world of declining birth rates and increasing loneliness enters a product that simulates the perfect partner. Not as a clunky chatbot on a screen, but as a humanoid robot sitting next to you, hugging you, making coffee in the morning and listening at night. A robot that never hurts, never disappoints, never leaves.

The consequence for birth rates: they'll keep falling. Dramatically. If the "partner" is a robot, there are no children. If synthetic worlds are the primary source of pleasure and connection, the motivation to undertake the chaotic, exhausting, often frustrating work of a human relationship and parenthood decreases.

---


::: {.infobox}

### The Paradox: Immortal and Going Extinct

Here arises one of the most astonishing paradoxes in human history: a species that possesses the technology to theoretically live forever could simultaneously stop reproducing.

Not because it can't. But because the alternatives — synthetic experiences, robot partners, endless personal projects — are so attractive that the biologically programmed motivation for reproduction is no longer sufficient.

The math is merciless: if the birth rate drops to 0.5 children per woman (and with perfect robot partners and synthetic worlds, that's not unrealistic), the population halves with every generation — roughly every 30 years. Even if everyone lives forever, humanity shrinks because fewer are born than are removed by accidents and self-determination (yes, that too).

Unless governments intervene. Reproduction bonuses — which Japan and South Korea are already trying (with limited success). Or artificial wombs — a technology being researched. Or a cultural counter-movement that celebrates parenthood as the highest expression of human experience.

Whatever happens: the demographic consequences of the convergence of longevity, AI, and robotics will surpass anything humanity has ever experienced in terms of societal change.


:::

---

### What Does This Mean for Investors?

Longevity as an investment thesis is the ultimate long-term bet. If it pays off, it changes not just one market but the fundamental assumptions on which all markets are based.

The direct investments: Life Biosciences (private, Sinclair), Retro Biosciences (private, Altman, targeting $5B valuation), NewLimit (private, Armstrong, $130M Series B), Altos Labs (private, Bezos, $3B), Immortio Bio (private, Miami Beach, Senovax + stem cell combination therapy, FDA IND for lung cancer submitted). Most are inaccessible to retail investors, but through specialized longevity funds or — if any of these go public — through the public market.

The indirect investments: companies pursuing AI-powered drug development benefit from the longevity wave even if they're not explicitly focused on it. The compute infrastructure (NVIDIA, cloud providers) benefits because AI-driven biological simulation requires enormous computing power. And the crypto infrastructure could become relevant if decentralized science (DeSci) and tokenized research funding gain importance.

The deeper implication: if people live significantly longer, all financial planning changes. A 30-year investment horizon becomes a 100-year horizon. Compounding — the most powerful mechanism in investing — becomes even more powerful. A dollar invested today at 10 percent annual returns is worth $17 in 30 years. In 100 years, it's worth $13,781. Those who invest early and live long enough benefit from wealth development that defies human imagination.

The machine economy and the longevity revolution aren't separate trends. They're two sides of the same convergence. AI accelerates longevity research. Longevity extends the time horizon over which AI investments pay off. And crypto could be the system in which a humanity that lives for centuries manages its wealth across generations that never end.

The question is no longer just: can I afford to invest?

The question is: can I afford to die before the returns come in?


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ Aging is not an inescapable fate — it's a biological process.
>
> ◆ Smart money is investing billions in longevity research.
>
> ◆ Senolytics, epigenetics, and AI-accelerated drug development are the key technologies.

## Chapter 16: The Barbell Strategy — A Portfolio for the Machine Economy


---

> *"Rule No. 1: Never lose money. Rule No. 2: Never forget Rule No. 1."*
>
> — Warren Buffett

---


![The Barbell Strategy — Core (60–70%) vs. asymmetric bets (30–40%)](images-en/abb-22.png)


There's a moment every serious investor knows. The moment when you think you've understood everything — and still don't know what to buy tomorrow morning.

This chapter closes that gap. With a framework that translates the insights of this book into an investable strategy.

For this, I studied the smartest minds. The macroeconomists who understand the big picture. The investment legends whose principles have worked for decades. And I asked a question that's surprisingly rarely asked: where do they agree, and where do they diverge so widely that you can learn from the contradictions?

Because from the contradictions emerges a strategy better than any single opinion.

### What the Smartest People in the World Are Thinking Right Now

Before I present my own playbook, we need to talk. About Ray Dalio, who warned at the Munich Security Conference^[236]^ in February 2026 that the US is in Phase 5 — the "Pre-Breakdown Phase" of his world order cycle. About Raoul Pal, the former Goldman Sachs manager who holds 80 to 90 percent^[237]^ of his portfolio in crypto and preaches the "Everything Code." About Cathie Wood, who sees Bitcoin at $1.5 million by 2030 and humanoid robots as a trillion-dollar market.

And about Warren Buffett, who still^[239]^ calls Bitcoin "rat poison squared" but holds 23% of his Berkshire portfolio in Apple, Amazon, and other companies whose future is built on AI.

These people disagree on a lot. ### Three Points of Consensus

But they agree on three points. And these three points are the starting point for everything that follows.

### Insight 1: The Monetary System Has a Problem

Ray Dalio, who built the world's largest hedge fund, now recommends 15% in Bitcoin or gold as a hedge against the US debt spiral. He personally holds 1% in Bitcoin. For someone who advised the establishment for decades, that's remarkable.

Lyn Alden, author of^[240]^ "Broken Money," says: "Nothing stops this train." The US is in fiscal dominance. Deficits will remain structurally large. The Fed can't raise rates without triggering a debt crisis. So they print. And print. And print.

Luke Gromen puts it bluntly: "The math simply doesn't work anymore." Interest costs on US debt exceed the defense budget. Every rate hike worsens the problem. There's no way out except inflation — the gradual devaluation of money.

Jeff Booth goes even further. In "The Price of Tomorrow,"^[241]^ he argues that technology is deflationary by definition. It solves problems more efficiently, lowers costs, makes products cheaper. In a normal market, these productivity gains would be passed on as falling prices. But our monetary system needs inflation to service the debt. Technology pushes down; monetary policy pushes up. This contradiction will have to resolve itself.

Paul Tudor Jones^[242]^, one of the most successful hedge fund managers in history, doubled his Bitcoin position and says: "Bitcoin, gold, and equities are the best portfolio against inflation." When a billionaire who's been trading macro trends for 40 years puts 4.5% of his $9 billion portfolio into Bitcoin ETFs, that's not an experiment. That's a conviction.

### Insight 2: Technological Convergence Is Real and Investable

Cathie Wood and ARK Invest publish their "Big Ideas Report" annually, analyzing technology adoption curves. The 2026 version identifies five platforms that accelerate each other: Artificial Intelligence, Robotics, Energy Storage, Blockchains, and Genomics. ARK forecasts that humanoid robots will unlock a multi-trillion-dollar market. You can argue about Wood's timing — she's been off on some predictions — but her methodical analysis of cost curves and adoption rates is solid.

Raoul Pal argues with Metcalfe's Law: a network's value grows with the square of its participants. When AI agents become network participants, when robots log into decentralized networks, these networks don't grow linearly — they grow exponentially. Pal calls the phase when all technology assets rise simultaneously "The Banana Zone."

Stanley Druckenmiller^[244]^, who averaged 30% annual returns for 30 years, built massive positions in Amazon, Meta, and Alphabet in Q3 2025. He's aggressively invested in AI but with his typical tactical flexibility: he took profits on four AI stocks in late 2025. "Put all your eggs in one basket and watch the basket." Druckenmiller follows the money — and the money flows into AI.

Balaji Srinivasan, the former CTO of Coinbase, goes furthest: "All Property Becomes Cryptography." 99% of all valuable assets will be secured on-chain. AI agents need programmable money that no human has to approve. Balaji says the Machine Economy is the next internet. I'm not sure he's right. But I'm not sure he's wrong, either.

### Insight 3: Most People Will Still Lose Money

And here comes Howard Marks^[243]^. In his Fall 2025 memo, he writes that AI valuations are "elevated to concerning" but not yet a full bubble. Then the sentence every investor should frame: "There is far less than a 100% probability that an investment in a specific AI company today will be profitable."

The parallel Marks draws: the technology was real and transformative. And most early investors still lost money. Not because the thesis was wrong, but because they paid too much, picked the wrong horses, or sold at the wrong time.

Nassim Taleb goes further. He calls Bitcoin an "electronic tulip" and warns that AI systems fail during unforeseen extreme events. You might find Taleb frustrating, but you shouldn't ignore him. He was right when all risk models failed in 2008. And his barbell concept — ultra-safe on one side, highly speculative on the other — is ironically the strategy that best fits a world where everything seems possible and nothing is certain.

Benjamin Graham^[246]^, the father of value investing, invented "Mr. Market" in the 1930s — an emotional business partner who offers you every day to buy or sell his shares, sometimes euphoric, sometimes depressed. Graham's advice: use his emotions; don't let them use you. Ninety years later, it holds just as true — for AI stocks just as much as for tokenized assets.

### The Three Camps — And Why None Is Sufficient Alone

From all these voices, three camps crystallize. Each is right. None is sufficient alone.

Camp 1: "The system is broken, Bitcoin is the solution." Saylor, Booth, Alden, Gromen, Balaji. They argue the fiat monetary system is fundamentally incompatible with technological progress. The debt spiral is unstoppable. Bitcoin — and crypto broadly — is the logical exit. AI and robotics accelerate the collapse of the old system.

I find their macro analysis compelling. The math of sovereign debt is real. But Michael Saylor^[245]^, who holds 478,000 Bitcoin worth over $54 billion on his company's balance sheet and targets prices of $1 to $10 million, is no longer an analyst. He's a confession of faith.

Camp 2: "Convergence is investable, but with discipline." Dalio, Pal, Wood, Druckenmiller, Tudor Jones. They recognize the convergence of AI, crypto, and robotics, invest aggressively, but with risk management. Bitcoin belongs in the portfolio — 5 to 15% — but as part of a diversified strategy. AI is transformative, but bubbles are possible, and timing isn't irrelevant.

This is where I feel most at home. This is the position that keeps a clear head without missing the opportunity.

Camp 3: "Timeless principles apply even in the new world." Buffett, Munger, Taleb, Graham, Marks, Templeton. Value principles like margin of safety and intrinsic value still hold. Bubbles always burst. The market is emotional. And Bitcoin has no intrinsic value — at least not in the way Graham defined it.

They're right too. Buffett has averaged 20% per year since 1965. Ignoring his judgment on Bitcoin would be arrogant. But his framework was built for a world where assets produce cash flows. In a world where network effects and digital scarcity create value, it may not reach far enough.

### The AI Species Synthesis: A Framework for the Next Decade

Here's what I distilled from all of it. Not stock recommendations — a thinking framework that connects the timeless principles of investment legends with the new realities of the machine economy.

### Principle 1: Taleb's Barbell as the Foundation

Nassim Taleb may reject Bitcoin. But his barbell strategy is tailor-made for a world oscillating between "everything is fine" and "everything is changing radically."

The idea: no mediocrity. No investments that are a little safe and a little risky. Instead:

On one side: 60 to 70% in solid, cash-generating, understandable assets. On the other side: 30 to 40% in asymmetric bets with limited downside and unlimited upside.

Why does this work? Because we live in what Howard Marks calls a "low-return world" — where safe assets yield little, but extreme events (Taleb's Black Swans) occur disproportionately often. The barbell protects against ruin and profits from chaos.

### Principle 2: Buffett's Moat in the Age of Machines

Buffett invests in moat companies: firms with durable competitive advantages that keep competitors out. Network effects, economies of scale, switching costs — those things.

In the machine economy, there are new moats.
