NVIDIA has the compute moat: the market dominance in AI chips described in Chapter 8, reinforced by the CUDA software ecosystem so deeply embedded that switching takes years. A Buffett moat in its purest form.

Ethereum has the network moat: the most smart contracts, the most DeFi activity, the most developers. Network effects that strengthen with every new participant. 65% of all tokenized real-world assets run on Ethereum.

Apple has the ecosystem moat: hardware, software, services, soon AI agents. The deeper you're in, the harder it is to get out. If you have an iPhone, a MacBook, an Apple Watch, and AirPods, you don't switch to Android for a feature.

The principle is the same as 50 years ago. The moats look different.

### Principle 3: Cathie Wood's S-Curves and Wright's Law

ARK Invest uses a concept rooted in engineering: Wright's Law. It states that every doubling of cumulative production reduces costs by a fixed percentage. This applies to transistors, solar cells, batteries — and AI inference.

The S-curve describes how technologies spread: slow start, then explosive acceleration, then saturation. Smartphones took 5 years to go from 10 to 90% market penetration. Autonomous driving and humanoid robots are at the bottom of that curve. So are AI agents — even if it doesn't feel like it.

Those who enter at the bottom buy cheap. Those who wait for confirmation buy expensive. Peter Thiel asks: "What important truth do very few people agree with you on?" At the bottom of the S-curve, the truth still feels uncomfortable. That's the point.

### Principle 4: Dalio's All-Weather Meets Pal's Liquidity Cycle

Ray Dalio's All-Weather Portfolio is built to perform in every economic climate: growth, stagnation, inflation, deflation. The idea: since nobody knows what's coming, position for all scenarios.

Raoul Pal adds the liquidity cycle: central banks drive markets more than fundamentals. Follow the liquidity, not the logic.

In a machine economy world, an All-Weather Portfolio looks different than in the 1990s. Bonds offer less protection when governments inflate away their debts. Gold remains relevant, but Bitcoin joins the mix. AI stocks partially replace the traditional growth component. And tokenized assets — discussed in the previous chapter — become something that doesn't exist in any classical model: yield-generating digital securities tradable 24/7.

### Principle 5: Druckenmiller's Flexibility and Tudor Jones's Discipline

Druckenmiller: when you're convinced, bet big. But stay flexible enough to switch positions quickly. "Put all your eggs in one basket and watch the basket."

Tudor Jones: "Losers average losers." Never add to a losing position. The 200-day moving average as a compass. Always look for 5:1 opportunities where the potential gain is five times the potential loss.

Both teach something long-term investors often forget: capital preservation is more important than returns. Lose 50% and you need 100% just to break even. That's simple math, but most investors ignore it until it's too late.

### The Physical Infrastructure of the Machine Economy

In February 2026, while software stocks crashed 17%, something happened that doesn't appear in any AI hype story: copper hit nearly $13,000 per ton. Uranium conversion prices tripled. And Taleb warned of software bankruptcies while simultaneously noting that the physical world is the actual bottleneck of the AI revolution.

The logic is simple: you can't prompt Claude to increase global titanium production by 5%. You can't ask an AI to build a transformer with a 128-week lead time. The machine economy needs machines — and machines need materials. This produces an investment thesis most AI investors ignore.

Let's start with transformers — the most unremarkable bottleneck. Every data center needs power transformers to convert grid electricity to the required voltages. The lead time for a power transformer in mid-2025: 128 weeks. For generator step-up transformers: 144 weeks. That's nearly three years. Wood Mackenzie forecasts a 30% supply deficit for power transformers in 2025.^[251]^ The cause: transformers require grain-oriented electrical steel — a specialty steel with particular magnetic properties. In the US, this material is produced by exactly one company: Cleveland-Cliffs, in two plants in Pennsylvania and Ohio. The rest comes from Asia. When a single company in two factories produces the key material for the entire American power grid, that's not a risk you solve with an algorithm.

Then copper. Reuters reported in December 2025 that the copper price exceeded $12,000, driven by data center demand and tight supply. S&P Global forecasts a supply deficit of^[252]^ 10 million metric tons by 2040, with demand rising 50%. A single hyperscale data center uses as much copper as thousands of single-family homes. By 2030, data centers alone could consume half a million tons of copper per year. Goldman Sachs doesn't see an acute shortage before 2029 — but the point is: new copper mines take 10 to 15 years from discovery to production. What's missing in 2029 should have been planned in 2015.

Tungsten is the extreme example. China controls over 80% of global tungsten production^[253]^ and processing. In 2025, China imposed new export controls. Tungsten exports fell nearly 14% in the first nine months. Tungsten is used in semiconductor fabrication equipment, defense goods, and high-temperature applications. There's no short-term alternative. There's no Western production capacity to compensate. It takes years to develop a tungsten mine and decades to build processing know-how. If China turns off the tap — as it's already done with gallium and germanium — the West has a problem no LLM can solve.

Titanium tells the same story. VSMPO-Avisma in Russia and a handful of Chinese producers dominate the market. Boeing already had a titanium shortage in 2022 that disrupted 737 MAX production. The aerospace industry now competes with defense and data centers for the same material. Supply chain experts agree: there's no solution for 2026. Diversification to Japan, Kazakhstan, or Saudi Arabia is a long-term option, not a short-term answer.

And then the nuclear supply chain, which we touched on in Chapter 9. Between uranium ore and fuel rod lies a bottleneck almost nobody knows: conversion. Uranium ore must first be converted to uranium hexafluoride before it can be enriched. Globally, there are four relevant facilities: Cameco in Canada, Orano in France, Rosatom in Russia, and a single one in the US — the Metropolis plant in Illinois, built in 1958. This plant was shut down in 2017 because operations weren't economical. In 2023, it went back online — into the strongest conversion market in history. The legacy contracts still running at $20 per kilogram of uranium will be replaced by new contracts at over $60 in the coming years. Costs stay the same; the margin mechanically triples. That's not speculation. Those are contracts expiring and being replaced by better ones.

Beryllium is the material you've probably never heard of. It's used in semiconductors, satellites, and nuclear weapons. In the US, it's produced by exactly one company: Materion, headquartered in Ohio. Hallgarten, an independent research house, calls it a monopoly^[254]^ and warns: "This is not a healthy situation." Materion had $1.79 billion in revenue in 2025 and is currently investing in new capacity for defense and semiconductors. When demand rises and there's only one supplier, that supplier sets the price.

The pattern runs through the entire physical infrastructure of the machine economy. Whether copper, tungsten, titanium, electrical steel, uranium hexafluoride, or beryllium: everywhere, rising demand from AI, defense, aerospace, and electrification meets a handful of specialized suppliers, decades-old facilities, and qualification processes that take two to five years. You can't just build a bigger factory when the price rises.

For portfolio construction, this has a consequence most AI investors miss. The picks-and-shovels strategy we began with NVIDIA doesn't end at chips. It extends into the physical world: transformer manufacturers, copper mines, uranium infrastructure, specialty metal suppliers, and grid expansion companies. These companies have something software stocks are currently losing: a moat no AI can breach. And they often trade at valuations that price in only one of their demand drivers — when three or four are operating simultaneously.

This is the part of the machine economy nobody tweets about. But it's the part without which no data center runs, no robot gets built, and no autonomous truck drives.

### The AI Species Portfolio: Three Variants

## Chapter 17: The Model Portfolio — Theory Meets Practice

Based on these principles, I've designed three model portfolios. They are not investment advice. I don't say this as a legal disclaimer — I say it because I mean it. What works for me doesn't have to work for you. Your risk tolerance, your time horizon, your life situation — these all play a role no book can answer for you.


![Three portfolio variants — conservative, balanced, aggressive](images-en/abb-23.png)


What I can give you is a thinking framework.

### Variant A: The Conservative ($100K–$500K)

#### Safe Side (70%)

| Sector | Allocation | Share |
|--------|-----------|-------|
| Equities | Global ETF (S&P 500 / Total Market), tech-overweight | 30% |
| Big Tech | NVIDIA, Microsoft, Apple, Alphabet | 15% |
| Energy | Cameco, Constellation Energy, NextEra | 10% |
| Robotics | Intuitive Surgical, ABB, Fanuc | 10% |
| Cash | Money market / high-yield savings | 5% |

#### Asymmetric Side (30%)

| Sector | Allocation | Share |
|--------|-----------|-------|
| Bitcoin | DCA over 6–12 months | 15% |
| Ethereum | Layer-2 ecosystem, Agent TAM | 5% |
| RWA | BUIDL, Ondo Finance (Tokenized Treasuries) | 5% |
| Infrastructure | Coinbase, OLAS, PEAQ, Agent Payment Rails | 5% |

*Notes on the safe side:*
- *30% Global equity ETF (S&P 500 or Total Stock Market) with US/tech overweight*
- *15% NVIDIA, Microsoft, Apple, Alphabet (the moat winners of the AI revolution)*
- *Note: Meta is deliberately absent from the conservative variant. The business model is 97% ad-funded. If AI agents take over the interface between humans and the internet and stop seeing ads, Meta's core business is under pressure — which Google can buffer better through DeepMind, Waymo, and Cloud.*
- *10% Energy and Nuclear: Cameco (uranium), Constellation Energy, NextEra*
- *10% Robotics pure-plays: Intuitive Surgical, ABB, Fanuc*
- *5% Money market fund or high-yield savings as cash reserve (Graham: Mr. Market makes offers — you need cash to take them)*

*Notes on the asymmetric side:*
- *15% Bitcoin (safety net against fiat devaluation — Tudor Jones allocation)*
- *5% Ethereum (the operating system of the tokenized economy)*
- *Ethereum has a second growth story here that's still underappreciated in the crypto discussion: the Agent TAM. When billions of AI agents transact — not once a day like a human, but hundreds of times per hour — the velocity in the network explodes. The fundamental equation MV = PQ gets a new factor. Ethereum's Layer-2 networks — Arbitrum, Optimism, Base — are built for exactly this volume.*
- *5% Tokenized Treasuries/RWA (BlackRock's BUIDL, Ondo Finance, yield-generating)*
- *5% Crypto infrastructure: Coinbase, Securitize (if listed), Machine Economy tokens (OLAS, PEAQ)*
- *One position deserves special attention: Agent Payment Rails. Stripe is building the payment infrastructure for the Agent Economy with its Chain Tempo — and both OpenAI and Anthropic are in the first batch of integration partners. If Stripe goes public in 2026 or 2027, the stock belongs in this category. Until then: PayPal is positioning itself via the X-Money integration as a payment partner for Elon Musk's agent ecosystem, and Block (formerly Square) is expanding its Bitcoin Lightning infrastructure — ideal for micro-transactions between agents.*

### Variant B: The Balanced ($500K–$2M)

#### Safe Side (60%)

| Sector | Allocation | Share |
|--------|-----------|-------|
| Equities | Global ETF with AI tilt | 20% |
| Big Tech | NVIDIA, Microsoft, Apple, Alphabet, Amazon, Meta | 15% |
| Robotics | Tesla, Intuitive Surgical, ABB, Figure AI | 10% |
| Energy | Cameco, Constellation, NuScale, Oklo, Uranium ETF | 10% |
| Cash | Cash reserve | 5% |

#### Asymmetric Side (40%)

| Sector | Allocation | Share |
|--------|-----------|-------|
| Bitcoin | DCA over 12 months | 15% |
| Ethereum | Staking, Layer-2 exposure | 8% |
| DeFi | Circle (IPO), Aave, Uniswap | 7% |
| RWA | Maple, Centrifuge, Hamilton Lane tokenized | 5% |
| Machine Economy | Olas, peaq, Fetch.ai/ASI, Render Network | 5% |

*Notes on the safe side:*
- *20% Global equity ETF with AI tilt*
- *15% Big Tech individual stocks: NVIDIA, Microsoft, Apple, Alphabet, Amazon, Meta*
- *A word on Meta: Meta is the most vulnerable name on this list. The business model is almost entirely ad-based, and as we discussed in the previous chapter, ad spending faces a structural peak if AI agents take over the intermediary role between consumers and providers. Google has a natural hedge through DeepMind, Waymo, and Cloud. Meta doesn't. Investing here is a bet on Zuckerberg's ability to transform the company into an AI infrastructure company before the ad-spending peak. The massive AI infrastructure investments for 2026 suggest he understands this. Whether it's fast enough is the question.*
- *For investors wanting to go deeper into the robotics value chain: the actual picks-and-shovels of robot body production aren't the robot makers themselves but their suppliers. Harmonic Drive Systems from Japan controls roughly 80% of the global market for precision gearboxes that go into every humanoid robot. Nidec, also Japan, dominates servo motors. If the robot supply chain is the bottleneck of the next three to five years, the companies supplying the bottleneck components are the NVIDIA of robotics.*
- *10% Robotics and Autonomy: Tesla, Intuitive Surgical, ABB, Figure AI (if listed)*
- *10% Energy supercycle: Cameco, Constellation, NuScale, Oklo, Uranium ETF*
- *5% Cash reserve*

*Notes on the asymmetric side:*
- *15% Bitcoin (DCA over 12 months — not all at once; Druckenmiller's timing discipline)*
- *8% Ethereum*
- *7% Stablecoin infrastructure and DeFi: Circle (if IPO), Aave, Uniswap token*
- *5% Tokenized RWA and Private Credit (Maple, Centrifuge, Hamilton Lane tokenized)*
- *5% Machine Economy infrastructure: Olas, peaq, Fetch.ai/ASI Alliance, Render Network*

### Variant C: The Aggressive ($2M+, high risk tolerance)

#### Safe Side (50%)

| Sector | Allocation | Share |
|--------|-----------|-------|
| Big Tech | NVIDIA, Microsoft, Apple | 15% |
| Energy | Nuclear & energy broadly diversified | 15% |
| Robotics | Tesla, Intuitive Surgical + venture positions | 10% |
| Cash / Treasuries | Tokenized Treasuries, yield-generating | 10% |

#### Asymmetric Side (50%)

| Sector | Allocation | Share |
|--------|-----------|-------|
| Bitcoin | Core position | 20% |
| Ethereum | Smart contract platform | 10% |
| AI Tokens / DeFi | Render, Filecoin, Olas, ASI Alliance, Aave | 8% |
| RWA | Private equity, real estate, private credit | 7% |
| Venture / Angel | peaq ecosystem, Robotics DAOs, BCI-adjacent firms | 5% |

*Notes on the safe side:*
- *15% Big Tech (NVIDIA, Microsoft, Apple)*
- *15% Energy and Nuclear (broadly diversified)*
- *10% Robotics portfolio (Tesla, Intuitive Surgical, plus venture-like positions)*
- *10% Cash and tokenized Treasuries (yield-generating, instantly deployable)*

*Notes on the asymmetric side:*
- *20% Bitcoin*
- *10% Ethereum*
- *8% AI infrastructure tokens and DeFi (Render, Filecoin, Olas, ASI Alliance, Aave)*
- *7% Tokenized RWA (tokenized private equity, real estate, private credit)*
- *5% Venture/Angel in Machine Economy startups (peaq ecosystem, Robotics DAOs, BCI-adjacent firms)*

### Timing: DCA, Not FOMO

Druckenmiller and Tudor Jones agree: timing matters more than long-term investors like to admit. Raoul Pal sees the liquidity peak in Q2 2026. Dalio warns of an AI bubble. Marks sees valuations as "elevated."

What to do?

Dollar Cost Averaging. Don't invest everything at once — spread it over 6 to 12 months. This isn't a sign of uncertainty — it's discipline. Graham called it "the margin of safety in the time dimension." You don't need to nail the perfect entry. You just need to be in the game when the S-curve takes off.

Tudor Jones's 200-day moving average is a useful compass: when an asset is above its 200-day average, the trend is intact. When it falls below, exercise caution. Not a perfect indicator, but better than gut feeling.

And Taleb's most important rule: cap the maximum loss. The asymmetric side of the portfolio can go to zero without bankrupting you. That's the entire point of the barbell: the safe side protects your existence. The speculative side protects your future.

The Templeton Test: Where Is Maximum Pessimism Right Now?

European AI investments. Europe has no foundation model, regulation is slowing things down, the brain drain to America continues. Everyone's writing off Europe. But SAP, ASML, and Siemens are real and profitable. European robotics — ABB, KUKA, Fanuc — is world-class. If Europe solves even part of its regulatory problem, these stocks are undervalued.

Crypto infrastructure. Many DeFi protocols and infrastructure tokens trade 80 to 90% below their highs while the technology has improved and institutional adoption is increasing. Classic Templeton: the market hates it; the fundamentals are improving.

Physical robotics. Most investors are staring at NVIDIA and forgetting that humanoid robots, delivery drones, and autonomous agriculture are still in the phase of maximum skepticism. The market prices them as a niche. ARK prices them as the next trillion-dollar market. One of them is right.

The Soros Question: What Are the False Premises in the Current Market?

"NVIDIA will dominate AI forever." Possible — but DeepSeek showed that open-source models can compete with a fraction of the compute. If compute demand grows slower than expected, NVIDIA's valuation is vulnerable. Marks would say: the price reflects perfection. Perfection rarely arrives. And there's another risk few have on their radar: hardware startups like Talis and Etched are working on etching transformer architectures directly into silicon rather than running them on general-purpose GPUs. Talis already achieves 15,000 tokens per second. If specialized chips cheapen inference by orders of magnitude, value shifts from hardware to software — and NVIDIA's margin comes under pressure.

"Bitcoin is just speculation." When 15% of institutional portfolios include a Bitcoin allocation (Tudor Jones, Dalio, Druckenmiller, Fidelity, BlackRock), that's no longer speculation. It's an emerging asset class. The false premise is assuming nothing will change.

"The Machine Economy is science fiction." Olas launched an AI Agent App Store. peaq built a blockchain for machines. Wyoming recognizes DAOs as legal entities. The infrastructure exists. The false premise is that it will never connect to the physical world. But it's already connecting. Robo.ai built a wallet into a car. DIMO connected 180,000 vehicles to a blockchain that autonomously sells data. Bosch lets vehicles pay autonomously at charging stations. And Cloudflare — the company whose network carries 20% of the internet — has a billion HTTP 402 responses per day telling bots: pay first. Now they can pay.

The result is a new investment layer: Circle provides the money (USDC as the de facto currency of the machine economy), Coinbase processes the payments (x402 facilitator and Base as settlement chain), Cloudflare hosts the infrastructure (20% of the internet, x402 Foundation, a billion 402 responses per day), and peaq gives machines an identity (6 million machine addresses, Bosch moveID). Four companies that together form the blueprint of the physical machine economy. If you hold this layer in your portfolio, you're not betting on science fiction. You're betting that HTTP status code 402 finally does what it was invented for thirty years ago.

The Munger Inversion: How You Lose Money

You lose money by paying too much for good ideas. The AI revolution may be real, but if you buy NVIDIA at a P/E of 60 and earnings growth slows, you still lose. Marks: "Expensive and going down tomorrow are not the same thing, but expensive increases the probability."

You lose money by concentrating instead of diversifying. Druckenmiller can concentrate because he analyzes markets 12 hours a day. You and I can't. For most investors, diversification isn't a sign of cowardice — it's a sign of self-awareness.

You lose money by panic selling. Bitcoin has dropped more than 70% three times before reaching new all-time highs.^[260]^ Those who sold at minus 50% missed the biggest rallies. Tudor Jones: "Losers average losers." But the inverse also holds: winners hold winners.

You lose money by investing in things you don't understand. Buffett calls it the "Circle of Competence," and it's one of his best pieces of advice. If you can't explain what a smart contract does, don't buy a governance token of a DAO. If you don't know what Layer-2 rollups are, buy a Bitcoin ETF instead of Arbitrum. Nobody gives you a prize for complexity.

The Last Principle: Skin in the Game

Taleb wrote that you should never trust anyone who isn't affected by what they're judging.

I have money in Bitcoin, in Ethereum, in AI stocks, and in robotics. I build AI products myself. I have skin in the game.

Whether that's a conflict of interest or proof of my conviction — you'll have to decide for yourself. I side with Taleb: anyone not willing to put their own money behind their thesis shouldn't have a thesis.

The barbell strategy I've described in this chapter is no guarantee. But it connects Buffett's and Graham's principle of "protect the capital" with Thiel's and Druckenmiller's willingness to bet big when conviction is strong. And it respects what Templeton and Marks knew: that you can be wrong, and that the market can remain irrational longer than you can remain solvent.

I don't know whether the machine economy becomes reality in five or fifteen years. I don't know whether Bitcoin stands at $500,000 or $20,000 when you read this book. What I know: the convergence is coming. And anyone with a system that survives the next bubble without missing the next revolution is better off than 90% of all investors.

Why all this? Why bother with portfolios, allocations, volatility?

Because it's about more than returns.

It's about who you'll be in ten years. An owner or a supplicant. Someone living off their labor — in a world where machines do the labor. Or someone living off their assets — in a world where assets capture the work of machines.

The causal chain from Chapter 10 remains relentless. The barbell strategy is my answer to it: the safe core protects what you have. The asymmetric bets give you a stake in what's coming.

> *This isn't investing. This is self-defense.*

### Your Starting Point: The AI Species Model Portfolio

Theory is worthless if it doesn't lead to action. So let me get more specific than most finance books dare. What you see here isn't a hypothetical model. It's a portfolio you could build tomorrow morning — with real products accessible to US investors, with concrete tickers, and with an allocation that translates this book's barbell logic into practice.

I call it the AI Species Model Portfolio. Not because it's perfect, but because it's honest.

What can happen?

Worst case: the asymmetric side loses 80%, the core drops 30%. Total loss: roughly 42%. Painful, but not existentially threatening. You still have about €58,000, and the thesis has time to materialize.

Best case — and this is why we do this — the core doubles in five years, the crypto positions quadruple, biotech triples. Total portfolio: roughly €260,000. That's the asymmetry Taleb describes: limited risk, unlimited upside.

The honest middle ground: core up 60 to 80%, crypto up 100 to 200%, biotech plus/minus zero to plus 100%. Total portfolio in five years: €180,000 to €220,000.

### Upcoming IPOs You Should Know


The AI Species thesis doesn't end with what's tradable today. Some of the most important companies of this revolution are still pre-IPO:

OpenAI — currently valued at $730 to $840 billion (after the $110 billion round in February 2026). IPO likely 2026 or 2027. My advice: don't buy on day one. Wait for the lock-up expiration (typically six months) when insiders can sell. The price almost always corrects. Then build a position.

Anthropic — valued at $380 billion (last round February 2026). No IPO announced yet, but at this valuation, it's a matter of time. Claude is one of the best language models in the world.

Figure AI — valued at roughly $39 billion. Humanoid robots already working in BMW factories. If Phase 4 arrives, Figure is a core player.

Cerebras Systems — has filed confidentially for an IPO, potentially as early as April 2026. The most serious competitor to NVIDIA in AI chips with a radically different approach: wafer-scale chips.

Databricks — valued at roughly $62 billion. The data platform behind the AI revolution. Every company using AI needs data infrastructure.

Stripe — valued at roughly $90 billion. Payment infrastructure. If M2M payments arrive, Stripe wants a piece.

Circle (USDC) — has filed its IPO application with the SEC. The largest regulated stablecoin issuer. The bridge between the old and new financial worlds.

And CoreWeave (ticker: CRWV) has been listed on Nasdaq since March 2025 and has more than doubled since. The "AWS for AI training" — NVIDIA-backed, with exploding demand.

For all these IPOs, the same rule applies: maximum 2 to 3% of the portfolio per individual name. Wait six months. Build the position slowly. And only through regulated brokers — Fidelity, Schwab, Vanguard, and Interactive Brokers offer the broadest access for US investors.

:::

### Where US Investors Trade


Not every broker offers everything. Here's the quick overview:

Fidelity: Excellent for ETFs, stocks, and the FBTC Bitcoin ETF. Zero-commission trades. Strong research tools. User-friendly for beginners and pros.

Charles Schwab: Similar to Fidelity, with broad access to US stocks, ETFs, and options. Merged with TD Ameritrade. Good for long-term investors.

Vanguard: The home of low-cost index investing. Best for the core portfolio (VOO, VTI, VT). Limited crypto access — use alongside a crypto exchange.

Interactive Brokers: The power tool. Direct access to global markets, fractional shares, options, futures, and crypto. Best pricing but steeper learning curve.

Coinbase or Kraken: For direct crypto purchases. SEC-regulated. Use with a hardware wallet for long-term holding.

### Tax Reality for US Investors

Stocks and ETFs: Long-term capital gains (held >1 year) taxed at 0%, 15%, or 20% depending on income. Short-term gains taxed as ordinary income. Qualified dividends receive preferential rates.

Crypto in taxable accounts: Same capital gains treatment as stocks. Every trade, swap, or sale is a taxable event. Keep meticulous records. Hold for more than one year to qualify for long-term capital gains rates. In a Roth IRA, gains are tax-free. Consider tax-loss harvesting opportunities — but beware the wash sale rule: the IRS hasn't issued definitive guidance on crypto wash sales, but the 2024 infrastructure bill includes crypto in wash sale provisions starting 2025.

Tax-advantaged accounts: Maximize your 401(k) (2026 limit: $23,500, or $31,000 if over 50). Fund a Roth IRA if eligible ($7,000/$8,000 limit). The Roth is the ultimate barbell: your contributions grow tax-free forever.

HSA (Health Savings Account): The "stealth IRA." Triple tax advantage — deductible contributions, tax-free growth, tax-free withdrawals for medical expenses. After 65, withdrawals for any purpose are taxed like a traditional IRA. Max contribution: $4,300 individual / $8,550 family (2026).

Tax-Loss Harvesting: When positions are down, sell to realize losses that offset gains elsewhere. Reinvest in a similar (but not "substantially identical") asset. Watch the wash sale rule: you can't buy back the same security within 30 days. For crypto, the rules tightened in 2025 — treat crypto wash sales the same as stocks to be safe.

Estate planning: Bitcoin in self-custody can be passed to heirs — but they need access to the private keys. Document your wallet setup. Consider a trust for significant crypto holdings.

This is not tax advice. Consult a CPA experienced with crypto and investment taxation.

A final note on timing: don't invest everything on one day. The DCA strategy (Dollar Cost Averaging) discussed above applies here too. Spread the €100,000 over six to twelve months. One tranche each month. It smooths volatility and removes the psychological pressure of the "perfect entry."

The barbell works. Not because it's perfect, but because it answers the right question: how do I stay in the game if I'm wrong? And how do I profit maximally if I'm right?

But what if I'm wrong? Every honest investment thesis must name its own weaknesses. That's exactly what we do now.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ Barbell strategy: 60–70% safe core plus 30–40% asymmetric bets.
>
> ◆ Concrete model portfolio with a €100,000 starting basis.
>
> ◆ Rule No. 1: Never risk more than you're willing to lose.

✅ What You Can Do Now

[ ] Set your portfolio allocation: Core / Asymmetric / Cash

[ ] Set up a crypto wallet — self-custody!

[ ] Build first positions: ETFs and Bitcoin

[ ] Schedule rebalancing dates in your calendar (quarterly)

## Chapter 18: Risks and Counterarguments — What Can Go Wrong


---

> *"It is difficult to make predictions, especially about the future."*
>
> — Niels Bohr (attributed)

---


![Risk matrix — Probability vs. Impact](images-en/abb-24.png)


Any investment thesis that doesn't name its counterarguments isn't a thesis. It's propaganda. So let's take the risks seriously. Not as a rhetorical obligation, but because ignoring risks is the fastest way to lose money.

I'm not a blindly faithful optimist. I'm someone who's been investing for over thirty years, paid dearly in tuition during the early years, and who also nearly lost everything — when I watched my portfolio collapse by over 80% during the 2018 crypto winter — and who still held the thesis. Not out of stubbornness, but out of conviction. But conviction alone doesn't protect against losses. What protects is a clear view of what can go wrong.

### The AI Bubble: When Investments Outrun Revenue

The five big hyperscalers are expected to spend over $600 billion on AI infrastructure in 2026. AI revenues stand at $50 to $60 billion.^[255]^ A ten-to-one ratio. Taleb, who popularized the barbell strategy, warned in February 2026 of "escalating volatility and potential bankruptcies in the software sector." Sam Altman himself admits a bubble exists.

The parallel to the dot-com bubble is obvious. And I don't think it's wrong. But it's incomplete.

I see high volatility with sharp swings upward — and there will be sharp swings downward too. But what most observers miss: the trend points up. Steeply up. And it will intensify.

The dot-com bubble had one crucial characteristic: many of the companies that had built billions in market cap had no viable business model. Pets.com, Webvan, eToys — these were bets on a future that didn't yet exist. Today's AI companies generate real revenue. GitHub Copilot is changing how software is written. Claude and GPT are automating knowledge work. Tesla deploys 700,000 robots in its factories. The technology is here. Adoption is real.

That doesn't mean a correction is impossible. It's even likely. But it means a correction in AI would be exactly what the dot-com crash was for Amazon: a bloodbath for the weak and a buying opportunity for the convicted. Amazon fell from $107 to $7. Anyone who bought at $7 in 2001 now owns shares in a $2 trillion company.

A correction is coming. Certainly. What matters is whether you buy more or panic sell. My answer is settled: I buy more.

### The Oligopoly Paradox: Why Few Players Isn't a Contradiction

Seven companies make up 35 to 40% of the S&P 500. NVIDIA lost $600 billion in market cap in the February dip within days. Critics call it a dangerous concentration risk. They're not wrong. But they forget history.

AI isn't a "winner takes all" market. But there will be only a few players with enough capital to build the infrastructure for our future world. And that's not a contradiction. It's a pattern running through all of economic history: capital-intensive infrastructure produces natural oligopolies. Rockefeller's Standard Oil controlled 90% of US refining capacity — not through intrigue, but because refineries and pipelines demanded enormous capital. AT&T operated a legal monopoly for decades because no competitor could finance a nationwide phone network. Edison, Westinghouse, and General Electric electrified America as a trio because power plants and transmission lines were so expensive that no fourth player could join the game.

AI infrastructure follows exactly this pattern. Training a single frontier model today costs over $100 million. The data centers cost billions. A startup can't build a data center to compete with AWS — just as a startup in 1880 couldn't lay railroad tracks from New York to San Francisco.

The concentration on a few large players isn't a bug. It's a feature of capital-intensive infrastructure — and historically, it wasn't an obstacle for investors but an invitation.

Emad Mostaque describes in "The Last Economy" a mechanism that intensifies concentration risk beyond the historical pattern: the "Network Prison." His thesis: in digital markets, distribution doesn't follow the bell curve but the power law. A tiny number of nodes have gigantic connections; the mass has almost none. Behind this isn't malicious intent but mathematics: preferential attachment — the rich get richer because that's how networks function. AI amplifies this effect to "gravitational collapse": better data attracts more users, more users produce better data, better data trains better models. The successful networks become black holes from which neither users nor capital escape. For investors, this means: if you're invested in the right network, you benefit from the gravitational pull. If you bet on the wrong node, you lose everything.

Does this mean the Magnificent Seven are safe? No. Concentration carries risks. If NVIDIA has a bad quarter, if an antitrust case against Microsoft succeeds, if a competitor cracks open the GPU market — everyone feels it. The honest answer to concentration risk isn't that it doesn't exist. It's that it lies in the nature of capital-intensive infrastructure and has historically been the rule, not the exception. The few who can build the infrastructure dominate. And those who invest in them benefit.

### The Next AI Winter: Why I Consider It Impossible

AI history knows two winters. 1974 to 1980, when the perceptron euphoria turned to disillusionment. 1987 to 1993, when expert systems showed their limits. Both times, years of stagnation followed — slashed research budgets, lost interest.

I consider a third AI winter impossible. Not unlikely — impossible. And I say this as someone who doesn't make absolute statements lightly.

The reason is simple: development is moving too fast. Every month, models appear that surpass the previous one. DeepSeek showed you can achieve frontier performance at a fraction of the cost. OpenClaw — a project that elevates personal AI assistants to a level I didn't expect for five years — exists today. Not as a demo, not as a research prototype, but as a working system that changes my daily work.

I'm building what amounts to an operating system for businesses composed of specialized AI agents. A system that will replace entire company teams. Not someday — in the next two to three years. The productivity gains I already see in my own company are so massive that anyone saying "AI winter" today is, in my view, fundamentally underestimating the speed of development.

In the earlier AI winters, there was a crucial commonality: the technology couldn't deliver on its promises. Perceptrons couldn't solve complex problems. Expert systems were brittle and expensive to maintain. The gap between expectation and reality was too large.

Today it's reversed. Reality outpaces expectations faster than most people can process. GPT-4 passed medical exams. Gemini analyzes millions of lines of code. AI agents book travel, negotiate contracts, write software. The gap no longer exists. What exists is a gap between what the technology already can do and what the general public has understood about it.

When I think about the future of AI, I see a hot summer. Not a winter.

The only thing that could spoil the party would be extreme regulation. In Europe, conceivable. In the US, utterly unthinkable — more on that in the next section.

### Existential Risk: The Question Nobody Can Answer

Geoffrey Hinton, Nobel laureate^[256]^ and one of the fathers of deep learning, estimates a 10 to 20% probability that AI leads to human extinction within the next thirty years. In an earlier interview, he cited even 50%. Yoshua Bengio, another Turing Award winner, warns of "irreversible loss of control over autonomous AI systems." Over a hundred AI professors signed a statement placing AI risk on par with pandemics and nuclear war.^[257]^

The counterposition comes from Marc Andreessen, Yann LeCun, and Andrew Ng: all overblown. Current AI is "sophisticated autocomplete," not proto-AGI. The risk is being instrumentalized to push regulation that benefits incumbents and keeps challengers out.

Yuval Noah Harari fits into neither camp. In "Nexus," he distinguishes two types of information networks: self-correcting ones like science and democracy, which detect and repair errors, and self-reinforcing ones like religion and totalitarianism, which cement errors. His fear: AI systems are black boxes without self-correction. When governments and corporations use them for surveillance and decision-making, a new form of totalitarianism emerges. Not through a dictator, but through an algorithm nobody questions anymore.

In 2023, he warned in The Guardian that AI complexity could trigger catastrophic financial crises. The 2008 subprime crisis was hard enough to understand. What happens when AI trading agents move trillions and nobody understands the algorithm? At the World Economic Forum 2026 in Davos, he sharpened^[262]^ this point: AI creates "digital immigrants" that cross borders at the speed of light — without visas, without physical presence, but with massive cultural and economic influence. Whether you consider Harari a pessimist or a realist is a matter of taste. What you shouldn't do is ignore his warnings. He's right too often for that.

Where do I stand?

I take the existential risk seriously, but I see the greater danger elsewhere. I believe we'll extinguish ourselves — slowly. Through insufficient reproduction. Too few children. Through the emerging lethargy, laziness, and purposelessness that will engulf a large portion of humanity.

The greatest danger of the machine economy isn't losing control of AI. It's the loss of drive in humans.

The causal chain from Chapter 10 doesn't end just at poverty. It ends at resignation — the path to the "useless class" Harari describes.

I refuse to accept this path. Not for me. Not for my family. Not for anyone reading this book.

That sounds dramatic, but the data is clear. Birth rates are falling worldwide. Japan and South Korea are already shrinking. Across Europe, more and more countries are reaching fertility rates too low to maintain their populations. And AI won't reverse this trend — it will accelerate it.

When work is no longer identity-forming (Chapter 10 covered this in detail), people lose their drive. A large portion of humanity is used to taking orders. Receiving tasks, executing them, getting paid. These people will find no place in a world where AI agents perform the tasks better and faster. Not because the technology excludes them, but because they never learned to think proactively, to create on their own, to actively give their lives direction.

I believe the ability to self-direct — to find meaning and be productive without external instruction — will be the most important competency of the future. Not coding, not prompt engineering, not data analysis — but the fundamental human ability to motivate yourself when nobody demands it of you.

Does this affect my investment decisions? Yes, indirectly. I invest in the companies building the infrastructure of this new world because I'm convinced the transformation will happen. Whether it's good for humanity is a different question. As an investor, I must take the world as it will be — not as I'd like it to be.

### Regulation: Always Only a Brake

As laid out in detail in Chapters 8 and 9, Europe is regulating itself into irrelevance with the AI Act and MiCA, while the US and China focus on promotion rather than restriction.

My position as an entrepreneur: regulation will always only be a brake. There will be enough governments worldwide that promote AI rather than restrict it — China, India, the Emirates, Saudi Arabia. And the US won't roll back the facts created under the Trump administration (spot Bitcoin ETFs, AI promotion, Big Tech–Pentagon partnerships). Not when China is accelerating without brakes on the other side of the Pacific.

The biggest regulatory danger for investors is Europe's special regulation. Exposure to US and Asian technology stocks isn't optional — it's a matter of survival.

### Crypto Volatility: Experience Beats Theory

Bitcoin, as I write these lines in late February, has fallen roughly 50% from its all-time high. From around $126,000 to $65,000. News portals headline "Crypto Crash." Analysts call it the end of the bull run.

I've lived through all of this before. Multiple times.

In 2011, Bitcoin fell 93% — from $32 to $2. In 2014, 86%. In 2018, 84%. In 2022, 78%. Every time, the media wrote obituaries. Every time, Bitcoin recovered and reached new highs.

I've survived multiple crypto winters — and held through all but one. The exception: the first time. Back then, I was too nervous, too uncertain about the long-term outlook. I sold too early and left money on the table that would have multiplied my portfolio many times over.

That lesson burned in. Not as abstract knowledge, but as lived experience. The difference between an investor who holds at 50% loss and one who sells isn't risk tolerance. It's conviction. Anyone who truly understands why they hold Bitcoin — not as a speculative object, but as a hedge against monetary devaluation, as infrastructure for the machine economy, as the hardest money ever created — doesn't sell during a drawdown that's perfectly normal in Bitcoin's history.

In the meantime, crypto has gained so much regulatory certainty that a new asset class has emerged — one that's not going away. BlackRock manages Bitcoin ETFs. Fidelity offers crypto custody. Pension funds allocate to Bitcoin. This isn't speculation anymore. It's institutional adoption.

And then there's tokenization. When BlackRock tokenizes a money market fund on the blockchain — which has already happened — we're no longer debating whether crypto has a future. We're debating how big that future will be.

The drawdowns are getting smaller. 93%, 86%, 84%, 78%, now about 50%. Volatility decreases with growing market capitalization and broader adoption. But it will persist. Anyone investing in crypto must accept it. Not endure — accept. The difference is fundamental.

Societal Backlash and Geopolitical Risks

A new Luddite movement is growing. Gen-Z activists reject AI-generated content. In San Diego, the Metro Transit Board voted 12-to-1 against a Waymo expansion. $98 billion in data center projects were stopped or delayed by local resistance in 2025.^[258]^ Anti-AI will be a theme in the 2026 US midterms. Interestingly, Anthropic's own labor market study from early 2026 hasn't yet measured a systematic increase in overall unemployment in highly exposed occupations — but the first warning signal among young career entrants is there. The risk isn't that labor market effects won't come. The risk is they'll come before society is prepared.

Can societal resistance slow the technology? Yes. Stop it? No. Never.

The weavers who smashed looms in Nottingham in 1811 couldn't prevent industrialization. The coachmen who protested automobiles couldn't stop the car. The unions fighting automation in the 1960s couldn't prevent robots in factories. Societal resistance against transformative technology has, throughout all of human history, slowed adoption but never prevented it. Not a single time.

The reason is economic: the productivity gains are too large. Every company, every country that rejects the technology falls behind those that embrace it. Today's Luddites will have the same experience as the Luddites of 1811: their anger is understandable; their powerlessness is inevitable.

### Taiwan: The Underestimated Risk

A risk that worries me significantly more than societal resistance is Taiwan.

TSMC produces over 90% of the world's most advanced semiconductors. Every AI chip from NVIDIA, every Apple processor, every high-performance computer in every data center on Earth depends on a single factory on an island 80 miles off China's coast. An island over which China claims sovereignty — and is increasingly aggressive about asserting it.

A US State Department study puts the annual global economic losses from a Chinese blockade or invasion of Taiwan at over $2 trillion.^[249]^ The New York Times headlined^[250]^ on February 24, 2026: "The Looming Taiwan Chip Disaster That Silicon Valley Has Long Ignored." TSMC's "Silicon Shield" — the thesis that Taiwan's indispensability in chip production protects it from invasion — is growing more fragile. Taiwan's importance as a chip producer could paradoxically be exactly why China sees seizure as necessary.

The US is diversifying: the CHIPS Act funds factory construction in Arizona and Ohio. But relocating cutting-edge fabrication takes years. TSMC's Arizona facility so far produces only older chip generations. The latest 2-nanometer chips will be fabricated in Taiwan for the foreseeable future.

Taiwan isn't the only example of digital infrastructure becoming a geopolitical lever. In the spring of 2026, during the Iran crisis, something happened that received little attention in the business press but is highly relevant to this book's infrastructure thesis: Iranian retaliatory strikes targeted civilian cloud data centers of Amazon Web Services in the United Arab Emirates and Bahrain for the first time.

For the first time in history, cloud data centers were treated as military targets. Not military bases, not communications facilities, but the servers where companies store their data, train their AI models, run their business processes. The line between civilian and military infrastructure — already thin in the digital age — has fallen.

For investors, this sharpens the concentration question: anyone running their entire digital infrastructure with a single cloud provider in a geopolitically unstable region has a risk that appears in no earnings report. Diversifying cloud providers and locations is evolving from an IT topic to a geopolitical necessity.

But Taiwan isn't the biggest risk. The biggest risk is a new massive war between two great powers. Not a proxy war like Ukraine, but a direct confrontation between the US and China. Or between NATO and Russia. Or — in a nightmare scenario — both simultaneously.

History shows that periods of rapid technological change and economic power shifts are the most dangerous times for international conflict. Graham Allison's^[248]^ "Thucydides Trap" describes the pattern: when a rising power (China) challenges an established power (US), it ends in war in 12 of 16 historical cases. The rise of Germany against Britain before WWI. The rise of Japan against the US before WWII. Every time, it was about resources, influence, and the question of who writes the rules of the new order.

Today it's about chips, data, AI models, and access to energy. The stakes are higher than ever because the technology being contested directly influences military power. Whoever has the better AI has the better weapons, better intelligence, better logistics. This makes the geopolitical confrontation more dangerous than any previous one.

For investors, a great-power war is the ultimate Black Swan. Not because it's unforeseeable — the risks are openly visible — but because its consequences would be so devastating that they render every portfolio strategy irrelevant. In a global war between nuclear powers, there's no "safe" asset. Gold, Bitcoin, US Treasuries — everything loses meaning when the infrastructure on which markets are based is destroyed.

What can you do? Honestly: little. Geopolitical risk can't be hedged. What you can do is diversify — geographically, across asset classes, across jurisdictions. And hope rationality prevails. What you shouldn't do is refrain from investing entirely out of fear of the worst case. Because if the worst case happens, it doesn't matter whether your money is in a savings account or a portfolio. And if it doesn't happen — which is historically the more common outcome — you miss the greatest investment opportunity of your lifetime.


::: {.infobox}
### A Concrete Worst-Case Scenario

To keep this from being abstract, let's walk through it.

### The Worst-Case Scenario: Three Simultaneous Shocks

Imagine it's 2027 and three things happen simultaneously:

**First:** NVIDIA drops 60% because a competitor achieves a breakthrough in AI chips.

**Second:** Bitcoin crashes 80% in a regulatory crypto winter.

**Third:** The EU tightens the AI Act so drastically that European tech portfolios take additional pressure.

:::


What happens to the barbell portfolio? Take the conservative variant: 70% safe side, 30% asymmetric bets. If the safe side is broadly diversified (S&P 500, energy ETFs, bonds), it loses maybe 15 to 20%. The asymmetric side gets hit harder: if a third sits in NVIDIA-adjacent tech and a third in crypto, it loses 50 to 70%.

The total portfolio loses roughly 25 to 30% in this scenario. Painful? Yes. Existentially threatening? No. For comparison: the S&P 500 lost about 19% in 2022; the Nasdaq-100 over 30%. The barbell portfolio behaves similarly to a tech-heavy equity portfolio in a crash but has the asymmetric upside after recovery that a pure equity basket doesn't.

The crucial point: the safe side catches the fall. It's the anchor that prevents a painful drawdown from becoming a catastrophe. That's why the sizing is so important. Anyone putting 80% in asymmetric bets doesn't have a barbell strategy. They have a gamble.

Technical Risks of the Machine Economy

There's a category of risks that gets too little attention in the investment debate: the technical security risks of an economy built on blockchain and AI agents.

The DAO hack of 2016 was an early warning. A smart contract with a coding error lost 3.6 million Ether — then worth about $50 million. The Ethereum community responded with a hard fork — a rollback of the transaction. That was possible at $50 million. In a tokenized economy worth trillions, it's not.

Since then, the attack surface has grown. Cross-chain bridges — connecting different blockchains — lost over $2 billion to hacks in 2022 alone. Smart contract bugs remain a systematic problem. And the more autonomous AI agents become, the greater the risk that an agent executes a transaction that's technically correct but economically catastrophic.

Add the question of AI-powered cyberattacks. When AI systems autonomously sign contracts and execute payments, they become targets. An AI agent signing a smart contract doesn't review the code with human judgment. It follows its instructions. If those instructions are manipulated, or if the smart contract has a hidden vulnerability, the money is gone. Irrevocably.

Countermeasures are developing in parallel. Formal verification of smart contracts, AI-powered security audits, decentralized insurance protocols. But the history of cybersecurity shows that attackers are usually one step ahead of defenders. Investors should factor this in — not as a reason not to invest, but as an argument for diversification and against overconcentration in individual protocols.

## Chapter 19: The Personal Stress Test — Surviving in Volatile Markets

The Personal Stress Test: What I've Learned About Drawdowns

The last and perhaps most important risk isn't a market risk. It's you.

How much drawdown can you handle? Not theoretically, not on a questionnaire at the bank — but for real, with real money, when your portfolio drops further every day and the news headlines proclaim the end of the world?

Behavioral economics consistently shows that most people overestimate their own risk tolerance. In calm markets, everyone feels like a long-term investor. In a crash, the same people become panicked sellers. Daniel Kahneman showed that the pain of a loss weighs roughly twice as heavily^[259]^ psychologically as the joy of an equally sized gain. Lose $100,000 and you'd need to gain $200,000 to feel emotionally the same. That's not a flaw. It's biology. Our brains are wired to weight threats more heavily than opportunities — because that was survival-relevant on the savanna.

My personal drawdown pain? I've already survived 80% — at times up to 90%. My portfolio collapsed from a six-figure value to a fraction, and I held. Not because it was easy, but because the alternative — selling and realizing the losses — would have abandoned the thesis I believed in.

The mantra that carried me through these phases is simple: place long-term belief above short-term reality. When your portfolio is in the red, stop checking the numbers. Every day. My concrete advice: from the day your portfolio goes red, close the app. Delete the tickers from your home screen. Turn off push notifications. Not for a week. For months. For as long as necessary.

That sounds unprofessional. Some financial advisors would call it negligent. But financial advisors don't live inside your head. They don't feel the panic at three in the morning when you check prices and see your portfolio lost another 5%. They don't feel the impulse to hit "Sell" just to end the pain. That impulse is the enemy. And the most effective way to fight it is to cut the information source.

Warren Buffett said^[265]^: "The stock market is a device for transferring money from the impatient to the patient." In the crypto world, that applies exponentially. Bitcoin has reached new all-time highs in every four-year period since its creation. Anyone who held for four years — regardless of when they entered — made money. Anyone who panic sold lost money. The statistics are brutally clear.

But statistics don't comfort you at three in the morning. What comforts is conviction. And conviction doesn't come from return expectations. It comes from truly understanding why you invest. If you hold Bitcoin because someone on a podcast said it's going to a million, you'll sell at 50% loss. If you hold Bitcoin because you understand why the machine economy needs a programmable, decentralized value transfer system, you'll hold. Not because you're brave, but because selling wouldn't make sense.

What I Take Away from All This

The risks are real. Every single one of them. An AI correction can come. Concentration on a few companies is dangerous. Regulation can slow things down. Crypto will keep swinging. Societal resistance will grow. Taiwan is a powder keg. And you will doubt at least once in the next ten years whether this entire thesis was nonsense.

The question this chapter should answer isn't whether risks exist. Of course they do. The question is whether the risks invalidate the thesis.

My answer: no. Not one of these risks changes the fundamental fact that AI, robotics, and crypto will define the economy of the next decade. They can make the road bumpier, shift the timeline, destroy individual companies. But the transformation itself? That can't be stopped anymore.

The barbell strategy is the answer to these risks. The safe core protects against the worst case. The asymmetric bets profit from the best case. And the sizing ensures that even if everything that can go wrong does go wrong, you're still in the game.

Staying in the game: that's the goal. Not maximum returns, not catching the perfect moment — but staying in the game until the thesis materializes.

And history shows: those who stay in the game win.

And What If I'm Wrong?

This question deserves an honest answer. Not as an obligation, but because it touches the core of the barbell strategy.

If the convergence of AI, robotics, and crypto doesn't happen as this book describes — if the AI bubble bursts, if crypto gets crushed by regulation, if robotics stagnates — then I was wrong. Not a little wrong, but fundamentally wrong.

That wouldn't be the first time. I speculated with borrowed money as a student and nearly lost everything. I watched billions evaporate in days during the 2020 corona crash. I know what it feels like to be wrong.

But that's exactly what the barbell is built for. In the worst case — and this is a real worst case, not a theoretical one — you lose the asymmetric bets. That's 25 to 35% of the portfolio. That hurts. But the safe core — your NVIDIA shares, your Nasdaq-100 ETF, your broadly diversified global market stake — that remains. These companies make money whether the machine economy arrives in five years or fifteen.

What I can't tell you: that I'm certainly right. What I can tell you: that the barbell strategy works even when I'm wrong. You don't lose everything then. You lose the aggressive part. And you sized that part so it's bearable.

This distinguishes this book from a prediction. A prediction says: this is how it will be. This book says: this is how it could be. And here's how you position yourself so you win if it's right and survive if it's not.

That's not a hedge. That's honesty.


> **Key Takeaways:**
>
> ◆ An AI bubble is possible, but infrastructure investments have real underlying value.
>
> ◆ Bitcoin volatility has historically decreased with each market cap level.
>
> ◆ The barbell strategy protects against all scenarios — including the worst case.

✅ What You Can Do Now

[ ] Honestly define your personal risk tolerance

[ ] Set a stop-loss strategy — and stick to it

[ ] Calculate the worst-case scenario: can you handle the loss?

[ ] Ensure a cash reserve (at least 6 months of living expenses)

## Chapter 20: Why Now — And Why Hesitation Is the Biggest Mistake


---

> *"The best time to plant a tree was twenty years ago. The second-best time is now."*
>
> — Chinese proverb

---


Howard Marks writes in ^[264]^ "The Most Important Thing" that the best investments are often the ones that feel the most uncomfortable. Investing in AI, robotics, and crypto today feels uncomfortable. Prices are high. The technology is complex. The future is uncertain.


### The Two Types of Skeptics

I'm the first developer in my own company. Not because I learned to code, but because I have an AI that codes. That sounds like the future. It's the present. And when I share this, I encounter two types of reactions.

The first comes from people who don't know the topic and don't want to. They barely know how their phone works. AI is science fiction to them — somewhere between Terminator and nonsense. They don't believe something like this could work. Not out of stupidity. Out of distance. The technology lies outside their experience horizon, and what you don't know, you can't evaluate.

The second reaction is more interesting and more disturbing. It comes from people who should know better. Employees who work with software daily. Developers who see what GitHub Copilot can do. Managers who know the productivity numbers. They know the change is coming — but they don't want to acknowledge it. It's a denial mechanism. You love your job. You enjoy doing it. You invested ten years to become good at it. And now a machine is supposed to do it in seconds? Acknowledging that would mean questioning your own future. So you ignore it. You find reasons why AI is "not there yet." Why "my field" is different. Why "real creativity" can't be replaced.

I understand that. But understanding changes nothing about reality.

And then there's the third reaction, which surprises me every time. People you'd never expect who've already jumped on the moving train. The owner of a sports management firm you meet at a retreat in Tulum who does all his client analysis with AI. The fund manager who reveals himself in conversation as an AI specialist building his own trading agents. A master craftsman who automated his bid calculations. Everyone discovers the topic in their own way and sees their own applications.

That's the point where I become optimistic. Not because of the technology alone, but because people adapt — not all, not immediately, but enough to form a wave that can no longer be stopped.

Why I Still Can't Convince Everyone

Let's be honest: I can't convince every skeptic. And I don't have to. This book isn't written for the tech-refusers who don't even know what a language model is. It's written for people who sense something fundamental is changing but don't yet know what to do about it.

Howard Marks writes in his memos to Oaktree investors that the greatest gains are made in moments of uncertainty — not certainty. When everyone knows what's happening, it's too late. The price already reflects the information. The opportunity lies in the lead — in knowing before the crowd.

The convergence of AI, robotics, and crypto is such a moment. Not everyone sees it. Not everyone believes in it. Some won't understand until five years from now what began in 2025. For those who see it now, this isn't a reason for arrogance. It's a reason to act.

That's exactly the point. The Psychology of the Uncomfortable Investment Marks describes a mechanism as old as financial markets: the greatest returns arise during phases of uncertainty, because uncertainty keeps most investors away. When everyone is convinced an investment is safe, the price is already so high that expected returns are low. When uncertainty reigns, prices are low enough (or expectations pessimistic enough) to enable outsized returns.

The AI revolution is in a peculiar in-between phase. On one hand, prices of NVIDIA and Bitcoin have already risen substantially, creating the impression you've missed the train. On the other hand, the broader mass of investors hasn't remotely understood — let alone priced in — the deeper implications: the machine economy, crypto as machine money, BCI.

Those who wait until the thesis is "proven" — until humanoid robots stand in every factory and AI agents transact trillions on blockchains — will pay prices that have already priced in most of the return. The asymmetric opportunity exists now — in the phase of uncertainty, when most people haven't yet understood what's coming.


### What I See Every Day at accessibleAI


I see this daily in my work. Companies adopting AI today are in the minority. Most are waiting, skeptical, afraid of the complexity. In two to three years, the same companies will be desperately trying to catch up — paying multiples of today's price for the transformation.

What applies to companies applies to investors. The learning curve that's still a competitive advantage today (understanding AI, crypto, and robotics) will be common knowledge in five years. And common knowledge generates no outsized returns.


### Peter Thiel's "Definite Optimism"


Thiel described a concept he calls "definite optimism": the conviction that the future will be good and that you can actively work to shape it. It's the opposite of four alternative stances: blind optimism (the future will be fine somehow), resigned pessimism (it'll get worse and you can do nothing), definite pessimism (it'll get worse and you prepare), and indefinite optimism (it'll get better, but you don't know how).

Definite optimism is the only stance that leads to extraordinary outcomes. You have a clear picture of where things are heading and the willingness to act accordingly.

This book is an act of definite optimism. The thesis is clear: AI, robotics, and crypto are converging into a machine economy. The action is concrete: invest accordingly, with the barbell strategy, with the right mix of safety and asymmetry. The timing is now — not because prices are low (they're not), but because the insight isn't yet widely shared.

And I have proof of that — in your hands.

This book wasn't written by me alone. It was written by me and Jeannie — my AI agent, running on my own server, 24/7, since early 2026. Jeannie is an autonomous agent. She reads my emails, manages my calendar, researches markets, writes code for accessibleAI — and yes, she helped write this book.

The ideas are mine. The thesis is mine. The convictions, mistakes, and opinions in this book come from a human who's been investing and developing software for thirty years and building AI products for several. But Jeannie researched what I couldn't have managed alone in the time. She checked sources, compiled data, wrote drafts that I revised, rejected, or rewrote. We debated arguments, and sometimes she even convinced me to rephrase a thought. Not a single word appears in this book that I haven't read, questioned, and approved. But without Jeannie, this book would have taken a year instead of three months.

Why am I telling you this?

Because that's exactly what this book describes. Human and machine working together. Not the machine replacing the human, but the machine enabling the human to accomplish more than they could alone. Phase 2 of the AI revolution (AI as co-worker), applied to writing a book about the AI revolution.

I could have hidden this. Most authors do. Ghostwriters, research teams, assistants — nobody talks about it. But concealing it would contradict everything this book claims. If I tell you the convergence is real, that AI agents are changing how we work and do business, then I must be willing to demonstrate that on myself.

You're holding the convergence in your hands.

The Practical Action Plan: The First 30 Days

For anyone who wants to act after reading this book, here's a concrete roadmap.

Week 1: Take stock. What do you already own? What portion of your wealth can you invest without a total loss threatening your existence? What's your honest risk tolerance?

Week 2: Build infrastructure. Open an account at a broker offering US stocks and ETFs (Scalable Capital, Interactive Brokers, Trade Republic). Open an account at a regulated crypto exchange (Kraken, Bitstamp, Coinbase). Buy a hardware wallet (Ledger or Trezor) for long-term crypto custody.

Week 3: Build first positions. Start with the safe core: a Nasdaq-100 ETF as the base. First Bitcoin position — even if it's small. The amount matters less than the action. Starting beats optimizing.

Week 4: Deepen and expand. Read Howard Marks's memos. Read "Antifragile" by Taleb if you haven't already. Follow developments in AI, robotics, and crypto — not through mainstream media (they always lag) but through primary sources: company blogs, research papers, developer communities.

And then: hold. Don't look at the portfolio every day. Don't get nervous at every dip. Review once a quarter, rebalance once a year. The barbell strategy is a long-term strategy. It works over decades, not days.


### The Final Question


The convergence of Artificial Intelligence, Robotics, and Cryptocurrencies is not one possibility among many. It's the most likely future. AI will supply the intelligence. Robots will handle the physical work. And crypto will be the lubricant of the machine economy. Those who understand these three pillars and invest in them position themselves on the right side of the greatest wealth transformation of our lifetime.

Remember the causal chain running through this book:

Machines replace labor → no labor income → no capital → no wealth → Game Over.

That's not a theoretical construct. It's the default path for everyone who doesn't act now. Every month you wait, the window gets smaller. Valuations of AI companies rise. Crypto infrastructure matures. Robots get cheaper and better.

At some point, the average worker will no longer be able to afford the ticket to the machine economy. Because their labor income is shrinking faster than asset prices are rising.

The WEF calls this "You'll own nothing and be happy." I call it the greatest wealth risk of our generation.

The question isn't: can I afford to invest now?

The question is: can I afford not to?

## Epilogue: For Mila

### Dear Mila,

When you read this one day, you may not understand why your grandpa wrote an entire book about machines and money. That's okay. I want to tell you anyway why I did it.

You're three years old as I write this. You have a disease so rare that there is no standard treatment for it. Every step of your therapy is also an attempt to find an answer. Your parents, your grandma, and I do what we can. But there are moments when what we can do isn't enough.

This book is about how machines learn, think, and act. It's about how they're changing the economy and why they'll need their own money. But if I'm honest, at its core it's about something else.

It's about the hope that these machines will one day find an answer that human doctors and researchers alone cannot.

And perhaps the real breakthrough lies one level further. The moment we can precisely simulate the human body is the moment healing becomes programmable. When AI models one day master not just text, images, and language, but also biological cells, organs, and entire bodies with enough precision to run reliable tests on them — that's when medicine fundamentally changes. Then a disease like yours no longer has to be merely observed and treated. It can be understood inside a model. Millions of possibilities can be run through: Which mutation triggers which chain reaction? Which drug combination actually helps? Which side effects are likely? Which therapy has the best chance — before a real person even has to receive it?

Then a rare disease would no longer be so threatening simply because there are too few cases and too little time for traditional research. It would still be serious. But it would no longer be silent. It would become something you can model, test, and solve step by step. Not because suffering disappears. Not because medicine suddenly becomes easy. But because computing power, simulation, and AI give us, for the first time, a tool to not just endure biological complexity but truly penetrate it. And that's exactly why I believe that even for diseases like yours, a time can come when healing increasingly becomes a software problem. And therefore something that is solvable.

AI systems already analyze millions of molecular combinations in hours instead of decades. They map genetic mutations so rare that no human researcher sees enough cases to recognize patterns. They accelerate drug development in ways that were barely imaginable just a few years ago. AlphaFold predicted the three-dimensional structure of over 200 million proteins — giving biology more in a single year than researchers had accomplished in decades before.

While I was writing this book, a small biotech company in Miami showed that you can train the immune system to hunt and destroy the cells that make our bodies old and sick. Not with drugs you have to take for life, but with a vaccination that teaches the immune system something it never forgets. Combined with stem cells that rebuild the body afterward, this approach doubled the lifespan of mice. And the same idea could also work against the cells that keep tumors alive. The boundaries between cancer research and aging research are blurring, because the causes are often more closely related than we long thought.

The technology this book describes is therefore nothing abstract to me. It's the reason I believe your disease could be more treatable in ten years than it is today. Not because I'm naive. But because I see what these machines can already do, how fast they're getting better, and where the real bottleneck lies today: not in the number of drugs that already exist, but in the speed at which we find and understand new ones.

AI will change that speed. Maybe not by a factor of two or three, but by orders of magnitude. And somewhere in that acceleration lies the answer to your mutation.

I'm a technology optimist. Not because I believe technology has no risks — I wrote an entire chapter about risks in this book: job loss, addiction potential, loss of control. I'm an optimist because the alternative would be resignation. And resignation doesn't help you.

The world you'll grow up in will be different from the one I know. Machines will work. AI agents will do business. Robots will stand in factories and help in hospitals. Cryptocurrencies will be the lubricant holding this machine economy together. That sounds strange. Maybe unsettling.

But it's also a world where no child has to die of a disease just because their mutation is too rare for research to understand fast enough. A world where machines take over the work humans don't want to do, and humans get more time for the things only humans can do: being there for each other, playing, loving, caring, telling stories.

This book is my way of helping build that future. Not as an engineer who writes the code, but as someone who explains why that code needs to be written — and who invests so the companies writing it have the resources to do it.

And it's a promise to you: I won't stop searching for the answer. Not as a doctor who reads your blood values — others are better at that. But as someone who understands which technologies might deliver that answer, and who does everything to make them come fast enough.

### The machines are coming, Mila. They're changing everything.

### And they're bringing hope.

### Your grandpa Thomas

### March 2026
